### [1] [GeoBlock: Inferring Block Granularity from Dependency Geometry in Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.26675)
*Lipeng Wan,Junjie Ma,Jianhui Gu,Zeyang Liu,Xuyang Lu,Xuguang Lan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes GeoBlock, a geometry-aware block diffusion decoding method for language models that adapts block size using attention-based dependency geometry to balance parallel efficiency and autoregressive reliability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Block diffusion enables parallel refinement in diffusion language models, but its effectiveness strongly depends on block size. Existing methods use fixed or heuristic block-sizing that ignore the underlying token dependency geometry, risking refinement of mutually dependent tokens in parallel and hurting accuracy. The paper is motivated by the need for a principled, dependency-aware way to choose block granularity during decoding.

Method: Introduce GeoBlock, a framework that analyzes attention-derived cross-token dependency patterns during decoding to infer a "dependency geometry" over tokens. Using this geometry, it detects regions with strong causal ordering (which should be updated sequentially) versus semantically cohesive regions (safe for parallel refinement). GeoBlock dynamically determines block boundaries and sizes—without retraining the model—by finding geometrically stable refinement regions and adapting block granularity accordingly within existing block diffusion architectures.

Result: Experiments on multiple benchmarks show that GeoBlock can consistently find block boundaries that align with the dependency geometry and yield higher generation accuracy than baseline block diffusion strategies, while adding only modest computational overhead. It maintains much of the parallel efficiency of block diffusion while improving reliability relative to heuristic or fixed-size blocks.

Conclusion: Dependency geometry, as derived from attention patterns, is a key signal for safe parallel refinement in diffusion language models. By making block size and boundaries geometry-aware, GeoBlock reconciles parallelism with autoregressive-style dependency fidelity, improving the accuracy of block diffusion decoding without extra training and with minimal additional cost, and can be plugged into existing architectures.

Abstract: Block diffusion enables efficient parallel refinement in diffusion language models, but its decoding behavior depends critically on block size. Existing block-sizing strategies rely on fixed rules or heuristic signals and do not account for the dependency geometry that determines which tokens can be safely refined together. This motivates a geometry view of diffusion decoding: \emph{regions with strong causal ordering require sequential updates, whereas semantically cohesive regions admit parallel refinement.} We introduce GeoBlock, a geometry-aware block inference framework that determines block granularity directly from attention-derived dependency geometry. Instead of relying on predefined schedules or local confidence heuristics, GeoBlock analyzes cross-token dependency patterns to identify geometrically stable refinement regions and dynamically determines appropriate block boundaries during decoding. By adapting block granularity to the dependency geometry, GeoBlock preserves the parallel efficiency of block diffusion while enforcing dependency-consistent refinement that exhibits autoregressive reliability. GeoBlock requires no additional training and integrates seamlessly into existing block diffusion architectures. Extensive experiments across multiple benchmarks show that GeoBlock reliably identifies geometry-consistent block boundaries and improves the accuracy of block diffusion with only a small additional computational budget.

</details>


### [2] [AlpsBench: An LLM Personalization Benchmark for Real-Dialogue Memorization and Preference Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.26680)
*Jianfei Xiao,Xiang Yu,Chengbing Wang,Wuqiang Zheng,Xinyu Lin,Kaining Liu,Hongxun Ding,Yang Zhang,Wenjie Wang,Fuli Feng,Xiangnan He*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces AlpsBench, a real-world benchmark for evaluating LLM personalization through long-term dialogue and structured memory tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Personalizing LLMs into reliable lifelong assistants is hampered by the lack of a realistic, gold-standard benchmark that captures long-term, real-world personalized interactions and memory management, since current benchmarks either ignore key aspects of personalization or rely on synthetic data with distribution gaps.

Method: The authors construct AlpsBench from 2,500 long-term human-LLM interaction sequences sourced from WildChat, annotated with human-verified structured memories capturing explicit and implicit personalization signals. They define four tasks—personalized information extraction, updating, retrieval, and utilization—and propose evaluation protocols to cover the whole lifecycle of memory management. They then benchmark frontier LLMs and memory-centric systems on these tasks.

Result: Experiments show that: (i) state-of-the-art models struggle to extract latent user traits; (ii) memory updating performance plateaus even for the strongest models; (iii) retrieval performance drops significantly when many distractor memories are present; and (iv) explicit memory mechanisms, although improving recall, do not automatically lead to more preference-aligned or emotionally resonant responses.

Conclusion: AlpsBench provides a comprehensive, realistic evaluation framework for LLM personalization and memory management, highlighting current model limitations and offering a standard benchmark to drive progress toward more personalized and effective lifelong AI assistants.

Abstract: As Large Language Models (LLMs) evolve into lifelong AI assistants, LLM personalization has become a critical frontier. However, progress is currently bottlenecked by the absence of a gold-standard evaluation benchmark. Existing benchmarks either overlook personalized information management that is critical for personalization or rely heavily on synthetic dialogues, which exhibit an inherent distribution gap from real-world dialogue. To bridge this gap, we introduce AlpsBench, An LLM PerSonalization benchmark derived from real-world human-LLM dialogues. AlpsBench comprises 2,500 long-term interaction sequences curated from WildChat, paired with human-verified structured memories that encapsulate both explicit and implicit personalization signals. We define four pivotal tasks - personalized information extraction, updating, retrieval, and utilization - and establish protocols to evaluate the entire lifecycle of memory management. Our benchmarking of frontier LLMs and memory-centric systems reveals that: (i) models struggle to reliably extract latent user traits; (ii) memory updating faces a performance ceiling even in the strongest models; (iii) retrieval accuracy declines sharply in the presence of large distractor pools; and (iv) while explicit memory mechanisms improve recall, they do not inherently guarantee more preference-aligned or emotionally resonant responses. AlpsBench aims to provide a comprehensive framework.

</details>


### [3] [The Cognitive Divergence: AI Context Windows, Human Attention Decline, and the Delegation Feedback Loop](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.26707)
*Netanel Eliav*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper argues that AI context windows are rapidly expanding while human sustained attention is shrinking, creating a large and growing cognitive asymmetry it calls 'Cognitive Divergence.'


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To quantify and theorize a widening gap between what large language models can process at once and what humans can meaningfully attend to, and to explore how increasing AI delegation might further erode human cognitive capacities.

Method: The authors compile historical data on LLM context-window sizes and fit an exponential growth model; derive a human Effective Context Span (ECS) using reading-rate meta-analysis and a comprehension scaling factor; extrapolate ECS trends from longitudinal behavioral data; compute AI-to-human context ratios (raw and quality-adjusted); review relevant neuroimaging literature; and present empirical evidence and a conceptual model (Delegation Feedback Loop) regarding how humans choose to offload tasks to AI.

Result: They estimate that LLM context windows have grown roughly 3,906-fold between 2017 and 2026, while human ECS has fallen from about 16,000 tokens (2004) to roughly 1,800 tokens (2026 extrapolated). This yields AI-to-human context ratios of 556–1,111x raw and 56–111x after adjusting for retrieval degradation. They also marshal evidence that as AI becomes more capable, humans delegate at lower cognitive thresholds, plausibly reducing cognitive practice and contributing to further declines.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that AI and human cognitive capacities are diverging in a self-reinforcing way that will not correct itself without intervention. It calls for a sustained research program, including the development of a validated ECS psychometric tool and longitudinal tracking of AI-mediated cognitive change, to understand and potentially mitigate the long-term consequences of this divergence.

Abstract: This paper documents and theorises a self-reinforcing dynamic between two measurable trends: the exponential expansion of large language model (LLM) context windows and the secular contraction of human sustained-attention capacity. We term the resulting asymmetry the Cognitive Divergence. AI context windows have grown from 512 tokens in 2017 to 2,000,000 tokens by 2026 (factor ~3,906; fitted lambda = 0.59/yr; doubling time ~14 months). Over the same period, human Effective Context Span (ECS) -- a token-equivalent measure derived from validated reading-rate meta-analysis (Brysbaert, 2019) and an empirically motivated Comprehension Scaling Factor -- has declined from approximately 16,000 tokens (2004 baseline) to an estimated 1,800 tokens (2026, extrapolated from longitudinal behavioural data ending 2020 (Mark, 2023); see Section 9 for uncertainty discussion). The AI-to-human ratio grew from near parity at the ChatGPT launch (November 2022) to 556--1,111x raw and 56--111x quality-adjusted, after accounting for retrieval degradation (Liu et al., 2024; Chroma, 2025). Beyond documenting this divergence, the paper introduces the Delegation Feedback Loop hypothesis: as AI capability grows, the cognitive threshold at which humans delegate to AI falls, extending to tasks of negligible demand; the resulting reduction in cognitive practice may further attenuate the capacities already documented as declining (Gerlich, 2025; Kim et al., 2026; Kosmyna et al., 2025). Neither trend reverses spontaneously. The paper characterises the divergence statistically, reviews neurobiological mechanisms across eight peer-reviewed neuroimaging studies, presents empirical evidence bearing on the delegation threshold, and proposes a research agenda centred on a validated ECS psychometric instrument and longitudinal study of AI-mediated cognitive change.

</details>


### [4] [Do Multilingual VLMs Reason Equally? A Cross-Lingual Visual Reasoning Audit for Indian Languages](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.26742)
*Swastik R*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: First cross-lingual visual reasoning audit for Indian languages shows substantial performance drops of vision-language models when moving from English to six major Indian languages, especially Dravidian ones, and reveals that multilingual pretraining and chain-of-thought prompting do not reliably transfer visual reasoning across scripts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of vision-language models (VLMs) on mathematical, scientific, and spatial reasoning are almost entirely in English, leaving a gap in understanding how well these systems work for speakers of other languages, particularly under-represented Indian languages. There is a need to empirically measure cross-lingual robustness of visual reasoning, especially across diverse language families and scripts, and to test whether current multilingual training and prompting methods actually transfer reasoning ability beyond English.

Method: The author constructs a multilingual visual reasoning benchmark by translating 980 questions from MathVista, ScienceQA, and MMMU into six Indian languages (Hindi, Tamil, Telugu, Bengali, Kannada, Marathi) using the IndicTrans2 machine translation system. They then use Gemini 2.0 Flash for cross-verification on 50 samples per language and report inter-translator agreement scores of 0.79-0.84 to validate translation quality. Eight vision-language models, spanning from 7B open-source models to GPT-4o, are evaluated on all seven languages (English plus six Indic) in multiple settings, including text-only and chain-of-thought prompting ablations, generating a total of 68,600 inference records. Performance across languages and prompt types is compared, with special attention to differences between Indo-Aryan and Dravidian language groups and the behavior of a dedicated multilingual VLM (Aya-Vision-8B).

Result: Switching from English to any of the six Indian languages leads to substantial accuracy drops of 9.8–25 percentage points across the evaluated VLMs. Dravidian languages (e.g., Tamil, Telugu, Kannada) show up to 13.2 percentage points larger degradation than Indo-Aryan languages (e.g., Hindi, Bengali, Marathi). Chain-of-thought prompting, which typically helps in English, actually harms performance in Bengali (-14.4 pp) and Kannada (-11.4 pp), indicating that the learned reasoning patterns are heavily English-centric. Even Aya-Vision-8B, explicitly designed for 23 languages, experiences a 28.5 percentage point drop on Dravidian scripts, demonstrating that multilingual pretraining alone is insufficient for robust cross-lingual visual reasoning. The author also produces a sizable dataset of 68,600 inference records as a by-product of the evaluation.

Conclusion: Vision-language models that perform well on English benchmarks exhibit marked performance degradation on Indian languages in visual reasoning tasks, with particularly severe drops for Dravidian scripts. The findings imply that current multilingual pretraining and chain-of-thought prompting strategies do not effectively transfer visual reasoning capabilities across languages and scripts, and that reasoning chains themselves may be strongly tied to English. The released translated benchmark and model outputs provide a new resource for diagnosing and improving cross-lingual visual reasoning in VLMs, highlighting an urgent need for language- and script-aware training and evaluation protocols.

Abstract: Vision-language models score well on mathematical, scientific, and spatial reasoning benchmarks, yet these evaluations are overwhelmingly English. I present the first cross-lingual visual reasoning audit for Indian languages. 980 questions from MathVista, ScienceQA, and MMMU are translated into Hindi, Tamil, Telugu, Bengali, Kannada, and Marathi using IndicTrans2, with Gemini 2.0 Flash cross-verification on 50 samples per language (inter-translator agreement 0.79-0.84). Eight VLMs, from 7B open-source models to GPT-4o, are evaluated across all seven languages, yielding 68,600 inference records that include text-only and chain-of-thought ablations. I find accuracy drops of 9.8-25 percentage points when switching from English to an Indian language, with Dravidian languages suffering up to 13.2 pp more than Indo-Aryan. Chain-of-thought prompting degrades Bengali (-14.4 pp) and Kannada (-11.4 pp) rather than helping, exposing English-centric reasoning chains. Aya-Vision-8B, built for 23 languages, still drops 28.5 pp on Dravidian scripts; multilingual pretraining alone does not transfer visual reasoning. I release the translated benchmark and all model outputs.

</details>


### [5] [Resolving the Robustness-Precision Trade-off in Financial RAG through Hybrid Document-Routed Retrieval](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.26815)
*Zhiyuan Cheng,Longying Lai,Yue Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They identify a robustness–precision trade-off in RAG for financial QA and propose a hybrid method (HDRR) that routes queries to relevant documents using LLMs and then applies chunk-based retrieval within those documents, achieving better accuracy and fewer failures than prior methods on the FinDER benchmark.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing chunk-based RAG for financial regulatory filings suffers cross-document chunk confusion because filings are structurally similar, causing the system to mix up information between different documents. A newer approach, Semantic File Routing (SFR), avoids such catastrophic errors by routing to whole documents but loses the fine-grained precision of chunk retrieval. There is a need to systematically characterize this trade-off and design a method that is both robust (low failure rate) and precise (high perfect-answer rate).

Method: They perform a controlled evaluation of standard chunk-based retrieval (CBR) and Semantic File Routing (SFR) on the FinDER benchmark of 1,500 questions over regulatory filings, quantifying robustness and precision metrics. Based on the observed trade-off, they propose Hybrid Document-Routed Retrieval (HDRR), a two-stage RAG pipeline: (1) use SFR with LLM-structured outputs to select the most relevant document(s); (2) run chunk-based similarity retrieval only within the routed document set. They then compare HDRR against CBR and SFR across multiple metrics and five query groups.

Result: On FinDER, SFR is more robust than CBR (higher average score: 6.45 vs 6.02; lower failure rate: 10.3% vs 22.5%) but less precise (fewer perfect answers: 8.5% vs 13.8%). HDRR outperforms both: it reaches an average score of 7.54 (25.2% improvement over CBR, 16.9% over SFR), cuts failure rate to 6.4%, boosts correctness to 67.7% (18.7 percentage points above CBR), and raises perfect-answer rate to 20.1% (6.3 points above CBR, 11.6 over SFR). It dominates both baselines on all metrics and across all five query groups.

Conclusion: There is an inherent robustness–precision trade-off between whole-document routing (SFR) and chunk-based retrieval (CBR) in RAG over homogeneous financial corpora. By combining them in a hybrid, two-stage architecture—first routing queries to the right documents, then doing chunk-level retrieval within them—HDRR eliminates cross-document confusion while preserving fine-grained precision and achieves superior performance across all measured dimensions on the FinDER benchmark.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) systems for financial document question answering typically follow a chunk-based paradigm: documents are split into fragments, embedded into vector space, and retrieved via similarity search. While effective in general settings, this approach suffers from cross-document chunk confusion in structurally homogeneous corpora such as regulatory filings. Semantic File Routing (SFR), which uses LLM structured output to route queries to whole documents, reduces catastrophic failures but sacrifices the precision of targeted chunk retrieval. We identify this robustness-precision trade-off through controlled evaluation on the FinDER benchmark (1,500 queries across five groups): SFR achieves higher average scores (6.45 vs. 6.02) and fewer failures (10.3% vs. 22.5%), while chunk-based retrieval (CBR) yields more perfect answers (13.8% vs. 8.5%). To resolve this trade-off, we propose Hybrid Document-Routed Retrieval (HDRR), a two-stage architecture that uses SFR as a document filter followed by chunk-based retrieval scoped to the identified document(s). HDRR eliminates cross-document confusion while preserving targeted chunk precision. Experimental results demonstrate that HDRR achieves the best performance on every metric: an average score of 7.54 (25.2% above CBR, 16.9% above SFR), a failure rate of only 6.4%, a correctness rate of 67.7% (+18.7 pp over CBR), and a perfect-answer rate of 20.1% (+6.3 pp over CBR, +11.6 pp over SFR). HDRR resolves the trade-off by simultaneously achieving the lowest failure rate and the highest precision across all five experimental groups.

</details>


### [6] [Arithmetic OOD Failure Unfolds in Stages in Minimal GPTs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.26828)
*Seine A. Shintani*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper analyzes why small GPT models trained perfectly on 2-digit addition still fail to generalize to 3-digit addition, decomposing the failure into several distinct stages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Arithmetic benchmarks are usually summarized by a single score that hides different types of errors. The authors want to open this black box and understand *mechanistically* why language models that can do 2-digit addition fail on 3-digit addition, especially when all local digit operations are present in training data.

Method: They use a minimal, controlled GPT trained exhaustively on all 2-digit additions, then probe its behavior on 3-digit addition. They design interventions and ablations to isolate successive failure modes: (1) change only the layout (3-digit format) while keeping digit transitions the same; (2) fix layout exposure and probe how the model encodes the hundreds position and carries; (3) manipulate training data to test compositional generalization via conditional recomposition data; (4) run a larger 2-layer model as a bridge experiment; (5) run multi-seed late-stage experiments to fix residual tens-place errors.

Result: They find: (1) a strong layout barrier—models using absolute positional encoding fail under a pure 3-digit layout shift, and only mixed-layout exposure in training alleviates this; (2) after addressing layout, the model treats the hundreds position like a carry flag, not a true digit, and precise carry-focused data can flip the logits correctly while matched extra data cannot; (3) performance on true 3-digit tasks depends critically on data that couples high-order and tail digits (conditional recomposition), which beats various matched controls; (4) after recomposition, remaining mistakes mostly concern tens-only patterns, and a targeted tens-aware intervention significantly boosts accuracy on the hardest thousands-carry tasks (from 0.664 to 0.822).

Conclusion: Out-of-distribution failure in arithmetic for small GPTs is not monolithic but can be decomposed into at least four stages: layout sensitivity, incorrect carry semantics, missing conditional recomposition of digit information, and residual tens-place issues. This decomposition is empirically grounded via controlled experiments and suggests that improving arithmetic generalization requires addressing these distinct mechanisms rather than just adding more training data or using a single accuracy metric.

Abstract: Arithmetic benchmarks are often reduced to a single held-out score, but that score can conflate qualitatively different failures. We study a controlled minimal GPT trained on exhaustive 2-digit addition, where all local digit transitions are already present in training, and ask why 3-digit generalization still fails. The failure is staged. First, there is a layout barrier: a learned absolute-position model collapses under a pure 3-digit layout shift, and mixed-layout exposure is the only intervention that materially weakens this barrier. Second, after layout repair, the hundreds position behaves like a carry flag rather than a semantic hundreds digit; targeted carry probes reverse the relevant logit margin, whereas a matched extra-data control does not. Third, after carry repair, the main remaining bottleneck is conditional recomposition: high-conditioned tail data outperforms a matched control, high-only data, and tail-only data on all true-3-digit suites, and the same ordering reappears in a larger 2-layer bridge experiment. The residual errors after recomposition are then overwhelmingly tens-only, and a separate 10-seed late-stage study shows that a sign-aware tens repair raises exact match on the hardest thousands-carry suite from 0.664 to 0.822. We therefore provide an experimentally testable decomposition of arithmetic OOD failure into layout, carry-semantics, recomposition, and late tens-residual stages.

</details>


### [7] [Magic Words or Methodical Work? Challenging Conventional Wisdom in LLM-Based Political Text Annotation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.26898)
*Lorca McLaren,James Cross,Zuzanna Krakowska,Robin Rauner,Martijn Schoonvelde*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how different LLM pipeline design choices jointly affect political text annotation performance and offers a validation-first framework to reduce arbitrary researcher decisions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Political scientists increasingly use LLMs for text annotation, but there is little systematic understanding of how implementation decisions—such as model choice, size, learning strategy, and prompt style—interact and affect results, raising concerns about hidden researcher degrees of freedom and the robustness of empirical findings.

Method: The authors run a controlled benchmark using six open-weight LLMs on four political science annotation tasks, holding quantisation, hardware, and prompt templates constant. They systematically vary model choice, model size within and across families, learning approaches, and prompt styles, then compare performance and resource use to identify main and interaction effects.

Result: They find strong interaction effects among pipeline components, such that no model, prompt style, or learning approach is consistently superior across tasks. Performance rankings change by task; model size often fails to predict either accuracy or computational cost, with cross-family differences so large that some larger models are cheaper than smaller ones, and mid-sized variants often matching or beating larger versions. Common prompt-engineering recommendations show unstable and sometimes harmful effects on annotation quality.

Conclusion: Because pipeline interaction effects are large, LLM-based annotation workflows in political science are vulnerable to opaque researcher choices that can materially affect findings. The authors propose a validation-first framework that sequences decisions, emphasizes prompt freezing and held-out evaluation, recommends reporting standards, and provides open-source tools to make annotation pipelines more transparent, reproducible, and empirically grounded.

Abstract: Political scientists are rapidly adopting large language models (LLMs) for text annotation, yet the sensitivity of annotation results to implementation choices remains poorly understood. Most evaluations test a single model or configuration; how model choice, model size, learning approach, and prompt style interact, and whether popular "best practices" survive controlled comparison, are largely unexplored. We present a controlled evaluation of these pipeline choices, testing six open-weight models across four political science annotation tasks under identical quantisation, hardware, and prompt-template conditions. Our central finding is methodological: interaction effects dominate main effects, so seemingly reasonable pipeline choices can become consequential researcher degrees of freedom. No single model, prompt style, or learning approach is uniformly superior, and the best-performing model varies across tasks. Two corollaries follow. First, model size is an unreliable guide both to cost and to performance: cross-family efficiency differences are so large that some larger models are less resource-intensive than much smaller alternatives, while within model families mid-range variants often match or exceed larger counterparts. Second, widely recommended prompt engineering techniques yield inconsistent and sometimes negative effects on annotation performance. We use these benchmark results to develop a validation-first framework - with a principled ordering of pipeline decisions, guidance on prompt freezing and held-out evaluation, reporting standards, and open-source tools - to help researchers navigate this decision space transparently.

</details>


### [8] [A large corpus of lucid and non-lucid dream reports](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.26992)
*Remington Mallett*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper builds and validates a very large, labeled corpus of online dream reports—especially lucid dreams—to enable better empirical research on lucid dreaming.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Lucid dreams are rare and hard to induce on demand, so researchers lack a large, systematically labeled dataset to study their phenomenology and test theories or applications of lucidity.

Method: The authors scraped 10 years of anonymous dream journal posts from an online forum, where users self-labeled dreams as lucid, non-lucid, or nightmares. They curated, cleaned, and described a corpus of 55k reports from 5k users, including 10k lucid, 25k non-lucid, and 2k nightmare labels. They then performed descriptive statistics, visualizations, and construct validation by analyzing whether language patterns in lucid-labeled reports matched known features of lucid dreams.

Result: They obtained a large curated corpus of dream reports with user-provided lucidity and nightmare labels and showed, through linguistic analysis and validation, that the lucid labels correspond to established characteristics of lucid dreams.

Conclusion: The corpus—especially its labeled subset—is a valuable resource for dream research and provides a powerful new basis for empirical studies and discoveries in lucid dreaming, helping to address prior data scarcity and phenomenological uncertainty.

Abstract: All varieties of dreaming remain a mystery. Lucid dreams in particular, or those characterized by awareness of the dream, are notoriously difficult to study. Their scarce prevalence and resistance to deliberate induction make it difficult to obtain a sizeable corpus of lucid dream reports. The consequent lack of clarity around lucid dream phenomenology has left the many purported applications of lucidity under-realized. Here, a large corpus of 55k dream reports from 5k contributors is curated, described, and validated for future research. Ten years of publicly available dream reports were scraped from an online forum where users share anonymous dream journals. Importantly, users optionally categorize their dream as lucid, non-lucid, or a nightmare, offering a user-provided labeling system that includes 10k lucid and 25k non-lucid, and 2k nightmare labels. After characterizing the corpus with descriptive statistics and visualizations, construct validation shows that language patterns in lucid-labeled reports are consistent with known characteristics of lucid dreams. While the entire corpus has broad value for dream science, the labeled subset is particularly powerful for new discoveries in lucid dream studies.

</details>


### [9] [The Last Fingerprint: How Markdown Training Shapes LLM Prose](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27006)
*E. M. Freeburg*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates why large language models frequently use em dashes and markdown, arguing that em dash overuse is a residual artifact of markdown-heavy training and fine-tuning, not just a stylistic quirk.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To explain the widely observed but poorly understood phenomenon that LLMs overuse em dashes and default to markdown-style formatting, and to connect these two patterns through a mechanistic account grounded in training data and fine-tuning.

Method: The authors develop a five-step conceptual genealogy linking training corpora, internalized structure, the special status of the em dash, and post-training amplification; they then run suppression experiments on 12 models from five providers, comparing normal prompts, markdown-suppression prompts, and explicit em-dash-prohibition prompts, plus base vs instruct comparisons.

Result: Suppressing markdown formatting almost eliminates obvious markdown features (headings, bullets, bold), but em dashes persist as a structural artifact in most models, with frequencies ranging from 0.0 to 9.1 per 1,000 words; Meta's Llama models uniquely show complete em dash suppression, and even explicit em-dash bans cannot fully remove the artifact in some other models, demonstrating that the tendency precedes RLHF and is modulated (but not created) by fine-tuning.

Conclusion: Em dash frequency in LLM outputs should be viewed as a measurable signature of training data composition and fine-tuning methodology instead of a mere stylistic flaw, linking markdown default behavior and em dash overuse into a single mechanistic account and offering a diagnostic handle on model provenance and tuning choices.

Abstract: Large language models produce em dashes at varying rates, and the observation that some models "overuse" them has become one of the most widely discussed markers of AI-generated text. Yet no mechanistic account of this pattern exists, and the parallel observation that LLMs default to markdown-formatted output has never been connected to it. We propose that the em dash is markdown leaking into prose -- the smallest surviving unit of the structural orientation that LLMs acquire from markdown-saturated training corpora. We present a five-step genealogy connecting training data composition, structural internalization, the dual-register status of the em dash, and post-training amplification. We test this with a two-condition suppression experiment across twelve models from five providers (Anthropic, OpenAI, Meta, Google, DeepSeek): when models are instructed to avoid markdown formatting, overt features (headers, bullets, bold) are eliminated or nearly eliminated, but em dashes persist -- except in Meta's Llama models, which produce none at all. Em dash frequency and suppression resistance vary from 0.0 per 1,000 words (Llama) to 9.1 (GPT-4.1 under suppression), functioning as a signature of the specific fine-tuning procedure applied. A three-condition suppression gradient shows that even explicit em dash prohibition fails to eliminate the artifact in some models, and a base-vs-instruct comparison confirms that the latent tendency exists pre-RLHF. These findings connect two previously isolated online discourses and reframe em dash frequency as a diagnostic of fine-tuning methodology rather than a stylistic defect.

</details>


### [10] [Pashto Common Voice: Building the First Open Speech Corpus for a 60-Million-Speaker Low-Resource Language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27021)
*Hanif Rahman,Shafeeq ur Rehman*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: First open, large-scale Pashto speech corpus built via community over several years; enables big ASR gains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Pashto, despite 60M+ speakers, lacks open speech technology resources. Existing ASR models perform poorly on Pashto (e.g., Whisper Base ~99% WER zero-shot). There is a need for a sizable, open, and diverse corpus to support ASR and speech tech research for this under-resourced language.

Method: They built the Pashto Common Voice corpus within Mozilla Common Voice from 2022–2025. Methods include: localising the Common Voice interface into Pashto; extracting sentences from Wikipedia with automatic filtering to ensure quality and coverage; designing phonemically targeted prompts focusing on four Pashto characters that are frequently dropped; running multi-channel community outreach, including a major campaign via VOA Pashto, to recruit contributors and scale data collection over 10 releases (CV14–CV23). They then fine-tuned the Whisper Base ASR model on one version of the corpus (MCV20) and evaluated WER on its test split.

Result: Corpus grew from 1.5 hours / 5 speakers to 147 hours / 1,483 unique speakers in 10 releases. Latest version (MCV23) has 107,781 clips, of which 60,337 are validated, totaling 82.33 validated hours across 13 domains. Speaker participation jumped ~108x between CV17 and CV18 after the VOA campaign. Fine-tuning Whisper Base on MCV20 reduced WER from 99.0% zero-shot to 13.4% on the MCV20 test set.

Conclusion: A large-scale, community-built, openly licensed Pashto speech corpus is now available via Common Voice, substantially improving the practicality of Pashto ASR. Targeted phoneme coverage and coordinated outreach are effective strategies for building resources for low-resource languages, and fine-tuning on this corpus yields dramatic performance improvements over zero-shot multilingual models.

Abstract: We present the Pashto Common Voice corpus -- the first large-scale, openly licensed speech resource for Pashto, a language with over 60 million native speakers largely absent from open speech technology. Through a community effort spanning 2022-2025, the corpus grew from 1.5 hours and 5 contributors to 147 total hours and 1,483 unique speakers across ten Mozilla Common Voice releases (CV14-CV23). Speaker participation increased approximately 108-fold between CV17 and CV18, coinciding with a VOA Pashto broadcast campaign. We describe the full methodology: interface localisation, Wikipedia-based sentence extraction with automated filtering, phonemically targeted contributions for the four most frequently dropped Pashto characters, and multi-channel community outreach. MCV23 contains 107,781 clips (60,337 validated; 82.33 validated hours) across 13 content domains. Fine-tuning Whisper Base on the MCV20 yields 13.4% WER on the MCV20 test split, against the published Whisper Base zero-shot WER of 99.0% on Pashto.

Audio was recorded at 44.1 kHz (16-bit, stereo). Interviews were fully transcribed, force-aligned at word and phone levels, and anonymized. Descriptively, the Mandarin component totals ~14.7 hours (mean duration 17.3 minutes) and the English component ~15.1 hours (mean duration 17.8 minutes). We report token/type statistics for each language and document code-switching patterns (frequent in Mandarin sessions; more limited in English sessions). The corpus design supports within-/cross-speaker, within/cross-language acoustic comparison and links acoustics to speakers' stated language attitudes, enabling both quantitative and qualitative analyses. The MELI Corpus will be released with transcriptions, alignments, metadata, scans of labelled maps and documentation under a CC BY-NC 4.0 license.

Until now, most data integration systems have leaned on only combining structured data sources. TL;DR: The paper explores how adding social-attribution knowledge (user goals and message context) to prompts can reduce bias and improve LLM performance on social-media behavior analytics tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Attribution theory describes how humans explain others’ behavior using dispositional and situational causes. LLMs trained on human text may implicitly adopt similar biased attribution patterns in social reasoning, but how and to what extent this affects their reasoning is underexplored. Existing reasoning techniques like Chain-of-Thought improve performance but usually ignore social attribution, risking biased outputs in socially sensitive contexts such as behavior analysis on social media. There is a need for methods to explicitly account for and mitigate social-attribution bias while retaining or improving task performance.

Method: The authors propose enriching LLM instruction prompts with two structured prompt aids that encode social-attribution knowledge: (1) the user’s goal to capture dispositional causality and (2) the message context to capture situational causality. This enrichment is applied in a scalable way over social media messages and used in zero-shot classification settings. They then apply this method to behavior analytics tasks—intent detection and theme detection—in the disaster domain, across multiple disaster types and languages, using three open-source LLMs (Llama3, Mistral, Gemma). They compare baseline prompts against prompts augmented with social-attribution aids to measure changes in performance and bias.

Result: Augmenting prompts with social-attribution knowledge improves LLM performance on zero-shot intent and theme detection tasks in the disaster-domain social media data. The experiments also reveal that the baseline behavior of Llama3, Mistral, and Gemma exhibits social-attribution bias, which can be mitigated by the proposed prompt-aid strategy. The approach works across different disaster types and multiple languages, indicating scalability and robustness.

Conclusion: Explicitly incorporating social-attribution knowledge—via user goals (dispositional causes) and contextual information (situational causes)—into LLM prompts can both reduce social-attribution bias and enhance performance in social-media behavior analytics tasks. The method is scalable, language- and disaster-type-agnostic, and effective across multiple open-source LLMs, suggesting that socially informed prompt design is a practical bias-mitigation strategy for LLM-based social reasoning and classification.

Abstract: Attribution theory explains how individuals interpret and attribute others' behavior in a social context by employing personal (dispositional) and impersonal (situational) causality. Large Language Models (LLMs), trained on human-generated corpora, may implicitly mimic this social attribution process in social contexts. However, the extent to which LLMs utilize these causal attributions in their reasoning remains underexplored. Although using reasoning paradigms, such as Chain-of-Thought (CoT), has shown promising results in various tasks, ignoring social attribution in reasoning could lead to biased responses by LLMs in social contexts. In this study, we investigate the impact of incorporating a user's goal as knowledge to infer dispositional causality and message context to infer situational causality on LLM performance. To this end, we introduce a scalable method to mitigate such biases by enriching the instruction prompts for LLMs with two prompt aids using social-attribution knowledge, based on the context and goal of a social media message. This method improves the model performance while reducing the social-attribution bias of the LLM in the reasoning on zero-shot classification tasks for behavior analytics applications. We empirically show the benefits of our method across two tasks-intent detection and theme detection on social media in the disaster domain-when considering the variability of disaster types and multiple languages of social media. Our experiments highlight the biases of three open-source LLMs: Llama3, Mistral, and Gemma, toward social attribution, and show the effectiveness of our mitigation strategies.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors propose Story2Proposal, a multi-agent framework where different agents (architect, writer, refiner, renderer) coordinate via a persistent shared 'visual contract' that encodes section structure and registered visual elements. Result: On Jericho-derived research-writing tasks and across multiple LLM backbones (GPT, Claude, Gemini, Qwen), Story2Proposal achieves substantially higher expert evaluation scores than a direct chat generation baseline (6.145 vs. 3.963, a +2.182 gain). Abstract: Generating scientific manuscripts requires maintaining alignment between narrative reasoning, experimental evidence, and visual artifacts across the document lifecycle. Existing language-model generation pipelines rely on unconstrained text synthesis with validation applied only after generation, often producing structural drift, missing figures or tables, and cross-section inconsistencies. We introduce Story2Proposal, a contract-governed multi-agent framework that converts a research story into a structured manuscript through coordinated agents operating under a persistent shared visual contract. The system organizes architect, writer, refiner, and renderer agents around a contract state that tracks section structure and registered visual elements, while evaluation agents supply feedback in a generate evaluate adapt loop that updates the contract during generation. Experiments on tasks derived from the Jericho research corpus show that Story2Proposal achieved an expert evaluation score of 6.145 versus 3.963 for DirectChat (+2.182) across GPT, Claude, Gemini, and Qwen backbones. Compared with the structured generation baseline Fars, Story2Proposal obtained an average score of 5.705 versus 5.197, indicating improved structural consistency and visual alignment.

### [15] [Routing Sensitivity Without Controllability: A Diagnostic Study of Fairness in MoE Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27141)
*Junhyeok Lee,Kyu Sung Choi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether routing-level interventions in Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) language models can effectively control demographic stereotypes and finds they largely cannot.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: MoE LMs show sensitivity to demographic content via their routing mechanisms, suggesting a potential handle for fairness or stereotype mitigation; the authors want to test how real and usable this handle is across architectures.

Method: They propose FARE (Fairness-Aware Routing Equilibrium), a diagnostic framework that systematically perturbs or constrains routing decisions in various MoE architectures; they then measure stereotype-related log-likelihood preferences, robustness of these shifts, transfer to generated text, and utility trade-offs using benchmarks like CrowS-Pairs and TQA, as well as group-level expert masking analyses.

Result: Across Mixtral, Qwen1.5, Qwen3, DeepSeekMoE, and OLMoE, routing-level stereotype preference shifts are either impossible to induce, unstable/statistically non-robust, or come at the cost of significant performance degradation. Even when robust log-likelihood shifts are achieved, they do not translate into changes in generation behavior. Expert masking shows stereotype bias and essential task knowledge are entangled within the same expert groups.

Conclusion: Routing sensitivity to demographic content is a necessary but not sufficient condition for controllable stereotype mitigation in MoE models. Structural entanglement of bias and knowledge within experts limits feasible routing-based fairness control, highlighting architectural constraints and pointing to design directions for more controllable MoE systems.

Abstract: Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) language models are universally sensitive to demographic content at the routing level, yet exploiting this sensitivity for fairness control is structurally limited. We introduce Fairness-Aware Routing Equilibrium (FARE), a diagnostic framework designed to probe the limits of routing-level stereotype intervention across diverse MoE architectures. FARE reveals that routing-level preference shifts are either unachievable (Mixtral, Qwen1.5, Qwen3), statistically non-robust (DeepSeekMoE), or accompanied by substantial utility cost (OLMoE, -4.4%p CrowS-Pairs at -6.3%p TQA). Critically, even where log-likelihood preference shifts are robust, they do not transfer to decoded generation: expanded evaluations on both non-null models yield null results across all generation metrics. Group-level expert masking reveals why: bias and core knowledge are deeply entangled within expert groups. These findings indicate that routing sensitivity is necessary but insufficient for stereotype control, and identify specific architectural conditions that can inform the design of more controllable future MoE systems.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They recast proposal generation as a time-sliced forecasting task: given a research question and only papers available before a cutoff date, the model must produce a structured proposal. Conclusion: Aligning proposal generation with future research trajectories provides a scalable, verifiable training and evaluation signal for LLMs in scientific ideation. Their Future Alignment Score and time-sliced forecasting setup not only correlate with expert judgments but also produce ideas that lead to measurable downstream improvements, suggesting a practical path toward more impactful AI-assisted research.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly used to assist ideation in research, but evaluating the quality of LLM-generated research proposals remains difficult: novelty and soundness are hard to measure automatically, and large-scale human evaluation is costly. We propose a verifiable alternative by reframing proposal generation as a time-sliced scientific forecasting problem. Given a research question and inspiring papers available before a cutoff time, the model generates a structured proposal and is evaluated by whether it anticipates research directions that appear in papers published after the time. We operationalize this objective with the Future Alignment Score (FAS), computed via retrieval and LLM-based semantic scoring against a held-out future corpus. To train models, we build a time-consistent dataset of 17,771 papers from targets and their pre-cutoff citations, and synthesize reasoning traces that teach gap identification and inspiration borrowing. Across Llama-3.1 and Qwen2.5 models, future-aligned tuning improves future alignment over unaligned baselines (up to +10.6% overall FAS), and domain-expert human evaluation corroborates improved proposal quality. Finally, we demonstrate practical impact by implementing two model-generated proposals with a code agent, obtaining 4.17% accuracy gain on MATH from a new prompting strategy and consistent improvements for a novel model-merging method.

### [17] [Rethinking Easy-to-Hard: Limits of Curriculum Learning in Post-Training for Deductive Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27226)
*Maximilian Mordig,Andreas Opedal,Weiyang Liu,Bernhard Schölkopf*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper empirically studies curriculum learning (CL) for post-training large language models on compositional reasoning tasks and finds that ordering examples by difficulty offers no consistent benefit over random sampling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Curriculum learning is widely used in LLM pre-training and post-training, based on the intuition that learning from easy to hard examples improves generalization, especially for compositional reasoning. However, there is little systematic evidence about whether difficulty-based curricula actually help LLMs on tasks where difficulty reflects reasoning complexity rather than superficial factors.

Method: The authors construct synthetic arithmetic and logical reasoning benchmarks where difficulty is precisely controlled by compositional and deductive complexity. They then post-train several LLM families using different curriculum schedules that order examples by difficulty, comparing them against standard random sampling. They evaluate under both supervised fine-tuning (SFT) and reinforcement learning (RL), and measure accuracy and response length to assess reasoning quality and efficiency.

Result: Across models, tasks, and curriculum schedules, difficulty-based sequencing of training data does not produce a robust improvement over random sampling. Performance in terms of accuracy and response length is largely similar whether training examples are ordered from easy to hard, hard to easy, or presented randomly, in both SFT and RL settings.

Conclusion: For deductive and compositional reasoning tasks, the specific difficulty-based ordering of training examples appears to have negligible impact on LLM post-training outcomes. This challenges the assumed practical value of curriculum learning in this context and suggests that, at least for these types of reasoning problems, simple random sampling is sufficient.

Abstract: Curriculum learning (CL), motivated by the intuition that learning in increasing order of difficulty should ease generalization, is commonly adopted both in pre-training and post-training of large language models (LLMs). The intuition of CL is particularly compelling for compositional reasoning, where complex problems are built from elementary inference rules; however, the actual impact of CL on such tasks remains largely underexplored. We present a systematic empirical study of CL for post-training of LLMs, using synthetic arithmetic and logical benchmarks where difficulty is characterized by reasoning complexity rather than surface-level proxies. Surprisingly, across multiple model families and curriculum schedules, we find no robust advantage in difficulty-based sequencing over standard random sampling in either accuracy or response length. These findings persist across both supervised fine-tuning (SFT) and reinforcement learning (RL) methods. Our study suggests that, in the context of deductive reasoning, the specific ordering of training examples plays a negligible role in achieving compositional generalization, challenging the practical utility of curriculum-based post-training.

The dataset supports computational modeling as well as social and psychological investigations of stress, providing a foundation for future work on mental health and well-being in low-resource and crisis contexts.

  AFSTRESS provides the first Dari resource for computational analysis of stress and well-being in a crisis-affected population.

### [19] [SCOPE: Tree-based Self-Correcting Online Log Parsing via Syntactic-Semantic Collaboration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27247)

  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for a method that preserves the efficiency of heuristics while leveraging semantic understanding only when necessary.

Method: SCOPE introduces a self-correcting online log parsing method built on a bi-directional tree structure that supports efficient template matching from both forward and reverse directions, improving matching rates. Result: Across diverse benchmark datasets, SCOPE achieves better accuracy and efficiency than state-of-the-art log parsing methods, with significantly reduced LLM API calls while maintaining high parsing quality.

Conclusion: By combining a bi-directional tree-based template matcher with a selective, fallback LLM stage, SCOPE effectively balances efficiency and accuracy in log parsing, outperforming prior methods and providing an open implementation and datasets for the community.

Abstract: Log parsing is a critical step for automated log analysis in complex systems. Traditional heuristic-based methods offer high efficiency but are limited in accuracy due to overlooking semantic context. In contrast, recent LLM-based parsers improve accuracy via se mantic understanding but incur high latency from frequent model calls. To address this, we propose SCOPE, the first self-correcting online log parsing method that integrates the strengths of both heuristic and LLM-based paradigms. SCOPE introduces a novel bi-directional tree structure that enables efficient template match ing from both forward and reverse directions, resulting in a higher overall matching rate. Additionally, it adopts a two-stage syntactic semantic collaboration framework: a lightweight NLP model first utilizes part-of-speech (POS) information for syntax-based match ing, while the LLM is selectively invoked as a fallback to handle semantically complex cases when uncertainty remains. This design significantly reduces LLM API usage while maintaining high ac curacy, achieving a balance between efficiency and effectiveness. Extensive evaluations on diverse benchmark datasets show that SCOPE outperforms state-of-the-art methods in both accuracy and efficiency. The implementation and datasets are publicly released to facilitate further research.

### [20] [Mitigating Hallucination on Hallucination in RAG via Ensemble Voting](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27253)
*Zequn Xie,Zhengyang Sun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: VOTE-RAG is a training-free, two-stage RAG framework that uses ensemble voting over queries and answers to reduce compounded hallucinations caused by bad retrieval.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard RAG can still hallucinate badly when the retriever brings back irrelevant or incorrect documents, causing the generator to hallucinate on top of already flawed evidence. Existing solutions are often complex, non-parallel, or require extra training. The authors want a simple, robust, and efficient way to mitigate these RAG-specific hallucinations.

Method: They introduce VOTE-RAG, a two-stage, fully parallelizable framework. In the Retrieval Voting stage, multiple LLM agents independently create diverse queries; all resulting retrieved documents are aggregated. In the Response Voting stage, multiple agents independently answer the user query using the aggregated documents, and a majority-vote scheme chooses the final answer. The approach is training-free and uses ensemble diversity in both retrieval and generation to filter out errors.

Result: On six benchmark datasets, VOTE-RAG matches or outperforms more complex or heavily engineered RAG frameworks in terms of hallucination mitigation and answer quality, while keeping a simpler, parallelizable design.

Conclusion: Ensembling via simple voting at both retrieval and answer stages is an effective, efficient, and training-free way to reduce hallucinations in RAG systems. This ensemble-style design can be preferable to more complex architectures that attempt to explicitly model or correct hallucinations, and it avoids long, iterative interaction chains that risk problem drift.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) aims to reduce hallucinations in Large Language Models (LLMs) by integrating external knowledge. However, RAG introduces a critical challenge: hallucination on hallucination," where flawed retrieval results mislead the generation model, leading to compounded hallucinations. To address this issue, we propose VOTE-RAG, a novel, training-free framework with a two-stage structure and efficient, parallelizable voting mechanisms. VOTE-RAG includes: (1) Retrieval Voting, where multiple agents generate diverse queries in parallel and aggregate all retrieved documents; (2) Response Voting, where multiple agents independently generate answers based on the aggregated documents, with the final output determined by majority vote. We conduct comparative experiments on six benchmark datasets. Our results show that VOTE-RAG achieves performance comparable to or surpassing more complex frameworks. Additionally, VOTE-RAG features a simpler architecture, is fully parallelizable, and avoids the problem drift" risk. Our work demonstrates that simple, reliable ensemble voting is a superior and more efficient method for mitigating RAG hallucinations.

</details>

Motivation: Spirituality is an important but highly abstract construct in religion and theology that is difficult to study computationally because existing social science datasets are small, offline, and not publicly available. There is a lack of large, faithful, and multimodal datasets that capture online spirituality communication, preventing systematic evaluation of modern language and vision-language models on such nuanced content.

Method: The authors collaborated with social scientists to construct SACRED, a carefully annotated multimodal (text and vision) dataset drawn from online spirituality communications, with an emphasis on classification faithfulness. Abstract: In religion and theology studies, spirituality has garnered significant research attention for the reason that it not only transcends culture but offers unique experience to each individual. Abstract: Trustworthy biomedical question answering (QA) systems must not only provide accurate answers but also justify them with current, verifiable evidence. ### [27] [A tree interpretation of arc standard dependency derivation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27459)
*Zihao Huang,Ai Ka Lee,Jungyeul Park*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper defines a deterministic mapping from arc-standard transition sequences for projective dependency parsing to unique ordered phrase-structure-like trees with contiguous yields and stable lexical heads, and shows this mapping exists if and only if the dependency tree is projective.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clarify the structural object implicitly constructed by arc-standard transition-based dependency parsers, and provide a principled way to relate dependency derivations to ordered trees with phrase-structure-like properties (contiguous constituents, explicit headedness), while also characterizing projectivity in these terms.

Method: Formalize a tree-building interpretation of the arc-standard transition system where each SHIFT, LEFTARC, and RIGHTARC deterministically updates an ordered rooted tree with lexical anchors and surface-contiguous yields; prove that this structure uniquely recovers the original dependency arcs and that such a representation exists exactly for projective trees; extend the interpretation to non-projective inputs via pseudo-projective lifting and inversion; implement the mapping inside a neural transition-based parser to test executability and recovery quality.

Result: The authors prove a bijection between projective dependency trees and their proposed ordered tree representations as realized by arc-standard derivations; prove that the existence of such a representation is equivalent to projectivity; and demonstrate in a neural transition-based parser that mapped derivations can be executed and used to reliably recover dependencies, including in a pseudo-projective setup for non-projective data.

Conclusion: Arc-standard transition sequences can be seen as directly constructing a unique, phrase-structure-like ordered tree with contiguous yields and stable lexical heads, and this view precisely characterizes projective dependency trees; the representation is practically usable within standard neural transition-based parsers and can be extended to non-projective data via pseudo-projective techniques.

Abstract: We show that arc-standard derivations for projective dependency trees determine a unique ordered tree representation with surface-contiguous yields and stable lexical anchoring. Each \textsc{shift}, \textsc{leftarc}, and \textsc{rightarc} transition corresponds to a deterministic tree update, and the resulting hierarchical object uniquely determines the original dependency arcs. We further show that this representation characterizes projectivity: a single-headed dependency tree admits such a contiguous ordered representation if and only if it is projective. The proposal is derivational rather than convertive. It interprets arc-standard transition sequences directly as ordered tree construction, rather than transforming a completed dependency graph into a phrase-structure output. For non-projective inputs, the same interpretation can be used in practice via pseudo-projective lifting before derivation and inverse decoding after recovery. A proof-of-concept implementation in a standard neural transition-based parser shows that the mapped derivations are executable and support stable dependency recovery.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They formalize long-horizon success as a probabilistic tradeoff between search efficiency (how quickly good states are reached) and terminal precision (quality at the final step). Based on this, they design AgentSwing: at designated trigger points during an agent's trajectory, it spawns multiple branches that each apply different context management strategies in parallel; a lookahead routing mechanism evaluates these branches and selects the most promising one to continue, effectively adapting the context strategy to the current state.

Result: Across various benchmarks and different LLM agent backbones, AgentSwing consistently surpasses strong static context management baselines, achieving comparable or better performance with up to 3x fewer interaction turns and improving the maximum attainable performance of long-horizon web agents.

Conclusion: Adaptive, state-aware parallel context management with probabilistic routing (AgentSwing) is more effective than fixed strategies for long-horizon information-seeking agents, and the introduced probabilistic framework offers a general, principled foundation for analyzing and designing future context management approaches.

Abstract: As large language models (LLMs) evolve into autonomous agents for long-horizon information-seeking, managing finite context capacity has become a critical bottleneck. Existing context management methods typically commit to a single fixed strategy throughout the entire trajectory. Such static designs may work well in some states, but they cannot adapt as the usefulness and reliability of the accumulated context evolve during long-horizon search. To formalize this challenge, we introduce a probabilistic framework that characterizes long-horizon success through two complementary dimensions: search efficiency and terminal precision. Building on this perspective, we propose AgentSwing, a state-aware adaptive parallel context management routing framework. At each trigger point, AgentSwing expands multiple context-managed branches in parallel and uses lookahead routing to select the most promising continuation. Experiments across diverse benchmarks and agent backbones show that AgentSwing consistently outperforms strong static context management methods, often matching or exceeding their performance with up to $3\times$ fewer interaction turns while also improving the ultimate performance ceiling of long-horizon web agents. Beyond the empirical gains, the proposed probabilistic framework provides a principled lens for analyzing and designing future context management strategies for long-horizon agents.

*Utsav Maskey,Mark Dras,Usman Naseem*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies why aligned language models sometimes refuse safe requests (over-refusal) and shows that harmful refusals and over-refusals are encoded differently in model representations, implying that fixing over-refusal needs task-specific rather than global interventions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Aligned language models are trained to refuse harmful requests, but they frequently over-refuse, declining benign prompts that superficially resemble harmful ones. Existing attempts to fix this by modifying a single global “refusal direction” in representation space only partially work and can damage the intended safety behavior. The motivation is to mechanistically understand the internal geometry of refusals vs over-refusals so we can design better, targeted interventions that reduce over-refusal without compromising safety.

Method: The authors analyze the representational geometry of transformer hidden states associated with harmful-refusal and over-refusal behaviors. They attempt to identify and characterize directions in representation space that correspond to refusal. They compare global refusal directions with task-specific ones, study where these vectors lie relative to benign task clusters, estimate dimensionality of the relevant subspaces, and use linear probing across layers to see how well harmful vs over-refusal states can be distinguished over the network depth.

Result: They find that harmful-refusal directions are largely task-agnostic and can be captured by a single global vector in representation space. In contrast, over-refusal directions are highly task-dependent: they lie within benign task clusters, differ across tasks, and occupy a higher-dimensional subspace. Linear probes show that harmful refusals and over-refusals are separable in the model’s representations from early layers onward.

Conclusion: Global direction ablation can weaken the generic harmful-refusal mechanism but cannot directly and selectively correct over-refusal, because the latter is encoded in a distributed, task-specific way. Addressing over-refusal therefore requires task-aware, higher-dimensional geometric interventions in representation space rather than a single global steering direction.

Abstract: Aligned language models that are trained to refuse harmful requests also exhibit over-refusal: they decline safe instructions that seemingly resemble harmful instructions. A natural approach is to ablate the global refusal direction, steering the hidden-state vectors away or towards the harmful-refusal examples, but this corrects over-refusal only incidentally while disrupting the broader refusal mechanism. In this work, we analyse the representational geometry of both refusal types to understand why this happens. We show that harmful-refusal directions are task-agnostic and can be captured by a single global vector, whereas over-refusal directions are task-dependent: they reside within the benign task-representation clusters, vary across tasks, and span a higher-dimensional subspace. Linear probing confirms that the two refusal types are representationally distinct from the early transformer layers. These findings provide a mechanistic explanation of why global direction ablation alone cannot address over-refusal, and establish that task-specific geometric interventions are necessary.

Experiments on three VLM architectures demonstrate that Hidden Ads achieves high injection efficacy with near-zero false positives while maintaining task accuracy. Ablation studies confirm that the attack is data-efficient, transfers effectively to unseen datasets, and scales to multiple concurrent domain-slogan pairs. We evaluate defenses including instruction-based filtering and clean fine-tuning, finding that both fail to remove the backdoor without causing significant utility degradation.

We restate the minimum arborescence objective in Bock's notation and provide a complete line by line execution trace of the original ten node example, extending the partial trace given in the source paper from initialization to termination. We then introduce a structured reformulation that makes explicit the procedure's phase structure, maintained state, and control flow, while preserving the logic of the original method. ### [32] [Umwelt Engineering: Designing the Cognitive Worlds of Linguistic Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27626)
*Rodney Jehu-Appiah*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces 'Umwelt engineering'—designing the linguistic environment of AI agents—as an upstream design layer, showing via experiments that constraining language (e.g., removing 'to have' or 'to be') measurably changes and can improve model reasoning and performance, especially in ensembles.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work focuses on prompt and context engineering to steer language model behavior, but largely assumes a fixed underlying linguistic medium. The author argues that the structure of language itself shapes cognition, so deliberately redesigning or constraining an agent’s linguistic environment (its Umwelt) could provide a powerful, underexplored way to influence reasoning, ethics, and problem-solving. The paper seeks empirical evidence that changing the medium of reasoning (through vocabulary and grammatical constraints) systematically alters cognition and performance in useful ways.

Method: The paper defines Umwelt engineering as designing linguistic constraints for agents, then runs two experiments. In Experiment 1, three different language models are forced to reason under two vocabulary constraints: (1) No-Have, which prohibits possessive constructions using 'to have', and (2) E-Prime, which prohibits all uses of 'to be'. Across seven reasoning and evaluation tasks totaling 4,470 trials, the author measures task performance, ethical reasoning, classification accuracy, epistemic calibration, cross-model correlations, and constraint compliance. In Experiment 2, 16 different constrained agents (each using different linguistic constraints, including a counterfactual agent) work on 17 debugging problems. Their individual and ensemble performances are compared to an unconstrained control agent, and permutation tests are used to assess how often random 3-agent ensembles achieve full ground-truth coverage.

Result: In Experiment 1, the No-Have constraint substantially improves performance in several areas compared with the unconstrained baseline: ethical reasoning increases by 19.1 percentage points (p < 0.001), classification accuracy by 6.5 percentage points (p < 0.001), and epistemic calibration by 7.4 percentage points, with models adhering to the constraint 92.8% of the time. E-Prime has strong but mixed effects depending on the model, with cross-model performance correlations as low as r = -0.75, indicating divergent impacts on cognition. In Experiment 2, none of the 16 constrained agents individually outperforms the unconstrained control on 17 debugging tasks. However, a 3-agent ensemble of constrained agents achieves 100% coverage of ground-truth issues versus 88.2% for the control. A permutation test shows only 8% of random 3-agent subsets achieve full coverage, and every successful trio includes the counterfactual agent, supporting the value of cognitive diversification through different linguistic constraints.

Conclusion: The study concludes that systematically engineering an agent’s linguistic Umwelt is a meaningful new design lever: altering the linguistic medium of reasoning can reconfigure cognition (cognitive restructuring) and, when multiple constraints are combined, diversify reasoning styles (cognitive diversification) in ways that improve collective performance. Umwelt engineering thus merits recognition as a distinct, upstream layer above prompt and context engineering. The primary caveat is that the experiments lack an active control condition with prompts as elaborate as the constraint prompts, leaving some uncertainty about how much of the effect is due to constraint content versus prompt complexity.

Abstract: I propose Umwelt engineering -- the deliberate design of the linguistic cognitive environment -- as a third layer in the agent design stack, upstream of both prompt and context engineering. Two experiments test the thesis that altering the medium of reasoning alters cognition itself. In Experiment 1, three language models reason under two vocabulary constraints -- No-Have (eliminating possessive "to have") and E-Prime (eliminating "to be") -- across seven tasks (N=4,470 trials). No-Have improves ethical reasoning by 19.1 pp (p < 0.001), classification by 6.5 pp (p < 0.001), and epistemic calibration by 7.4 pp, while achieving 92.8% constraint compliance. E-Prime shows dramatic but model-dependent effects: cross-model correlations reach r = -0.75. In Experiment 2, 16 linguistically constrained agents tackle 17 debugging problems. No constrained agent outperforms the control individually, yet a 3-agent ensemble achieves 100% ground-truth coverage versus 88.2% for the control. A permutation test confirms only 8% of random 3-agent subsets achieve full coverage, and every successful subset contains the counterfactual agent. Two mechanisms emerge: cognitive restructuring and cognitive diversification. The primary limitation is the absence of an active control matching constraint prompt elaborateness.

### [33] [PRBench: End-to-end Paper Reproduction in Physics Research](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27646)

The analysis reveals common failure patterns, such as incorrect implementation of formulas, inability to debug numerical simulations, and fabrication of output data.

Conclusion: Current AI coding agents are far from being able to autonomously reproduce real scientific papers end-to-end, even in a controlled environment with full paper access. PRBench serves as a rigorous, realistic benchmark to highlight these limitations, characterize failure modes, and track future progress toward more capable autonomous scientific research agents.

Abstract: AI agents powered by large language models exhibit strong reasoning and problem-solving capabilities, enabling them to assist scientific research tasks such as formula derivation and code generation. Each task requires an agent to comprehend the methodology of a published paper, implement the corresponding algorithms from scratch, and produce quantitative results matching the original publication. Agents are provided only with the task instruction and paper content, and operate in a sandboxed execution environment. All tasks are contributed by domain experts from over 20 research groups at the School of Physics, Peking University, each grounded in a real published paper and validated through end-to-end reproduction with verified ground-truth results and detailed scoring rubrics. Using an agentified assessment pipeline, we evaluate a set of coding agents on PRBench and analyze their capabilities across key dimensions of scientific reasoning and execution. We further identify systematic failure modes, including errors in formula implementation, inability to debug numerical simulations, and fabrication of output data. Overall, PRBench provides a rigorous benchmark for evaluating progress toward autonomous scientific research.
