### [34] [Budget-Xfer: Budget-Constrained Source Language Selection for Cross-Lingual Transfer to African Languages](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27651)
*Tewodros Kederalah Idris,Roald Eiselen,Prasenjit Mitra*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Budget-Xfer, a framework that treats multi-source cross-lingual transfer as a budget-constrained allocation problem, showing that using multiple source languages is better than a single source under a fixed annotation budget, while specific multi-source allocation strategies matter less.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work on cross-lingual transfer for low-resource languages compares different source language choices but usually changes the total amount of training data at the same time. This confounds the effect of which languages are selected with the effect of how much data is used. There is a need for a principled framework that, under a fixed labeling or annotation budget, can guide how many and which source languages to pick and how much data to take from each for optimal transfer.

Method: The authors introduce Budget-Xfer, which formulates multi-source cross-lingual transfer as a budget-constrained resource allocation optimization problem. Under a fixed annotation budget B, the framework jointly decides (a) which source languages to select and (b) how many labeled instances to allocate from each. They instantiate and empirically compare four different allocation strategies within this framework. Experiments are run on two tasks—named entity recognition and sentiment analysis—for three African target languages (Hausa, Yoruba, Swahili) and two multilingual models, resulting in 288 total experimental configurations.

Result: Across all experiments, multi-source cross-lingual transfer consistently and significantly outperforms single-source transfer, with large effect sizes (Cohen's d ranging from 0.80 to 1.98). This improvement is attributed to a "structural budget underutilization bottleneck" when relying on a single source language under a fixed budget. However, among the different multi-source allocation strategies, performance differences are comparatively small and not statistically significant. Furthermore, the utility of using embedding similarity between languages as a proxy for source language selection depends on the task: random selection beats similarity-based selection for NER, whereas similarity-based selection helps more for sentiment analysis.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that, under a fixed annotation budget, it is generally better to use multiple source languages than a single source for cross-lingual transfer to low-resource targets. However, the precise multi-source allocation strategy is less critical than simply avoiding single-source setups. Additionally, commonly used heuristics like embedding similarity are not universally reliable: their value is task-dependent, so practitioners should validate or tune selection strategies per task rather than relying on similarity alone.

Abstract: Cross-lingual transfer learning enables NLP for low-resource languages by leveraging labeled data from higher-resource sources, yet existing comparisons of source language selection strategies do not control for total training data, confounding language selection effects with data quantity effects. We introduce Budget-Xfer, a framework that formulates multi-source cross-lingual transfer as a budget-constrained resource allocation problem. Given a fixed annotation budget B, our framework jointly optimizes which source languages to include and how much data to allocate from each. We evaluate four allocation strategies across named entity recognition and sentiment analysis for three African target languages (Hausa, Yoruba, Swahili) using two multilingual models, conducting 288 experiments. Our results show that (1) multi-source transfer significantly outperforms single-source transfer (Cohen's d = 0.80 to 1.98), driven by a structural budget underutilization bottleneck; (2) among multi-source strategies, differences are modest and non-significant; and (3) the value of embedding similarity as a selection proxy is task-dependent, with random selection outperforming similarity-based selection for NER but not sentiment analysis.

</details>


### [35] [The Degree of Language Diacriticity and Its Effect on Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27653)
*Adi Cohen,Yuval Pinter*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a quantitative, cross-linguistic framework to measure diacritic complexity and shows that higher complexity makes automatic diacritic restoration harder.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diacritics are crucial in many scripts for pronunciation and meaning, but their technological impact has only been studied language by language, without a unified, data-driven way to compare how much different writing systems rely on them or how that affects NLP tasks.

Method: The authors design corpus-based, information-theoretic metrics capturing how often diacritics appear, how ambiguous character–diacritic mappings are, and how structurally diverse diacritic combinations are. They compute these metrics for 24 corpora across 15 languages (covering scripts with single vs. multiple diacritics per character) and correlate them with the performance of BERT- and RNN-based models on diacritic restoration.

Result: They find a strong negative correlation between diacritic complexity and restoration accuracy: languages with more complex diacritic systems are harder for models to restore. In single-diacritic scripts, frequency-based and structural metrics behave similarly, but in multi-diacritic scripts, structural complexity best predicts model performance, outperforming purely frequency-based measures.

Conclusion: Quantifiable properties of diacritic systems—especially structural complexity in multi-diacritic scripts—directly impact the difficulty of diacritic restoration for neural models, indicating that orthographic complexity is not just a descriptive notion but has concrete consequences for language technology performance.

Abstract: Diacritics are orthographic marks that clarify pronunciation, distinguish similar words, or alter meaning. They play a central role in many writing systems, yet their impact on language technology has not been systematically quantified across scripts. While prior work has examined diacritics in individual languages, there's no cross-linguistic, data-driven framework for measuring the degree to which writing systems rely on them and how this affects downstream tasks. We propose a data-driven framework for quantifying diacritic complexity using corpus-level, information-theoretic metrics that capture the frequency, ambiguity, and structural diversity of character-diacritic combinations. We compute these metrics over 24 corpora in 15 languages, spanning both single- and multi-diacritic scripts. We then examine how diacritic complexity correlates with performance on the task of diacritics restoration, evaluating BERT- and RNN-based models. We find that across languages, higher diacritic complexity is strongly associated with lower restoration accuracy. In single-diacritic scripts, where character-diacritic combinations are more predictable, frequency-based and structural measures largely align. In multi-diacritic scripts, however, structural complexity exhibits the strongest association with performance, surpassing frequency-based measures. These findings show that measurable properties of diacritic usage influence the performance of diacritic restoration models, demonstrating that orthographic complexity is not only descriptive but functionally relevant for modeling.

Method: The authors construct a benchmark based on the longitudinal publication histories of 217 AI researchers across diverse subfields, treating each author’s body of work as an externalization of their cognitive process. They then evaluate several state-of-the-art LLMs and enhancement techniques on this benchmark.

Result: The study delivers an initial empirical assessment of how well current LLMs align with human-like cognitive trajectories at the individual level and how much various enhancement strategies improve this alignment, revealing current capabilities and limitations. Specific numerical results are not in the abstract, but the work establishes baseline performance and comparative insights across models and techniques.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the proposed benchmark and cognitive alignment metric provide a principled way to distinguish genuine cognitive pattern transfer from superficial behavioral imitation in LLMs. It positions their work as a first-step empirical foundation for rigorously studying to what extent LLMs can simulate human cognition and how far existing methods can enhance these abilities.

Abstract: An essential problem in artificial intelligence is whether LLMs can simulate human cognition or merely imitate surface-level behaviors, while existing datasets suffer from either synthetic reasoning traces or population-level aggregation, failing to capture authentic individual cognitive patterns. We introduce a benchmark grounded in the longitudinal research trajectories of 217 researchers across diverse domains of artificial intelligence, where each author's scientific publications serve as an externalized representation of their cognitive processes. To distinguish whether LLMs transfer cognitive patterns or merely imitate behaviors, our benchmark deliberately employs a cross-domain, temporal-shift generalization setting. A multidimensional cognitive alignment metric is further proposed to assess individual-level cognitive consistency. Through systematic evaluation of state-of-the-art LLMs and various enhancement techniques, we provide a first-stage empirical study on the questions: (1) How well do current LLMs simulate human cognition? and (2) How far can existing techniques enhance these capabilities?

*Fengxiang Li,Han Zhang,Haoyang Huang,Jinghui Wang,Jinhua Hao,Kun Yuan,Mengtong Li,Minglei Zhang,Pengcheng Xu,Wenhao Zhuang,Yizhen Shao,Zongxian Feng,Can Tang,Chao Wang,Chengxiao Tong,Fan Yang,Gang Xiong,Haixuan Gao,Han Gao,Hao Wang,Haochen Liu,Hongliang Sun,Jiabao Li,Jingwen Chang,Jun Du,Junyi Peng,Leizhen Cui,Meimei Jing,Mingqi Wu,Shangpeng Yan,Shaotong Qi,Suzhe Xu,Wenxuan Zhao,Xianda Sun,Xuan Xie,Yanbo Wang,Yao Xia,Yinghan Cui,Yingpeng Chen,Yong Wang,Yuze Shi,Zhiwei Shen,Ziyu Wang,Ming Sun,Lin Ye,Bin Chen*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Improve agentic code-generation systems across diverse domains (software engineering, web, terminal, web search, and general tasks) by moving beyond monolithic models, addressing scalability of RL for agentic coding, efficiency of training over tree-structured trajectories, and stability issues in MoE reinforcement learning, while achieving competitive real-world benchmark performance and releasing a practical open model.

Method: 1) Use a "Specialize-then-Unify" paradigm: decompose agentic coding into five expert domains (SWE, WebCoding, Terminal, WebSearch, General). Each expert is first trained with supervised fine-tuning and then further optimized via reinforcement learning on its domain-specific tasks. 2) After expert training, consolidate them into a single unified model using on-policy distillation, transferring capabilities of all experts into one agentic coder. 3) Build KwaiEnv, a modular infrastructure that can run tens of thousands of concurrent sandbox instances to support large-scale RL, and scale RL training along three axes: task complexity, intent alignment, and scaffold generalization. 4) Propose MCLA to stabilize mixture-of-experts RL training. 5) Introduce Tree Training to exploit tree-structured trajectories during RL and remove redundant computation, yielding up to 6.2x speedup.

Result: KAT-Coder-V2 reaches 79.6% on SWE-bench Verified, close to Claude Opus 4.6 at 80.8%; scores 88.7 on PinchBench, outperforming GLM-5 and MiniMax M2.7; ranks first in all three frontend aesthetics scenarios; and remains strong as a generalist agent with 46.8 on Terminal-Bench Hard and 93.9 on tau^2-Bench. The system infrastructure achieves significant training efficiency gains (up to 6.2x) while maintaining stable MoE RL training.

Conclusion: A specialized-then-unified agentic coding architecture, supported by scalable RL infrastructure and efficiency/stability techniques (MCLA, Tree Training), can produce an open, competitive, and versatile coding agent that rivals strong proprietary models across software engineering, web coding, terminal interaction, and general tool-use benchmarks.

Abstract: We present KAT-Coder-V2, an agentic coding model developed by the KwaiKAT team at Kuaishou. KAT-Coder-V2 adopts a "Specialize-then-Unify" paradigm that decomposes agentic coding into five expert domains - SWE, WebCoding, Terminal, WebSearch, and General - each undergoing independent supervised fine-tuning and reinforcement learning, before being consolidated into a single model via on-policy distillation. We develop KwaiEnv, a modular infrastructure sustaining tens of thousands of concurrent sandbox instances, and scale RL training along task complexity, intent alignment, and scaffold generalization. We further propose MCLA for stabilizing MoE RL training and Tree Training for eliminating redundant computation over tree-structured trajectories with up to 6.2x speedup. KAT-Coder-V2 achieves 79.6% on SWE-bench Verified (vs. Claude Opus 4.6 at 80.8%), 88.7 on PinchBench (surpassing GLM-5 and MiniMax M2.7), ranks first across all three frontend aesthetics scenarios, and maintains strong generalist scores on Terminal-Bench Hard (46.8) and tau^2-Bench (93.9). Our model is publicly available at https://streamlake.com/product/kat-coder.

### [39] [Retromorphic Testing with Hierarchical Verification for Hallucination Detection in RAG](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27752)
*Boxi Yu,Yuzhong Zhang,Liting Lin,Lionel Briand,Emir Muñoz*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces RT4CHART, a fine-grained hallucination detection framework for RAG systems that breaks down outputs into verifiable claims and hierarchically checks them against retrieved context, achieving state-of-the-art performance and revealing more hallucinations than prior benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs in RAG settings still hallucinate, and current faithfulness evaluation methods are either coarse-grained or not tightly grounded in retrieved evidence, making it hard to pinpoint and interpret specific hallucinations.

Method: RT4CHART decomposes answers into atomic claims, then performs hierarchical, local-to-global verification of each claim against the retrieved context, labeling them as entailed, contradicted, or baseless, and aligning these labels with specific answer spans and supporting/refuting evidence; the framework is evaluated on re-annotated RAGTruth++ and RAGTruth-Enhance benchmarks, along with ablation studies on its components.

Result: RT4CHART attains state-of-the-art hallucination detection: on RAGTruth++ it achieves an answer-level F1 of 0.776 (83% better than the strongest baseline), and on RAGTruth-Enhance it obtains a span-level F1 of 47.5%; the new annotations expose 1.68x more hallucination cases than in the original benchmark.

Conclusion: Hierarchical, claim-level, evidence-grounded verification significantly improves hallucination detection for RAG systems and enables interpretable auditing, while existing benchmarks underestimate hallucination prevalence, underscoring the need for finer-grained evaluation frameworks like RT4CHART.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) continue to hallucinate in retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), producing claims that are unsupported by or conflict with the retrieved context. Detecting such errors remains challenging when faithfulness is evaluated solely with respect to the retrieved context. Existing approaches either provide coarse-grained, answer-level scores or focus on open-domain factuality, often lacking fine-grained, evidence-grounded diagnostics.
  We present RT4CHART, a retromorphic testing framework for context-faithfulness assessment. RT4CHART decomposes model outputs into independently verifiable claims and performs hierarchical, local-to-global verification against the retrieved context. Each claim is assigned one of three labels: entailed, contradicted, or baseless. Furthermore, RT4CHART maps claim-level decisions back to specific answer spans and retrieves explicit supporting or refuting evidence from the context, enabling fine-grained and interpretable auditing.
  We evaluate RT4CHART on RAGTruth++ (408 samples) and RAGTruth-Enhance (2,675 samples), a newly re-annotated benchmark. RT4CHART achieves the best answer-level hallucination detection F1 among all baselines. On RAGTruth++, it reaches an F1 score of 0.776, outperforming the strongest baseline by 83%. On RAGTruth-Enhance, it achieves a span-level F1 of 47.5%.
  Ablation studies show that the hierarchical verification design is the primary driver of performance gains. Finally, our re-annotation reveals 1.68x more hallucination cases than the original labels, suggesting that existing benchmarks substantially underestimate the prevalence of hallucinations.

In this work, we present the first systematic study of long-tail entities in Data-to-Text generation. We introduce TailNLG, a new multilingual benchmark in English, Italian, and Spanish, built from Wikidata and covering entities with varying levels of popularity. We evaluate three different families of large language models in zero-shot settings and compare their performance on rare versus common entities, as well as against the established WebNLG benchmark. ### [41] [Understanding Teacher Revisions of Large Language Model-Generated Feedback](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27806)
*Conrad Borchers,Luiz Rodrigues,Newarney Torrezão da Costa,Cleon Xavier,Rafael Ferreira Mello*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Study of how teachers revise AI-generated formative feedback for students.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As large language models are used to generate feedback for students, it is important to understand how teachers interact with, modify, and ultimately control what AI-produced feedback reaches learners, because these human-in-the-loop decisions determine educational impact and tool effectiveness.

Method: Collected 1,349 pairs of AI-generated feedback and teacher-edited explanations from 117 teachers; conducted quantitative analysis of revision frequency and length, modeled revision likelihood using ML with sentence-embedding features to predict whether AI feedback will be revised, and performed qualitative coding to examine how revisions change the pedagogical type and style of feedback.

Result: Teachers left AI feedback unchanged in about 80% of cases; when they edited, they typically shortened initially longer AI feedback. Editing frequency varied widely by teacher, with about half never editing and about 10% editing most (over two-thirds) of instances. A classifier using only the AI feedback text achieved AUC=0.75 in predicting which feedback would be revised. Qualitative analysis found that revisions often simplified feedback, moving from detailed explanations toward concise, corrective messages.

Conclusion: Teachers generally accept AI-generated feedback but show diverse editing practices; when they do revise, they tend to simplify and shorten feedback. These patterns suggest that AI feedback tools should be designed to better reflect teacher preferences for brevity and corrective focus, and to minimize unnecessary editing effort while preserving pedagogical quality.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) increasingly generate formative feedback for students, yet little is known about how teachers revise this feedback before it reaches learners. Teachers' revisions shape what students receive, making revision practices central to evaluating AI classroom tools. We analyze a dataset of 1,349 instances of AI-generated feedback and corresponding teacher-edited explanations from 117 teachers. We examine (i) textual characteristics associated with teacher revisions, (ii) whether revision decisions can be predicted from the AI feedback text, and (iii) how revisions change the pedagogical type of feedback delivered. First, we find that teachers accept AI feedback without modification in about 80% of cases, while edited feedback tends to be significantly longer and subsequently shortened by teachers. Editing behavior varies substantially across teachers: about 50% never edit AI feedback, and only about 10% edit more than two-thirds of feedback instances. Second, machine learning models trained only on the AI feedback text as input features, using sentence embeddings, achieve fair performance in identifying which feedback will be revised (AUC=0.75). Third, qualitative coding shows that when revisions occur, teachers often simplify AI-generated feedback, shifting it away from high-information explanations toward more concise, corrective forms. Together, these findings characterize how teachers engage with AI-generated feedback in practice and highlight opportunities to design feedback systems that better align with teacher priorities while reducing unnecessary editing effort.

### [43] [Improving Clinical Diagnosis with Counterfactual Multi-Agent Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27820)
*Zhiwen You,Xi Chen,Aniket Vashishtha,Simo Du,Gabriel Erion-Barner,Hongyuan Mei,Hao Peng,Yue Guo*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM-based diagnostic tools usually reason over static clinical evidence and do not explicitly test how individual findings support or contradict alternative diagnoses, making their reasoning less interpretable and potentially less reliable. Inspired by how clinicians are trained via counterfactual questioning, the authors aim to make LLM diagnostic reasoning more transparent, robust, and faithful to clinical practice.

Method: The authors design a counterfactual multi-agent diagnostic framework in which specialist LLM agents iteratively discuss and refine differential diagnoses. A key component is counterfactual case editing, where specific clinical findings (symptoms, signs, labs) are modified, removed, or altered to see how diagnostic predictions change. They define a Counterfactual Probability Gap metric to quantify how much each finding supports a diagnosis based on changes in model confidence under these edits, and they feed these signals back into multi-round agent discussions to challenge weak hypotheses and prioritize better-supported ones.

Result: On three diagnostic benchmarks and across seven different LLMs, the proposed framework consistently outperforms standard prompting and prior multi-agent baselines in diagnostic accuracy, with the largest accuracy gains in complex or ambiguous cases. Human expert evaluation also judges the generated reasoning as more clinically useful, reliable, and coherent than that of competing methods.

Conclusion: Explicitly incorporating counterfactual evidence verification into LLM-based diagnostic systems improves both diagnostic performance and interpretability, aligning model reasoning more closely with clinical practice. The approach represents a promising direction for building more reliable and transparent AI tools for clinical decision support.

Abstract: Clinical diagnosis is a complex reasoning process in which clinicians gather evidence, form hypotheses, and test them against alternative explanations. In medical training, this reasoning is explicitly developed through counterfactual questioning--e.g., asking how a diagnosis would change if a key symptom were absent or altered--to strengthen differential diagnosis skills. As large language model (LLM)-based systems are increasingly used for diagnostic support, ensuring the interpretability of their recommendations becomes critical. However, most existing LLM-based diagnostic agents reason over fixed clinical evidence without explicitly testing how individual findings support or weaken competing diagnoses. In this work, we propose a counterfactual multi-agent diagnostic framework inspired by clinician training that makes hypothesis testing explicit and evidence-grounded. Our framework introduces counterfactual case editing to modify clinical findings and evaluate how these changes affect competing diagnoses. We further define the Counterfactual Probability Gap, a method that quantifies how strongly individual findings support a diagnosis by measuring confidence shifts under these edits. These counterfactual signals guide multi-round specialist discussions, enabling agents to challenge unsupported hypotheses, refine differential diagnoses, and produce more interpretable reasoning trajectories. Across three diagnostic benchmarks and seven LLMs, our method consistently improves diagnostic accuracy over prompting and prior multi-agent baselines, with the largest gains observed in complex and ambiguous cases. Human evaluation further indicates that our framework produces more clinically useful, reliable, and coherent reasoning. These results suggest that incorporating counterfactual evidence verification is an important step toward building reliable AI systems for clinical decision support.

### [44] [ProText: A benchmark dataset for measuring (mis)gendering in long-form texts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27838)

TL;DR: ProText is a dataset to evaluate how well models handle gendering and misgendering in long-form English text transformations.

They then use this dataset to probe state-of-the-art LLMs on text transformation tasks and analyze their gendering behavior.

We validated ProText through a mini case study, showing that even with just two prompts and two models, we can draw nuanced insights regarding gender bias, stereotyping, misgendering, and gendering. ### [45] [Model Capability Dominates: Inference-Time Optimization Lessons from AIMO 3](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27844)
*Natapong Nitarach*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether using diverse prompting strategies for multiple LLM reasoning attempts improves math problem solving beyond simple high-temperature sampling, and finds it does not.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To investigate if structured diversity in LLM reasoning strategies (via prompts) can meaningfully decorrelate errors in majority-vote ensembles, thereby improving mathematical reasoning on challenging IMO-level problems.

Method: They implement a 'Diverse Prompt Mixer' that assigns distinct reasoning strategies through different prompts to multiple LLM voters, and run 23+ experiments across 3 models on 50 AIMO/IMO-level problems on a single H100 GPU under a 5-hour time limit, comparing to standard high-temperature sampling and various inference-time optimizations.

Result: All structural diversity interventions fail to beat simple high-temperature sampling; high temperature already decorrelates errors enough, and alternative prompts decrease per-sample accuracy more than they reduce correlation; model capability differences (17-point gap) have about an order of magnitude more impact than any inference-time strategy.

Conclusion: Careful engineering of diverse prompting strategies for ensembles does not significantly enhance mathematical reasoning over basic high-temperature sampling; instead, raw model capability overwhelmingly determines performance, suggesting limited returns to inference-time prompt diversity for this task.

Abstract: Majority voting over multiple LLM attempts improves mathematical reasoning, but correlated errors limit the effective sample size. A natural fix: assign structurally different reasoning strategies to different voters to decorrelate errors. We test this Diverse Prompt Mixer in the AIMO~3 competition: 3 models, 23+ experiments, and 50 IMO-level problems on a single H100 80 GB with a 5-hour limit. Every intervention fails. High-temperature sampling already decorrelates errors sufficiently; weaker prompt strategies reduce per-attempt accuracy more than they reduce correlation. Across a 17-point model capability gap and every inference-time optimization we tried, model capability dominates by an order of magnitude.

### [46] [What can LLMs tell us about the mechanisms behind polarity illusions in humans? Experiments across model scales and training steps](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27855)
*Dario Paape*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Study uses Pythia scaling models to test whether two polarity illusions (NPI illusion, depth charge illusion) appear in LLMs and how they scale with size.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Polarity illusions in human sentence processing are often explained via rational inference mechanisms. The author wants to see if similar illusions emerge in LLMs that lack explicit rational inference, to inform theories of human sentence processing and the nature of these illusions.

Method: Evaluate Pythia LLMs of different sizes on linguistic stimuli designed to elicit two polarity illusions: the NPI illusion and the depth charge illusion. Measure how strongly each illusion is manifested across model scales.

Result: As model size increases, the NPI illusion weakens and eventually disappears, whereas the depth charge illusion grows stronger in larger models.

Conclusion: Polarity illusions need not be explained by rational inference over ill-formed sentences, since LLMs show such effects without that capacity. Instead, shallow 'good enough' processing and/or partial grammaticalization of prescriptively ungrammatical forms may underlie these illusions. The author offers a synthesis of accounts using construction grammar as the theoretical framework.

Abstract: I use the Pythia scaling suite (Biderman et al. 2023) to investigate if and how two well-known polarity illusions, the NPI illusion and the depth charge illusion, arise in LLMs. The NPI illusion becomes weaker and ultimately disappears as model size increases, while the depth charge illusion becomes stronger in larger models. The results have implications for human sentence processing: it may not be necessary to assume "rational inference" mechanisms that convert ill-formed sentences into well-formed ones to explain polarity illusions, given that LLMs cannot plausibly engage in this kind of reasoning, especially at the implicit level of next-token prediction. On the other hand, shallow, "good enough" processing and/or partial grammaticalization of prescriptively ungrammatical structures may both occur in LLMs. I propose a synthesis of different theoretical accounts that is rooted in the basic tenets of construction grammar.

### [47] [KazByte: Adapting Qwen models to Kazakh via Byte-level Adapter](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27859)
*Rauan Akylzhanov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose ByteKaz, a method to let a frozen Qwen2.5-7B model handle Kazakh text directly as raw bytes via a learned adapter, then fine-tune attention layers for Kazakh, aiming to match or exceed original performance while avoiding tokenizer inefficiencies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Kazakh suffers from inefficient tokenization in large language models built for high-resource languages: it gets many more tokens than equivalent English, increasing compute, reducing effective context window, and weakening morphological modeling. The authors want to remove this tokenizer disadvantage and improve Kazakh performance without retraining a whole model.

Method: 1) Introduce a small adapter that maps raw byte sequences of Kazakh text into the internal embedding space of a frozen Qwen2.5-7B model, effectively learning the model’s “internal language.” 2) Train this byte-level adapter while keeping Qwen frozen. 3) Freeze the trained adapter and then fine-tune only the attention layers of Qwen on Kazakh text. The approach completely bypasses the original tokenizer. The report focuses on architecture and training protocol design; experiments are still underway.

Result: There are no empirical results yet; the paper is a design and hypothesis report. The main outcome is the specification of the ByteKaz architecture, the two-stage training pipeline, and the claim that this approach should match or exceed Qwen2.5-7B’s original accuracy on Kazakh benchmarks while using bytes instead of subword tokens.

Conclusion: ByteKaz is proposed as a tokenizer-free, byte-level interface plus partial fine-tuning strategy for adapting Qwen2.5-7B to Kazakh. By learning an adapter that speaks the model’s internal representation language and then tuning only attention layers, they aim to overcome the tokenizer tax on low-resource, morphologically rich languages like Kazakh. Empirical validation is still in progress, so conclusions are currently theoretical and hypothesis-driven.

Abstract: Large language models fragment Kazakh text into many more tokens than equivalent English text, because their tokenizers were built for high-resource languages. This tokenizer tax inflates compute, shortens the effective context window, and weakens the model's grip on Kazakh morphology. We propose to bypass the tokenizer entirely by feeding raw bytes through a small adapter that learns to speak the internal language of a frozen Qwen2.5-7B. Once the adapter is trained, we freeze it and fine-tune only the attention layers of Qwen on Kazakh text. Our central hypothesis is that this two-stage process -- first teach the interface, then adapt the model -- should match or exceed the accuracy of the original Qwen2.5-7B on standard Kazakh benchmarks. This report describes the ByteKaz architecture and training protocol. Empirical validation is ongoing; this version stakes the design and hypotheses for the record.

In this work, we analyze the effects of layer pruning in the Whisper encoder when used as the acoustic backbone of SLAM-ASR. We further examine the extent to which LoRA-based fine-tuning can recover performance degradation caused by pruning. Experiments conducted across three Whisper variants (Small, Medium, Large-v2), three languages representing distinct resource levels (Danish, Dutch, English), and over 200 training runs demonstrate that pruning two encoder layers causes only 2-4% WER degradation, and that combining this pruning with LoRA adaptation consistently outperforms the unpruned baseline while reducing total parameters by 7-14%. Moreover, our error analysis reveals that LoRA primarily compensates through the language model's linguistic priors, reducing total word errors by 11-21% for Dutch and English, with substitutions and deletions showing the largest reductions. However, for low-resource Danish, the reduction is smaller (4-7%), and LoRA introduces increased insertion errors, indicating that compensation effectiveness depends on the LLM's pre-existing language proficiency and available training data.

### [53] [Rethinking Atomic Decomposition for LLM Judges: A Prompt-Controlled Study of Reference-Grounded QA Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28005)
*Xinran Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper compares atomic (decompose-and-verify) vs holistic LLM judges for reference-grounded answer evaluation and finds that holistic judging performs as well or better on most benchmarks, especially for detecting partial support.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Atomic decomposition is commonly used for LLM-based evaluation, but atomic prompts are also longer and richer, so it is unclear whether performance gains come from decomposition itself or from prompt richness. The paper aims to disentangle these factors and rigorously test whether atomic decomposition actually improves reference-grounded judgment quality.

Method: They set up a benchmark-style reference-support classification task where a judge must label candidate answers as fully supported, partially supported, or unsupported by a given reference. They design two judge types with matched inputs and rubric detail: (1) a self-decomposing atomic judge using a single prompt that asks the model to first decompose into claims and then verify; (2) a holistic judge that reasons about the answer as a whole without explicit decomposition. Experiments span three QA benchmarks (TruthfulQA, ASQA, QAMPARI), four LLM families, 200 source examples per dataset, and three pre-frozen prompt variants per design. They use paired source-level comparisons, cluster bootstrap, and aggregated statistics, and also perform sensitivity checks including comparison to human annotations and perturbations such as degrading reference quality.

Result: Across ASQA and QAMPARI, the holistic judge consistently matches or outperforms the atomic judge across all four model families, with statistically reliable gains in three of the four. On TruthfulQA, the atomic judge has a small advantage. The holistic gains are concentrated on partially supported cases, indicating better detection of incompleteness. Sensitivity analysis against human annotations shows that the relative ranking of judge types is stable whether using benchmark-style completeness labels or human factual correctness labels. Degrading reference quality causes the largest performance drop for both judge types.

Conclusion: For the studied setting—single-prompt self-decomposing atomic judges on three QA-style benchmarks—explicit atomic decomposition does not reliably outperform a prompt-controlled holistic approach; in fact, holistic judging is often better at detecting partial support. Thus, some presumed benefits of atomic decomposition may stem more from prompt richness than from decomposition itself. However, the conclusion is limited to single-prompt self-decomposing designs and QA-style tasks; multi-stage atomic pipelines and other task types remain open for investigation, and both judge families are highly sensitive to reference quality.

Abstract: Atomic decomposition -- breaking a candidate answer into claims before verifying each against a reference -- is a widely adopted design for LLM-based reference-grounded judges. However, atomic prompts are typically richer and longer, making it unclear whether any advantage comes from decomposition or from richer prompting. We study this for benchmark-style completeness-sensitive reference-support classification: classifying a candidate as fully supported, partially supported, or unsupported relative to a supplied reference. We compare a self-decomposing atomic judge (single-prompt decompose-and-verify) against a prompt-controlled holistic judge with the same inputs and a similarly detailed rubric. On 200 source examples per dataset across TruthfulQA, ASQA, and QAMPARI, with four model families, source-level paired tests, cluster bootstrap, and aggregation across three pre-frozen prompt variants per design family, we find the holistic judge matches or exceeds the atomic judge on two of three benchmarks: ASQA and QAMPARI favor holistic across all four families (statistically reliable in three of four), while TruthfulQA shows a small atomic edge. The holistic advantage is concentrated in partially\_supported cases -- incompleteness detection. A sensitivity check against human annotations confirms the ranking under both benchmark-completeness and human factual-correctness standards. Our finding is specific to the self-decomposing single-prompt pattern on three QA-style benchmarks with 200 source examples each; multi-stage atomic pipelines and non-QA tasks remain untested. Among perturbations examined, reference-quality degradation produced the largest accuracy drops for both judge families.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: App store reviews offer rich user feedback but are messy and hard to manually convert into clean, backlog-ready user stories. Existing automated methods underperform and are hard to replicate, motivating an investigation into whether modern LLMs can better transform raw reviews into usable agile requirements.

Method: The authors use the Mini-BAR dataset of 1,000+ health app reviews and prompt several LLMs (GPT-3.5 Turbo, Gemini 2.0 Flash, Mistral 7B Instruct) in zero-shot, one-shot, and two-shot settings to generate user stories. They then evaluate these stories with human raters using the RUST framework and with an automated RoBERTa classifier fine-tuned on UStAI to assess story quality.

Result: LLMs can generate fluent, well-structured user stories and, with few-shot prompting, can match or surpass humans on aspects of linguistic quality and formatting, as measured by both human judgment and automated metrics. However, they perform worse on producing independent and unique user stories, leading to redundancy and weaker backlogs.

Conclusion: LLMs are effective tools for transforming unstructured app reviews into actionable user stories, particularly in terms of fluency and format, but they are less capable of ensuring independence and uniqueness of requirements. They can reliably assist developers in requirements elicitation from reviews, yet still need complementary methods or safeguards to build high-quality agile backlogs.

Abstract: App store reviews provide a constant flow of real user feedback that can help improve software requirements. However, these reviews are often messy, informal, and difficult to analyze manually at scale. Although automated techniques exist, many do not perform well when replicated and often fail to produce clean, backlog-ready user stories for agile projects.
  In this study, we evaluate how well large language models (LLMs) such as GPT-3.5 Turbo, Gemini 2.0 Flash, and Mistral 7B Instruct can generate usable user stories directly from raw app reviews. Using the Mini-BAR dataset of 1,000+ health app reviews, we tested zero-shot, one-shot, and two-shot prompting methods.
  We evaluated the generated user stories using both human judgment (via the RUST framework) and a RoBERTa classifier fine-tuned on UStAI to assess their overall quality. Our results show that LLMs can match or even outperform humans in writing fluent, well-formatted user stories, especially when few-shot prompts are used. However, they still struggle to produce independent and unique user stories, which are essential for building a strong agile backlog.
  Overall, our findings show how LLMs can reliably turn unstructured app reviews into actionable software requirements, providing developers with clear guidance to turn user feedback into meaningful improvements.

### [58] [Beyond Cosine Similarity: Zero-Initialized Residual Complex Projection for Aspect-Based Sentiment Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28205)
*Yijin Wang,Fandi Sun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a new complex-valued projection and loss function to better disentangle aspect semantics from sentiment polarity in ABSA, achieving state-of-the-art performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In Aspect-Based Sentiment Analysis, existing real-valued embeddings tend to mix aspect meaning with sentiment polarity, making it hard to clearly separate what is being talked about from how it is being evaluated. Additionally, typical contrastive learning losses mistakenly treat semantically similar samples with the same polarity as negatives ('false-negative collisions'), which particularly harms performance on frequent aspects.

Method: They introduce a Zero-Initialized Residual Complex Projection (ZRCP) that maps textual features into a complex vector space where the phase primarily represents sentiment polarity and the amplitude encodes semantic strength and lexical richness. They also design an Anti-collision Masked Angle Loss, which is a contrastive-style objective defined over angles (phases) in the complex space and uses a mask to avoid treating same-polarity, same-aspect examples as negatives. They do not constrain amplitudes, focusing optimization on phase separation between polarities.

Result: The method increases the discriminative margin between opposite sentiment polarities by more than 50% while preserving cohesion among same-polarity instances of an aspect, and it reaches a state-of-the-art Macro-F1 score of 0.8851 on ABSA benchmarks.

Conclusion: Encoding ABSA representations in a complex space with phase-driven sentiment encoding and unconstrained amplitude, combined with an anti-collision masked angular loss, effectively disentangles sentiment from aspect semantics. Over-regularizing amplitudes harms subjective representation, and their design choice is key to achieving robust, fine-grained sentiment separation and SOTA performance.

Abstract: Aspect-Based Sentiment Analysis (ABSA) is fundamentally challenged by representation entanglement, where aspect semantics and sentiment polarities are often conflated in real-valued embedding spaces. Furthermore, standard contrastive learning suffers from false-negative collisions, severely degrading performance on high-frequency aspects. In this paper, we propose a novel framework featuring a Zero-Initialized Residual Complex Projection (ZRCP) and an Anti-collision Masked Angle Loss,inspired by quantum projection and entanglement ideas. Our approach projects textual features into a complex semantic space, systematically utilizing the phase to disentangle sentiment polarities while allowing the amplitude to encode the semantic intensity and lexical richness of subjective descriptions. To tackle the collision bottleneck, we introduce an anti-collision mask that elegantly preserves intra-polarity aspect cohesion while expanding the inter-polarity discriminative margin by over 50%. Experimental results demonstrate that our framework achieves a state-of-the-art Macro-F1 score of 0.8851. Deep geometric analyses further reveal that explicitly penalizing the complex amplitude catastrophically over-regularizes subjective representations, proving that our unconstrained-amplitude and phase-driven objective is crucial for robust, fine-grained sentiment disentanglement.

### [60] [Categorical Perception in Large Language Model Hidden States: Structural Warping at Digit-Count Boundaries](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28258)
*Jon-Paul Cacioli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that large language models exhibit categorical perception-like geometric warping in their hidden representations of Arabic numerals at structural boundaries (10, 100), even when they lack explicit semantic category knowledge.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To investigate whether the classic psychological phenomenon of categorical perception also arises in the internal representation geometry of large language models, and whether this depends on semantic knowledge or merely on structural properties of the input.

Method: The authors use representational similarity analysis across six LLMs from five architecture families, comparing how well two models fit hidden-state distances between numeral tokens: (1) a purely continuous log-distance model and (2) a CP-additive model that adds a boundary-boost term at digit-count transitions (10, 100). They test for boundary-specific warping and include a temperature domain control where there is no tokenisation discontinuity.

Result: In all primary layers of every model, the CP-additive model explains representational geometry better than the purely continuous model, but only at structurally defined numeral boundaries (10, 100), not at non-boundary controls or in the temperature domain. Models cluster into two types: some (e.g., Gemma, Qwen) both explicitly categorise and show geometric warping (“classic CP”), while others (e.g., Llama, Mistral, Phi) show boundary warping without being able to report the category distinction (“structural CP”).

Conclusion: Structural discontinuities in input formatting, like digit-count transitions in numerals, are sufficient to induce categorical-perception-like geometry in LLM hidden states, independently of whether the model has explicit semantic knowledge of the categories. Different architectures show distinct relationships between representational geometry and explicit categorisation behavior.

Abstract: Categorical perception (CP) -- enhanced discriminability at category boundaries -- is among the most studied phenomena in perceptual psychology. This paper reports that analogous geometric warping occurs in the hidden-state representations of large language models (LLMs) processing Arabic numerals. Using representational similarity analysis across six models from five architecture families, the study finds that a CP-additive model (log-distance plus a boundary boost) fits the representational geometry better than a purely continuous model at 100% of primary layers in every model tested. The effect is specific to structurally defined boundaries (digit-count transitions at 10 and 100), absent at non-boundary control positions, and absent in the temperature domain where linguistic categories (hot/cold) lack a tokenisation discontinuity. Two qualitatively distinct signatures emerge: "classic CP" (Gemma, Qwen), where models both categorise explicitly and show geometric warping, and "structural CP" (Llama, Mistral, Phi), where geometry warps at the boundary but models cannot report the category distinction. This dissociation is stable across boundaries and is a property of the architecture, not the stimulus. Structural input-format discontinuities are sufficient to produce categorical perception geometry in LLMs, independently of explicit semantic category knowledge.

To address this, we systematically investigate the relationship between temperature and judge performance through a series of controlled experiments, and further adopt a causal inference framework within our empirical statistical analysis to rigorously examine the direct causal effect of temperature on judge behavior, offering actionable engineering insights for the design of LLM-centric evaluation pipelines.

### [64] [Kernel-Smith: A Unified Recipe for Evolutionary Kernel Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28342)
*He Du,Qiming Ge,Jiakai Hu,Aijun Yang,Zheng Cai,Zixian Huang,Sheng Yuan,Qinxiu Cheng,Xinchen Xie,Yicheng Chen,Yining Li,Jiaxing Xie,Huanan Dong,Yaguang Wu,Xiangjun Huang,Jian Yang,Hui Wang,Bowen Zhou,Bowen Li,Qipeng Guo,Kai Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Kernel-Smith is a framework that uses an evolutionary, feedback-driven LLM agent plus post-training to automatically generate and optimize GPU kernels, achieving state-of-the-art performance on standard benchmarks and multiple GPU backends.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Writing and optimizing GPU kernels for different hardware and backends (e.g., Triton on NVIDIA, Maca on MetaX) is labor-intensive and requires deep expertise. Existing LLM-based code generators tend to be one-shot, unstable, and not tightly integrated with actual execution feedback, limiting their performance and reliability. The paper aims to build a stable, high-performance, and hardware-adaptive system that tightly couples LLMs with evaluation loops to generate kernels that are both correct and fast in practice.

Method: Kernel-Smith combines (1) an evaluation-driven evolutionary agent that keeps a population of GPU kernel candidates, uses an archive of top-performing diverse programs, and iteratively improves them via structured execution feedback (compilation success, correctness tests, runtime speedup); and (2) an evolution-oriented post-training pipeline that converts long evolutionary search trajectories into step-level supervised and reinforcement learning signals. The model is thus trained to act as a strong local improver inside the evolutionary loop instead of a pure one-shot generator. The framework includes backend-specific evaluation services for Triton on NVIDIA GPUs and Maca on MetaX GPUs, enabling hardware-aware evaluation and optimization.

Result: Under a unified evolutionary search protocol, the Kernel-Smith-235B-RL model achieves state-of-the-art overall performance on KernelBench with the Nvidia Triton backend, obtaining the best average speedup and surpassing strong proprietary models such as Gemini-3.0-pro and Claude-4.6-opus. On the MetaX MACA backend, the smaller Kernel-Smith-MACA-30B model outperforms larger models like DeepSeek-V3.2-think and Qwen3-235B-2507-think, showing effective cross-platform adaptation. Additionally, the same workflow yields concrete upstream optimizations that are integrated into real-world production systems including SGLang and LMDeploy.

Conclusion: Tightly integrating LLMs with an evaluation-driven evolutionary process and training them as local improvers yields a robust, high-performance framework for GPU kernel and operator generation. Kernel-Smith achieves state-of-the-art benchmark results across multiple backends and scales, and its optimized kernels can be transferred into real production deployments, suggesting that this approach is a practical path to automated, hardware-aware kernel optimization across heterogeneous GPU platforms.

Abstract: We present Kernel-Smith, a framework for high-performance GPU kernel and operator generation that combines a stable evaluation-driven evolutionary agent with an evolution-oriented post-training recipe. On the agent side, Kernel-Smith maintains a population of executable candidates and iteratively improves them using an archive of top-performing and diverse programs together with structured execution feedback on compilation, correctness, and speedup. To make this search reliable, we build backend-specific evaluation services for Triton on NVIDIA GPUs and Maca on MetaX GPUs. On the training side, we convert long-horizon evolution trajectories into step-centric supervision and reinforcement learning signals by retaining correctness-preserving, high-gain revisions, so that the model is optimized as a strong local improver inside the evolutionary loop rather than as a one-shot generator. Under a unified evolutionary protocol, Kernel-Smith-235B-RL achieves state-of-the-art overall performance on KernelBench with Nvidia Triton backend, attaining the best average speedup ratio and outperforming frontier proprietary models including Gemini-3.0-pro and Claude-4.6-opus. We further validate the framework on the MetaX MACA backend, where our Kernel-Smith-MACA-30B surpasses large-scale counterparts such as DeepSeek-V3.2-think and Qwen3-235B-2507-think, highlighting potential for seamless adaptation across heterogeneous platforms. Beyond benchmark results, the same workflow produces upstream contributions to production systems including SGLang and LMDeploy, demonstrating that LLM-driven kernel optimization can transfer from controlled evaluation to practical deployment.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This mismatch can obscure whose perspectives are being captured and whether models are truly subjectivity-aware, motivating a rethinking of evaluation criteria.

Method: This is a position paper. They then survey and code the experimental setups of 60 recent papers dealing with subjective NLP tasks to see which desiderata are addressed and where gaps lie.

Result: The analysis of 60 papers reveals systematic gaps: many works do not distinguish between ambiguous versus genuinely polyphonic inputs; they often fail to clearly represent or communicate subjectivity in outputs; some desiderata are considered in isolation with little exploration of trade-offs and interactions between them; and several dimensions of subjectivity remain rarely evaluated in practice.

Conclusion: Current evaluation practices for subjective NLP tasks are incomplete relative to the goals of subjectivity-sensitive models. The authors argue that their seven desiderata should guide future evaluation design, encourage clearer distinctions between different kinds of subjectivity, and promote user-centric, diversity-aware assessment of models that aim to reflect multiple perspectives.

Abstract: Subjective judgments are part of several NLP datasets and recent work is increasingly prioritizing models whose outputs reflect this diversity of perspectives. Such responses allow us to shed light on minority voices, which are frequently marginalized or obscured by dominant perspectives. It remains a question whether our evaluation practices align with these models' objectives. This position paper proposes seven evaluation desiderata for subjectivity-sensitive models, rooted in how subjectivity is represented in NLP data and models. The desiderata are constructed in a top-down approach, keeping in mind the user-centric impact of such models. We scan the experimental setup of 60 papers and show that various aspects of subjectivity are still understudied: the distinction between ambiguous and polyphonic input, whether subjectivity is effectively expressed to the user, and a lack of interplay between different desiderata, amongst other gaps.

### [66] [Tailoring AI-Driven Reading Scaffolds to the Distinct Needs of Neurodiverse Learners](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28370)
*Soufiane Jhilal,Eleonora Pasqua,Caterina Marchesi,Riccardo Corradi,Martina Galletti*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Study tests how different reading scaffolds (segmentation, pictograms, keywords) affect comprehension and experience for neurodiverse primary-school learners in supervised inclusive settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Neurodiverse learners need reading support, but richer scaffolds can sometimes overload attention and working memory. There is limited empirical evidence on how specific structural and semantic scaffolds impact comprehension and user experience in inclusive, supervised contexts. The authors aim to clarify these effects to guide better, adaptive support design.

Method: Within-subject pilot study with 14 primary-school learners with special educational needs and disabilities. Using an adapted reading interface, the study compared four reading modalities: (1) unmodified text, (2) sentence-segmented text, (3) segmented text with pictograms, and (4) segmented text with pictograms plus keyword labels. Reading comprehension was assessed with standardized questions; child- and therapist-reported experience measures and open-ended feedback were also collected.

Result: Learners showed heterogeneous responses. Some appeared to benefit from segmentation and pictograms (better comprehension patterns), while others seemed to experience increased coordination costs when visual scaffolds were added. Experience ratings showed small differences between modalities; some effects were related to clinical complexity, especially on perceived ease of understanding. Open feedback often requested simpler wording and more visual supports.

Conclusion: No single scaffold worked best for all neurodiverse learners. The study supports the need for calibrated, adjustable scaffolding that can be tailored to individual needs. It offers design implications for human-AI co-regulation in supervised inclusive reading environments, where supports are dynamically adapted rather than uniformly applied.

Abstract: Neurodiverse learners often require reading supports, yet increasing scaffold richness can sometimes overload attention and working memory rather than improve comprehension. Grounded in the Construction-Integration model and a contingent scaffolding perspective, we examine how structural versus semantic scaffolds shape comprehension and reading experience in a supervised inclusive context. Using an adapted reading interface, we compared four modalities: unmodified text, sentence-segmented text, segmented text with pictograms, and segmented text with pictograms plus keyword labels. In a within-subject pilot with 14 primary-school learners with special educational needs and disabilities, we measured reading comprehension using standardized questions and collected brief child- and therapist-reported experience measures alongside open-ended feedback. Results highlight heterogeneous responses as some learners showed patterns consistent with benefits from segmentation and pictograms, while others showed patterns consistent with increased coordination costs when visual scaffolds were introduced. Experience ratings showed limited differences between modalities, with some apparent effects linked to clinical complexity, particularly for perceived ease of understanding. Open-ended feedback of the learners frequently requested simpler wording and additional visual supports. These findings suggest that no single scaffold is universally optimal, reinforcing the need for calibrated, adjustable scaffolding and provide design implications for human-AI co-regulation in supervised inclusive reading contexts.

### [69] [Structural-Ambiguity-Aware Translation from Natural Language to Signal Temporal Logic](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28426)
*Kosei Fushimi,Kazunobu Serizawa,Junya Ikemoto,Kazumune Hashimoto*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes an ambiguity-preserving pipeline to translate natural language task descriptions into multiple candidate Signal Temporal Logic (STL) formulas with plausibility scores, instead of a single possibly incorrect formula.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Directly writing STL formulas is hard for non-experts, while natural language is easy but structurally ambiguous. Existing NL-to-logic systems typically force a single parse and single logical form, which can be unreliable when instructions are ambiguous, especially in safety-critical cyber-physical systems. The authors want a method that reflects ambiguity instead of hiding it.

Method: They design a three-stage pipeline based on Combinatory Categorial Grammar (CCG). (1) Perform ambiguity-preserving n-best syntactic parsing of the natural language instruction to keep multiple plausible parses. (2) For each parse, carry out STL-oriented, template-based semantic composition to obtain candidate STL formulas. (3) Canonicalize and deduplicate logically equivalent STL formulas while aggregating scores from different derivations, yielding a set of unique candidate STL formulas with plausibility scores.

Result: The method outputs a deduplicated set of STL candidate formulas each with a plausibility score, explicitly capturing multiple interpretations when the NL instruction is ambiguous, and collapsing unambiguous or canonically equivalent readings to a single STL formula. Case studies on representative task descriptions show that genuinely ambiguous inputs yield multiple distinct STL candidates, whereas unambiguous or equivalent derivations are merged into one formula.

Conclusion: Ambiguity-preserving NL-to-STL translation is feasible and useful. By retaining multiple parses and mapping them to STL with canonicalization and scoring, the system better reflects the true ambiguity of natural language instructions than one-best methods, making it more suitable for specifying timed, safety-critical tasks in cyber-physical systems.

Abstract: Signal Temporal Logic (STL) is widely used to specify timed and safety-critical tasks for cyber-physical systems, but writing STL formulas directly is difficult for non-expert users. Natural language (NL) provides a convenient interface, yet its inherent structural ambiguity makes one-to-one translation into STL unreliable. In this paper, we propose an \textit{ambiguity-preserving} method for translating NL task descriptions into STL candidate formulas. The key idea is to retain multiple plausible syntactic analyses instead of forcing a single interpretation at the parsing stage. To this end, we develop a three-stage pipeline based on Combinatory Categorial Grammar (CCG): ambiguity-preserving $n$-best parsing, STL-oriented template-based semantic composition, and canonicalization with score aggregation. The proposed method outputs a deduplicated set of STL candidates with plausibility scores, thereby explicitly representing multiple possible formal interpretations of an ambiguous instruction. In contrast to existing one-best NL-to-logic translation methods, the proposed approach is designed to preserve attachment and scope ambiguity. Case studies on representative task descriptions demonstrate that the method generates multiple STL candidates for genuinely ambiguous inputs while collapsing unambiguous or canonically equivalent derivations to a single STL formula.

Using log-probability-based \emph{moral justifiability} scores, we correlate each model's outputs with survey data covering a broad set of ethical topics. Our results show that many earlier or smaller models often produce near-zero or negative correlations with human judgments. In contrast, advanced instruction-tuned models achieve substantially higher positive correlations, suggesting they better reflect real-world moral attitudes. We provide a detailed regional analysis revealing that models align better with Western, Educated, Industrialized, Rich, and Democratic (W.E.I.R.D.) nations than with other regions. Method: The authors design PROClaim, a courtroom-inspired multi-agent system with specialized roles such as Plaintiff, Defense, and Judge. PROClaim shows that introducing courtroom-style procedure, adversarial evidence negotiation, and multiple diverse judges mitigates systematic biases and hallucinations, offering a more dependable foundation for high-stakes verification tasks. The release of code and data facilitates further research in this direction.

While retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) and multi-agent debate (MAD) address this, they are limited by one-pass retrieval and unstructured debate dynamics. We propose a courtroom-style multi-agent framework, PROClaim, that reformulates verification as a structured, adversarial deliberation. Our approach integrates specialized roles (e.g., Plaintiff, Defense, Judge) with Progressive RAG (P-RAG) to dynamically expand and refine the evidence pool during the debate. Furthermore, we employ evidence negotiation, self-reflection, and heterogeneous multi-judge aggregation to enforce calibration, robustness, and diversity. In zero-shot evaluations on the Check-COVID benchmark, PROClaim achieves 81.7% accuracy, outperforming standard multi-agent debate by 10.0 percentage points, with P-RAG driving the primary performance gains (+7.5 pp). We ultimately demonstrate that structural deliberation and model heterogeneity effectively mitigate systematic biases, providing a robust foundation for reliable claim verification. Our code and data are publicly available at https://github.com/mnc13/PROClaim.

### [74] [GraphWalker: Agentic Knowledge Graph Question Answering via Synthetic Trajectory Curriculum](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28533)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Agentic KGQA requires iterative interaction with KGs but suffers from scarce training data and poor generalization, partly because existing methods restrict exploration and rely on predefined reasoning trajectories. The paper aims to enable broader, more robust exploration and reasoning in KGQA agents.

Method: GraphWalker introduces Automated Trajectory Synthesis and Stage-wise Fine-tuning. In stage one, the agent is SFT-trained on structurally diverse trajectories created from constrained random-walk paths over the KG to learn broad exploration priors. In stage two, the agent is further fine-tuned on a small set of expert-curated trajectories to learn reflection and error recovery. A lightweight RL stage is then applied on top to refine behavior.

Result: Experiments on KGQA benchmarks CWQ and WebQSP show that GraphWalker reaches state-of-the-art performance. Additional experiments on GrailQA and a newly proposed GraphWalkerBench show improved generalization to out-of-distribution reasoning paths compared with prior methods.

Abstract: Agentic knowledge graph question answering (KGQA) requires an agent to iteratively interact with knowledge graphs (KGs), posing challenges in both training data scarcity and reasoning generalization. Specifically, existing approaches often restrict agent exploration: prompting-based methods lack autonomous navigation training, while current training pipelines usually confine reasoning to predefined trajectories. To this end, this paper proposes \textit{GraphWalker}, a novel agentic KGQA framework that addresses these challenges through \textit{Automated Trajectory Synthesis} and \textit{Stage-wise Fine-tuning}. GraphWalker adopts a two-stage SFT training paradigm: First, the agent is trained on structurally diverse trajectories synthesized from constrained random-walk paths, establishing a broad exploration prior over the KG; Second, the agent is further fine-tuned on a small set of expert trajectories to develop reflection and error recovery capabilities. Extensive experiments demonstrate that our stage-wise SFT paradigm unlocks a higher performance ceiling for a lightweight reinforcement learning (RL) stage, enabling GraphWalker to achieve state-of-the-art performance on CWQ and WebQSP. Additional results on GrailQA and our constructed GraphWalkerBench confirm that GraphWalker enhances generalization to out-of-distribution reasoning paths. The code is publicly available at https://github.com/XuShuwenn/GraphWalker

### [75] [Compressing Transformer Language Models via Matrix Product Operator Decomposition: A Case Study on PicoGPT](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28534)
*Younes Javanmard,Tanmoy Pandit,Masoud Mardani*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper compresses GPT-style transformers by replacing dense linear layers with Matrix Product Operator (MPO) factorizations, achieving large parameter reductions with minimal accuracy loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Transformer language models scale quadratically in parameters with hidden size, making them costly for deployment on resource-constrained devices. Existing compression methods like low-rank factorization and unstructured pruning can lack theoretical grounding or structured controllability over approximation quality. The authors aim to explore MPO decomposition as a principled, tensor-network-based way to compress transformers while preserving performance.

Method: They use Matrix Product Operator (MPO) decomposition to factorize all nn.Linear weight matrices in PicoGPT, a small GPT-2-style character-level model (~1M parameters). Each weight matrix is represented as a chain of low-rank MPO cores with controllable bond dimension chi. They design balanced factorization schemes for the five distinct weight-matrix shapes in the model. MPO cores are either initialised via TT-SVD from pretrained dense weights or randomly, and then trained end-to-end with standard PyTorch autograd (no custom backward). They evaluate several bond dimensions (chi in {4, 8, 16, 32}) and two-/three-site factorisation strategies on the Tiny Shakespeare dataset, comparing reconstruction error, compression ratio, and token-level accuracy to the dense baseline.

Result: MPO parameterization yields up to 13x compression per transformer block at chi = 4. With chi = 16, the model shrinks from 1,020,224 to 191,872 parameters (≈5.3x reduction) while retaining 97.7% of the dense model’s token accuracy (51.6% vs 52.8%). Reconstruction error decreases as chi increases, and three-site factorisations have lower reconstruction error than two-site ones at the same chi. The chi = 8 model achieves the best accuracy-per-parameter, beating the dense baseline by 2.7x on this metric.

Conclusion: MPO decomposition is an effective, theoretically grounded method for compressing transformer models. By replacing dense linear layers with MPO-based ones, significant parameter savings are achieved with only minor accuracy loss, and in some regimes improved accuracy-per-parameter over the dense baseline. This positions MPO parameterization as a practical alternative to standard low-rank approaches and unstructured pruning for transformer compression, particularly for resource-constrained deployment.

Abstract: Transformer-based language models achieve strong performance across NLP tasks, but their quadratic parameter scaling with hidden dimension makes deployment on resource-constrained hardware expensive. We study Matrix Product Operator (MPO) decomposition as a principled compression method for transformers. MPO factorises weight matrices into chains of low-rank cores, with approximation quality controlled by the bond dimension chi. We replace every nn.Linear layer in PicoGPT, a GPT-2-style character-level language model with about 1M parameters, with an MPOLinear module parameterised as an MPO chain. Cores are initialised either by TT-SVD from pretrained dense weights or from random initialisation, and trained using standard PyTorch autograd without a custom backward pass. We derive balanced factorisation schemes for the five distinct weight shapes in PicoGPT and evaluate bond dimensions chi in {4, 8, 16, 32} on Tiny Shakespeare. MPO compression achieves up to 13x compression per transformer block at chi = 4. At chi = 16, the model uses 191,872 parameters instead of 1,020,224 while retaining 97.7% of baseline token accuracy (51.6% vs 52.8%). Reconstruction error follows the expected trend and is lower for three-site than two-site factorisations at the same bond dimension. The chi = 8 model gives the best accuracy per parameter, exceeding the dense baseline by 2.7x on this metric. These results show that MPO parameterisation is a practical and theoretically grounded alternative to low-rank methods and unstructured pruning for transformer compression.

</details>

### [78] [Adaptive Block-Scaled Data Types](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28765)
*Jack Cook,Hyemin S. Lee,Kathryn Le,Junxian Guo,Giovanni Traverso,Anantha P. Chandrakasan,Song Han*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes IF4, an adaptive 4-bit quantization data type that switches between FP4 and INT4 per 16-value block, improving language model accuracy over existing NVFP4-style formats and remaining hardware friendly.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: NVFP4 is a popular 4-bit format for quantizing large language models because of good hardware support and information retention, but it has a problematic error distribution, especially on near-maximal values within each 16-value block, which degrades quantization quality. The authors are motivated to design a format that keeps the hardware advantages of block-scaled 4-bit types while reducing these quantization errors.

Method: They introduce Adaptive Block-Scaled Data Types that choose the most appropriate low-precision representation per block of values. Specifically, IF4 (Int/Float 4) operates on groups of 16 values: for each group, it selects either an FP4 or INT4 representation, then applies an E4M3 scale factor similar to NVFP4. The choice of representation is encoded using the previously unused sign bit of the scale factor. The same principle is extended to other bit-widths (IF3, IF6). They also design an IF4-compatible MAC unit to show that the scheme is practical for hardware accelerators.

Result: Across both quantized training and post-training quantization of language models, IF4 consistently yields lower training loss and higher downstream accuracy on many tasks compared to existing 4-bit block-scaled formats such as NVFP4. The hardware study shows that an IF4 MAC unit can be implemented efficiently, indicating that the approach is suitable for real-world accelerators.

Conclusion: Adaptive Block-Scaled Data Types, exemplified by IF4, effectively address the error distribution issues of NVFP4 while preserving block scaling and hardware efficiency. By adaptively choosing between FP4 and INT4 per block and reusing existing bits for signaling, the method improves quantization quality for large language models and is feasible to deploy in next-generation hardware designs.

Abstract: NVFP4 has grown increasingly popular as a 4-bit format for quantizing large language models due to its hardware support and its ability to retain useful information with relatively few bits per parameter. However, the format is not without limitations: recent work has shown that NVFP4 suffers from its error distribution, resulting in large amounts of quantization error on near-maximal values in each group of 16 values. In this work, we leverage this insight to design new Adaptive Block-Scaled Data Types that can adapt to the distribution of their input values. For four-bit quantization, our proposed IF4 (Int/Float 4) data type selects between FP4 and INT4 representations for each group of 16 values, which are then scaled by an E4M3 scale factor as is done with NVFP4. The selected data type is denoted using the scale factor's sign bit, which is currently unused in NVFP4, and we apply the same insight to design formats for other bit-widths, including IF3 and IF6. When used to quantize language models, we find that IF4 outperforms existing 4-bit block-scaled formats, achieving lower loss during quantized training and achieving higher accuracy on many tasks in post-training quantization. We additionally design and evaluate an IF4 Multiply-Accumulate (MAC) unit to demonstrate that IF4 can be implemented efficiently in next-generation hardware accelerators. Our code is available at https://github.com/mit-han-lab/fouroversix.

</details>
