### [79] [Bitboard version of Tetris AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.26765)
*Xingguo Chen,Pingshou Xiong,Zhenyu Luo,Mengfei Hu,Xinwen Li,Yongzhou Lü,Guang Yang,Chao Li,Shangdong Yang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A paper proposes a high-speed Tetris reinforcement learning framework using bitboard game representation, an afterstate actor network, and a buffer-optimized PPO algorithm, achieving much faster simulation and strong play, making Tetris a better RL benchmark.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RL Tetris implementations are too slow, have inefficient state evaluation, and use suboptimal training setups, which makes them impractical for large-scale reinforcement learning experiments. The authors want to provide a Tetris environment and learning setup that is both computationally efficient and algorithmically strong as a benchmark for sequential decision-making tasks.

Method: 1) Redesign the Tetris board and pieces using bitboard representations to use fast bitwise operations for collision detection, line clearing, and feature extraction, yielding major speedups over OpenAI Gym-Tetris. 2) Design an afterstate-evaluating actor network that takes advantage of Tetris afterstate properties to simplify value estimation versus standard action-value networks, using fewer parameters. 3) Propose a buffer-optimized PPO variant that improves the tradeoff between sampling and gradient updates. 4) Implement a Python-Java interface compatible with OpenAI Gym to integrate with existing RL frameworks.

Result: The bitboard optimization achieves a 53x speedup over OpenAI Gym-Tetris. The afterstate actor network outperforms traditional action-value networks while using fewer parameters. The buffer-optimized PPO can reach an average score of 3,829 on 10x10 Tetris within 3 minutes of training. The overall framework provides a fast and effective environment and learning setup for RL research on Tetris.

Conclusion: By combining a highly optimized bitboard-based Tetris engine with improved RL algorithms (afterstate actor network and buffer-optimized PPO) and a Gym-compatible interface, the framework significantly increases simulation speed and sample efficiency. This makes Tetris a more practical and powerful benchmark for scalable RL research in complex sequential decision-making tasks.

Abstract: The efficiency of game engines and policy optimization algorithms is crucial for training reinforcement learning (RL) agents in complex sequential decision-making tasks, such as Tetris. Existing Tetris implementations suffer from low simulation speeds, suboptimal state evaluation, and inefficient training paradigms, limiting their utility for large-scale RL research. To address these limitations, this paper proposes a high-performance Tetris AI framework based on bitboard optimization and improved RL algorithms. First, we redesign the Tetris game board and tetrominoes using bitboard representations, leveraging bitwise operations to accelerate core processes (e.g., collision detection, line clearing, and Dellacherie-Thiery Features extraction) and achieve a 53-fold speedup compared to OpenAI Gym-Tetris. Second, we introduce an afterstate-evaluating actor network that simplifies state value estimation by leveraging Tetris afterstate property, outperforming traditional action-value networks with fewer parameters. Third, we propose a buffer-optimized Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) algorithm that balances sampling and update efficiency, achieving an average score of 3,829 on 10x10 grids within 3 minutes. Additionally, we develop a Python-Java interface compliant with the OpenAI Gym standard, enabling seamless integration with modern RL frameworks. Experimental results demonstrate that our framework enhances Tetris's utility as an RL benchmark by bridging low-level bitboard optimizations with high-level AI strategies, providing a sample-efficient and computationally lightweight solution for scalable sequential decision-making research.

The model learns a shared latent space aligning textual instructions and level structures, while a threshold-based multi-positive contrastive supervision links semantically related levels across games. This representation allows language to guide which structural characteristics should be preserved when combining content from different games, enabling controllable blending through latent interpolation and zero-shot generation from compositional textual prompts. Experiments show that the learned representation supports controllable cross-game level blending and significantly improves blending quality within the same game genre, while providing a unified representation for language-conditioned multi-game content generation.

Recognition of trends towards calibration-focused, distribution-free methods and an emphasis on explainer variability.

Abstract: This paper surveys uncertainty-aware explainable artificial intelligence (UAXAI), examining how uncertainty is incorporated into explanatory pipelines and how such methods are evaluated. Across the literature, three recurring approaches to uncertainty quantification emerge (Bayesian, Monte Carlo, and Conformal methods), alongside distinct strategies for integrating uncertainty into explanations: assessing trustworthiness, constraining models or explanations, and explicitly communicating uncertainty. Evaluation practices remain fragmented and largely model centered, with limited attention to users and inconsistent reporting of reliability properties (e.g., calibration, coverage, explanation stability). Recent work leans towards calibration, distribution free techniques and recognizes explainer variability as a central concern. We argue that progress in UAXAI requires unified evaluation principles that link uncertainty propagation, robustness, and human decision-making, and highlight counterfactual and calibration approaches as promising avenues for aligning interpretability with reliability.

### [82] [Neuro-Symbolic Learning for Predictive Process Monitoring via Two-Stage Logic Tensor Networks with Rule Pruning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.26944)
*Fabrizio De Santis,Gyunam Park,Francesco Zanichelli*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Neuro-symbolic method that injects temporal and logical domain knowledge into sequence prediction models using Logic Tensor Networks with a two-stage optimization to balance rule satisfaction and accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Sequential prediction in domains like fraud detection and healthcare must obey domain-specific rules and regulations. Purely data-driven models ignore formal control-flow and compliance constraints, which can cause lower accuracy and regulatory violations, especially when compliant examples are rare. The paper aims to systematically inject formal domain knowledge into learning to improve accuracy and constraint adherence.

Method: Use Logic Tensor Networks to encode domain knowledge as differentiable logical constraints, specified in Linear Temporal Logic and first-order logic over event sequences and payloads. Propose a two-stage optimization: (1) pretrain with weighted axiom loss where data-driven loss is prioritized so the model first fits observed data while softly considering rules; (2) analyze rule satisfaction dynamics during training and prune axioms that are inconsistent with data or harmful, retaining only those that are both satisfiable and helpful for prediction. The resulting subset of rules is then enforced during fine-tuning.

Result: On four real-world event log datasets, injecting domain knowledge improves predictive performance versus baselines. However, naively enforcing all logical rules via LTNs can greatly hurt accuracy; the proposed two-stage optimization with weighted pretraining and rule pruning avoids this degradation and yields consistent gains. The benefits are largest in settings with strict compliance constraints and few compliant training samples, where the neuro-symbolic model outperforms purely data-driven approaches and maintains rule adherence.

Conclusion: Integrating formal temporal and control-flow domain knowledge into neural sequence models via LTNs is effective when combined with a careful optimization strategy. The two-stage procedure that first prioritizes data fitting and then prunes unhelpful rules enables domain knowledge to improve both accuracy and compliance rather than harm them, particularly in regulation-heavy domains with scarce compliant data. The framework demonstrates that neuro-symbolic learning can practically enhance event sequence prediction in real-world applications.

Abstract: Predictive modeling on sequential event data is critical for fraud detection and healthcare monitoring. Existing data-driven approaches learn correlations from historical data but fail to incorporate domain-specific sequential constraints and logical rules governing event relationships, limiting accuracy and regulatory compliance. For example, healthcare procedures must follow specific sequences, and financial transactions must adhere to compliance rules. We present a neuro-symbolic approach integrating domain knowledge as differentiable logical constraints using Logic Networks (LTNs). We formalize control-flow, temporal, and payload knowledge using Linear Temporal Logic and first-order logic. Our key contribution is a two-stage optimization strategy addressing LTNs' tendency to satisfy logical formulas at the expense of predictive accuracy. The approach uses weighted axiom loss during pretraining to prioritize data learning, followed by rule pruning that retains only consistent, contributive axioms based on satisfaction dynamics. Evaluation on four real-world event logs shows that domain knowledge injection significantly improves predictive performance, with the two-stage optimization proving essential knowledge (without it, knowledge can severely degrade performance). The approach excels particularly in compliance-constrained scenarios with limited compliant training examples, achieving superior performance compared to purely data-driven baselines while ensuring adherence to domain constraints.

This requirement, entering into force in August 2026, collides with fundamental constraints of current generative AI systems. Using synthetic data generation and automated fact-checking as diagnostic use cases, we show that compliance cannot be reduced to post-hoc labeling. In fact-checking pipelines, provenance tracking is not feasible under iterative editorial workflows and non-deterministic LLM outputs; moreover, the assistive-function exemption does not apply, as such systems actively assign truth values rather than supporting editorial presentation. In synthetic data generation, persistent dual-mode marking is paradoxical: watermarks surviving human inspection risk being learned as spurious features during training, while marks suited for machine verification are fragile under standard data processing. Across both domains, three structural gaps obstruct compliance: (a) absent cross-platform marking formats for interleaved human-AI outputs; (b) misalignment between the regulation's 'reliability' criterion and probabilistic model behavior; and (c) missing guidance for adapting disclosures to heterogeneous user expertise. Closing these gaps requires transparency to be treated as an architectural design requirement, demanding interdisciplinary research across legal semantics, AI engineering, and human-centered desi

They source problems from qualifying exams and standard advanced textbooks across fields like analysis, algebra, probability, and logic. Models are evaluated in an "agentic" setup that allows them to interact with tools and produce Lean 4 proofs, which are checked by the Lean 4 proof assistant for correctness. They then compare a range of state-of-the-art models on this benchmark.

Result: The best-performing foundation model on FormalProofBench achieves 33.5% accuracy, meaning it can produce a formally verified Lean 4 proof for roughly one-third of the tasks. Accuracy drops off sharply for other models. The authors also measure and report tool-usage patterns, common failure modes, computational cost, and latency characteristics for the evaluated models.

Conclusion: FormalProofBench reveals that even frontier AI models are currently far from reliably producing graduate-level, formally verified mathematical proofs, with top accuracy only around 33.5%. The benchmark, along with the detailed empirical analysis, provides a standardized, challenging testbed for future work on improving formal theorem proving capabilities in AI and for understanding their limitations and resource tradeoffs.

Abstract: We present FormalProofBench, a private benchmark designed to evaluate whether AI models can produce formally verified mathematical proofs at the graduate level. Each task pairs a natural-language problem with a Lean~4 formal statement, and a model must output a Lean proof accepted by the Lean 4 checker. FormalProofBench targets advanced undergraduate and graduate mathematics, with problems drawn from qualifying exams and standard textbooks across topics including analysis, algebra, probability, and logic. We evaluate a range of frontier models with an agentic harness, and find that the best-performing foundation model achieves 33.5% accuracy, with performance dropping rapidly after that. In addition to the accuracy numbers, we also provide empirical analysis of tool-use, failure modes, cost and latency, thereby providing a thorough evaluation of the formal-theorem proving abilities of frontier models.

### [86] [When Verification Hurts: Asymmetric Effects of Multi-Agent Feedback in Logic Proof Tutoring](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27076)
*Tahreem Yasir,Sutapa Dey Tithi,Benyamin Tabarsi,Dmitri Droujkov,Sam Gilson Yasitha Rajapaksha,Xiaoyi Tian,Arun Ramesh,DongKuan,Xu,Tiffany Barnes*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They build a benchmark and evaluation framework for LLM-based tutoring in propositional logic proofs and show that adding verifier models only helps when initial feedback is weak, and that all setups hit a complexity ceiling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are being used as tutors, but it is unclear how reliable they are in precise symbolic domains like logic proofs, especially at the level of step-by-step feedback rather than final answers. Prior evaluations are coarse (binary correctness, self-assessment) and don't align with a student's current proof state, limiting insight into how to design effective tutoring pipelines.

Method: They create a knowledge-graph-grounded benchmark consisting of 516 distinct proof states in propositional logic, each annotated with step-level feedback labels and difficulty/complexity metrics based on solution paths. They then design and compare three role-specialized LLM pipelines differing in access to solutions: a Tutor model that sees only partial solutions and gives step feedback; a Teacher model with full derivation access; and a Judge model that verifies or corrects the Tutor's feedback. They analyze performance across complexity levels and pipeline configurations.

Result: Verification via the Judge model boosts performance when the Tutor's step-level feedback is relatively inaccurate (below about 70% accuracy), but when Tutor feedback is already strong (over about 85%), adding the Judge actually lowers accuracy by 4–6 percentage points due to over-constraining or over-specifying feedback. All models and pipelines consistently fail on proof states above a complexity threshold (roughly 4–5), indicating a shared performance ceiling. The benchmark supports fine-grained, state-aligned measurement of these effects.

Conclusion: More verification or more context does not automatically make LLM tutors better for symbolic reasoning tasks like logic proofs. Instead, systems should adaptively select architectures based on task difficulty and the reliability of upstream components, routing easier or well-understood problems differently from harder ones. Their benchmark provides a way to study such adaptive, difficulty-aware tutoring designs and to characterize the limits of current LLMs in structured reasoning domains.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly used for automated tutoring, but their reliability in structured symbolic domains remains unclear. We study step-level feedback for propositional logic proofs, which require precise symbolic reasoning aligned with a learner's current proof state. We introduce a knowledge-graph-grounded benchmark of 516 unique proof states with step-level annotations and difficulty metrics. Unlike prior tutoring evaluations that rely on model self-assessment or binary correctness, our framework enables fine-grained analysis of feedback quality against verified solution paths. We evaluate three role-specialized pipelines with varying solution access: Tutor (partial solution access), Teacher (full derivation access), and Judge (verification of Tutor feedback). Our results reveal a striking asymmetry: verification improves outcomes when upstream feedback is error-prone (<70% accuracy), but degrades performance by 4-6 percentage points through over-specification when feedback is already reliable (>85%). Critically, we identify a shared complexity ceiling; no model or pipeline reliably succeeds on proof states exceeding complexity 4-5. These findings challenge the assumption that adding verifiers or richer context universally improves tutoring, motivating adaptive, difficulty-aware architectures that route problems by estimated complexity and upstream reliability.

</details>


### [87] [The Price of Meaning: Why Every Semantic Memory System Forgets](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27116)
*Sambartha Ray Barman,Andrey Starenky,Sofia Bodnar,Nikhil Narasimhan,Ashwin Gopinath*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that any AI memory system organized by semantic similarity inevitably suffers from interference, forgetting, and false recall due to the geometric properties that enable generalization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern AI memory and retrieval systems rely on semantic organization (similarity in a feature space) to enable generalization and flexible retrieval, but practitioners observe problems like interference and forgetting. The paper aims to understand whether these are contingent engineering issues or fundamental tradeoffs inherent to semantic memories.

Method: The authors define and analyze a class of systems called semantically continuous kernel-threshold memories, where retrieval is based on a monotone function of inner products in a semantic feature space of finite local intrinsic dimension. Using this formal framework, they derive theoretical results on representation rank, competitor mass, asymptotic forgetting behavior, and false recall under certain convexity conditions. They then empirically test these predictions across five different memory architectures: vector retrieval, graph memory, attention-based context, BM25-style filesystem retrieval, and parametric neural memory.

Result: They prove four main theorems: (1) useful semantic representations necessarily have finite effective rank; (2) finite local dimension implies nonzero probability of competitor items appearing in any retrieval neighborhood; (3) as the memory grows, retention decays to zero and produces power-law forgetting when arrivals follow power laws; (4) for associative lures satisfying a δ-convexity condition, adjusting thresholds cannot remove false recall. Empirically, they confirm that purely semantic systems display interference as forgetting and false recall, reasoning-augmented systems can partially compensate but produce catastrophic failures, and only systems that abandon semantic generalization avoid interference.

Conclusion: Interference and false recall are not just artifacts of current implementations but are mathematically unavoidable consequences of using semantic similarity–based memory with finite intrinsic dimension. Any architecture that benefits from semantic generalization must pay a corresponding cost in interference; architectures that avoid this cost do so by giving up semantic generalization. Thus, the tradeoff between meaning and interference is fundamental.

Abstract: Every major AI memory system in production today organises information by meaning. That organisation enables generalisation, analogy, and conceptual retrieval -- but it comes at a price. We prove that the same geometric structure enabling semantic generalisation makes interference, forgetting, and false recall inescapable. We formalise this tradeoff for \textit{semantically continuous kernel-threshold memories}: systems whose retrieval score is a monotone function of an inner product in a semantic feature space with finite local intrinsic dimension.
  Within this class we derive four results: (1) semantically useful representations have finite effective rank; (2) finite local dimension implies positive competitor mass in retrieval neighbourhoods; (3) under growing memory, retention decays to zero, yielding power-law forgetting curves under power-law arrival statistics; (4) for associative lures satisfying a $δ$-convexity condition, false recall cannot be eliminated by threshold tuning.
  We test these predictions across five architectures: vector retrieval, graph memory, attention-based context, BM25 filesystem retrieval, and parametric memory. Pure semantic systems express the vulnerability directly as forgetting and false recall. Reasoning-augmented systems partially override these symptoms but convert graceful degradation into catastrophic failure. Systems that escape interference entirely do so by sacrificing semantic generalisation.
  The price of meaning is interference, and no architecture we tested avoids paying it.

</details>

### [89] [daVinci-LLM:Towards the Science of Pretraining](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27164)
*Yiwei Qin,Yixiu Liu,Tiantian Mi,Muhang Xie,Zhen Huang,Weiye Si,Pengrui Lu,Siyuan Feng,Xia Wu,Liming Liu,Ye Luo,Jinlong Hou,Qipeng Guo,Yu Qiao,Pengfei Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents daVinci-LLM, a fully open, industrial-scale pretraining project that systematically studies how data processing depth, domain composition, and evaluation protocols affect the capability ceiling of LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Pretraining largely sets the upper bound of an LLM’s capabilities, but is poorly understood because companies with enough compute cannot be fully open, while academics lack resources for large-scale pretraining experiments. The authors want to fill this gap by running a large, open pretraining effort that can clarify how data processing and curriculum design shape model capabilities.

Method: They build a 3B-parameter model trained from scratch on 8T tokens using a two-stage curriculum that first focuses on foundational skills and then on reasoning-heavy data. They introduce and apply a “Data Darwinism” framework, a structured L0–L9 taxonomy covering data filtering through synthesis. They conduct more than 200 controlled ablation experiments varying processing depth, domain mixtures, and evaluation settings, and they openly release data pipelines, training logs, and experimental results.

Result: They find that deeper data processing consistently improves capabilities and should be treated as a scaling dimension comparable to data volume. Different domains (e.g., code, math, general web text) saturate at different rates and require adaptive strategies such as changing proportions and formats over time. Maintaining a compositional balance of data types allows targeted strengthening of certain skills without triggering broad performance regressions. They also show that choices in evaluation protocol can significantly alter the perceived trajectory of pretraining progress.

Conclusion: Industrial-scale but fully open pretraining is feasible and scientifically valuable. Data processing depth, adaptive domain strategies, and careful evaluation design are core levers for pushing the capability ceiling of LLMs. By releasing the entire experimental and training process, the work aims to seed a cumulative, reproducible science of pretraining that others can extend.

Abstract: The foundational pretraining phase determines a model's capability ceiling, as post-training struggles to overcome capability foundations established during pretraining, yet it remains critically under-explored. This stems from a structural paradox: organizations with computational resources operate under commercial pressures that inhibit transparent disclosure, while academic institutions possess research freedom but lack pretraining-scale computational resources. daVinci-LLM occupies this unexplored intersection, combining industrial-scale resources with full research freedom to advance the science of pretraining. We adopt a fully-open paradigm that treats openness as scientific methodology, releasing complete data processing pipelines, full training processes, and systematic exploration results. Recognizing that the field lacks systematic methodology for data processing, we employ the Data Darwinism framework, a principled L0-L9 taxonomy from filtering to synthesis. We train a 3B-parameter model from random initialization across 8T tokens using a two-stage adaptive curriculum that progressively shifts from foundational capabilities to reasoning-intensive enhancement. Through 200+ controlled ablations, we establish that: processing depth systematically enhances capabilities, establishing it as a critical dimension alongside volume scaling; different domains exhibit distinct saturation dynamics, necessitating adaptive strategies from proportion adjustments to format shifts; compositional balance enables targeted intensification while preventing performance collapse; how evaluation protocol choices shape our understanding of pretraining progress. By releasing the complete exploration process, we enable the community to build upon our findings and systematic methodologies to form accumulative scientific knowledge in pretraining.

The method robustly discovers physically valid microstructures that satisfy multiple coupled objectives.

Conclusion: AutoMS shows that integrating LLMs as active components within a multi-agent, simulation-coupled evolutionary framework can effectively navigate complex, discontinuous physical design spaces. The system bridges high-level semantic design intent and low-level physical rigor, improving both success rate and computational efficiency over traditional optimization and LLM-only baselines. This suggests a promising direction for autonomous agent systems in scientific and engineering design tasks, especially for multi-physics, multi-objective inverse problems.

Abstract: Designing microstructures that satisfy coupled cross-physics objectives is a fundamental challenge in material science. This inverse design problem involves a vast, discontinuous search space where traditional topology optimization is computationally prohibitive, and deep generative models often suffer from "physical hallucinations," lacking the capability to ensure rigorous validity. To address this limitation, we introduce AutoMS, a multi-agent neuro-symbolic framework that reformulates inverse design as an LLM-driven evolutionary search. Unlike methods that treat LLMs merely as interfaces, AutoMS integrates them as "semantic navigators" to initialize search spaces and break local optima, while our novel Simulation-Aware Evolutionary Search (SAES) addresses the "blindness" of traditional evolutionary strategies. Specifically, SAES utilizes simulation feedback to perform local gradient approximation and directed parameter updates, effectively guiding the search toward physically valid Pareto frontiers. Orchestrating specialized agents (Manager, Parser, Generator, and Simulator), AutoMS achieves a state-of-the-art 83.8\% success rate on 17 diverse cross-physics tasks, nearly doubling the performance of traditional NSGA-II (43.7\%) and significantly outperforming ReAct-based LLM baselines (53.3\%). Furthermore, our hierarchical architecture reduces total execution time by 23.3\%. ### [92] [Quantification of Credal Uncertainty: A Distance-Based Approach](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27270)
*Xabier Gonzalez-Garcia,Siu Lun Chau,Julian Rodemann,Michele Caprio,Krikamol Muandet,Humberto Bustince,Sébastien Destercke,Eyke Hüllermeier,Yusuf Sale*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Credal sets are a powerful way to represent both aleatoric (data-inherent) and epistemic (model) uncertainty, but there is no well-established way to numerically quantify and decompose these uncertainties in the multiclass setting. Existing measures are typically tailored to binary classification or to single probability distributions, leaving a gap for credal sets in modern ML tasks.

Method: The authors define uncertainty measures for credal sets using Integral Probability Metrics (IPMs), which assess distances between probability measures. They systematically construct measures for total, aleatoric, and epistemic uncertainty by considering distances between elements of the credal set, and between the credal set and reference distributions. They then specialize this general construction to total variation distance to obtain closed-form or easily computable formulas, and analyze their properties (semantic meaning, axiomatic desiderata, tractability).

Result: They obtain a family of uncertainty measures that (1) cleanly separate total, aleatoric, and epistemic components, (2) have interpretable meanings in terms of distances between distributions, (3) satisfy natural properties one would want from uncertainty measures, and (4) can be computed efficiently, particularly when using total variation distance. In the binary case, these measures reduce to known uncertainty metrics; in the multiclass case, they yield new, principled generalizations. Experiments show these measures perform competitively for tasks like uncertainty estimation and are cheap to compute.

Conclusion: A distance-based IPM framework provides a principled, unified way to quantify and decompose uncertainty for credal sets, bridging a gap between theory and practice, especially for multiclass classification. The instantiation with total variation distance is both theoretically justified and practically effective, recovering known binary measures and extending them coherently to the multiclass setting.

Abstract: Credal sets, i.e., closed convex sets of probability measures, provide a natural framework to represent aleatoric and epistemic uncertainty in machine learning. Yet how to quantify these two types of uncertainty for a given credal set, particularly in multiclass classification, remains underexplored. In this paper, we propose a distance-based approach to quantify total, aleatoric, and epistemic uncertainty for credal sets. Concretely, we introduce a family of such measures within the framework of Integral Probability Metrics (IPMs). The resulting quantities admit clear semantic interpretations, satisfy natural theoretical desiderata, and remain computationally tractable for common choices of IPMs. We instantiate the framework with the total variation distance and obtain simple, efficient uncertainty measures for multiclass classification. In the binary case, this choice recovers established uncertainty measures, for which a principled multiclass generalization has so far been missing. Empirical results confirm practical usefulness, with favorable performance at low computational cost.

### [98] [Beyond Completion: Probing Cumulative State Tracking to Predict LLM Agent Performance](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27343)
*Dengzhe Hou,Lingyu Jiang,Deng Li,Zirui Li,Fangzhou Lin,Kazunori D Yamada*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a new metric (WMF-AM) to measure how well LLM agents track intermediate working memory state during tasks, and show it correlates strongly with agent performance beyond task-completion rate.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard task-completion rate for LLM agents misses differences in how well models track intermediate state; models can have similar completion rates but very different working memory and arithmetic state-tracking capabilities. The authors want a better, calibrated measure of working-memory fidelity, especially for cumulative arithmetic under load, that can explain agent performance differences.

Method: They design WMF-AM, a "no-scratchpad" probing task focused on cumulative arithmetic state tracking under working-memory load. They evaluate this probe on 20 open-weight models spanning 0.5B–35B parameters across 13 model families, and compare its scores to performance on a released, deterministic 10-task agent benchmark. They use pre-specified statistical analysis (Kendall's tau with Bonferroni correction) to quantify correlation between WMF-AM and agent performance, and conduct construct-isolation ablations (K = 1 control, non-arithmetic ceiling condition, yoked cancellation) to identify what cognitive abilities the probe truly measures. They also calibrate difficulty via K to keep the probe in a range where it discriminates between models.

Result: WMF-AM scores strongly predict agent performance, with Kendall's tau = 0.612, statistically significant (p < 0.001) and with a relatively tight confidence interval. Exploratory partial-correlation analyses indicate that WMF-AM retains predictive power even when controlling for overall task completion and model size. Ablation studies suggest that the main challenge is cumulative state tracking under working-memory load, not just single-step arithmetic or simple entity tracking. The K-calibration maintains discriminative power where fixed-depth benchmarks fail to distinguish models.

Conclusion: Working Memory Fidelity-Active Manipulation is a promising, calibrated probe of LLM agents' working-memory state tracking that predicts agent performance better than task-completion rate alone and isolates cumulative arithmetic state tracking as a key capability. However, its generalization beyond the tested open-weight models remains an open question, so further validation is needed.

Abstract: Task-completion rate is the standard proxy for LLM agent capability, but models with identical completion scores can differ substantially in their ability to track intermediate state. We introduce Working Memory Fidelity-Active Manipulation (WMF-AM), a calibrated no-scratchpad probe of cumulative arithmetic state tracking, and evaluate it on 20 open-weight models (0.5B-35B, 13 families) against a released deterministic 10-task agent battery. In a pre-specified, Bonferroni-corrected analysis, WMF-AM predicts agent performance with Kendall's tau = 0.612 (p < 0.001, 95% CI [0.360, 0.814]); exploratory partial-tau analyses suggest this signal persists after controlling for completion score and model scale. Three construct-isolation ablations (K = 1 control, non-arithmetic ceiling, yoked cancellation) support the interpretation that cumulative state tracking under load, rather than single-step arithmetic or entity tracking alone, is the primary difficulty source. K-calibration keeps the probe in a discriminative range where prior fixed-depth benchmarks become non-discriminative; generalization beyond this open-weight sample remains open.

### [99] [LLM Readiness Harness: Evaluation, Observability, and CI Gates for LLM/RAG Applications](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27355)
*Alexandre Cristovão Maiorano*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a "readiness harness" that turns LLM/RAG evaluation into an automated deployment decision workflow with aggregated readiness scores and Pareto frontiers, and they show it works on ticket-routing and BEIR grounding tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM/RAG evaluations are often offline, fragmented, and not directly actionable for deployment decisions; teams lack a systematic, operational way to decide if a system is actually ready to ship under real constraints like latency, cost, and safety policies.

Method: They design a harness that unifies automated benchmarks, observability (OpenTelemetry), and CI quality gates under a minimal API. Result: The harness achieves complete coverage of the experimental matrix and reveals that readiness is multidimensional: different models lead under different constraints and datasets. Conclusion: Their framework provides a reproducible, operationally grounded way to turn evaluation signals into automated ship/no-ship decisions for LLM and RAG systems, emphasizing that deployment readiness cannot be reduced to a single metric.

Abstract: We present a readiness harness for LLM and RAG applications that turns evaluation into a deployment decision workflow. The system combines automated benchmarks, OpenTelemetry observability, and CI quality gates under a minimal API contract, then aggregates workflow success, policy compliance, groundedness, retrieval hit rate, cost, and p95 latency into scenario-weighted readiness scores with Pareto frontiers. We evaluate the harness on ticket-routing workflows and BEIR grounding tasks (SciFact and FiQA) with full Azure matrix coverage (162/162 valid cells across datasets, scenarios, retrieval depths, seeds, and models). Results show that readiness is not a single metric: on FiQA under sla-first at k=5, gpt-4.1-mini leads in readiness and faithfulness, while gpt-5.2 pays a substantial latency cost; on SciFact, models are closer in quality but still separable operationally. Ticket-routing regression gates consistently reject unsafe prompt variants, demonstrating that the harness can block risky releases instead of merely reporting offline scores. The result is a reproducible, operationally grounded framework for deciding whether an LLM or RAG system is ready to ship.

They also extend the ReviewCritique dataset with fine-grained annotations for segments, deficiencies, error types, rebuttal actions, and gold rebuttal alignments for evaluation.

Result: Experiments and a user study show that direct LLM rebuttal generation performs poorly in factual correctness and targeted refutation. Segment-wise generation, especially in the proposed automated sequential approach with author-in-the-loop, significantly improves factual correctness and the strength of refutation.

Conclusion: Structured, author-in-the-loop rebuttal generation with DEFEND provides more factually accurate and targeted responses to reviewers than direct LLM-based rebuttal generation, while reducing author cognitive load and effort.

Abstract: Rebuttal generation is a critical component of the peer review process for scientific papers, enabling authors to clarify misunderstandings, correct factual inaccuracies, and guide reviewers toward a more accurate evaluation. We observe that Large Language Models (LLMs) often struggle to perform targeted refutation and maintain accurate factual grounding when used directly for rebuttal generation, highlighting the need for structured reasoning and author intervention. To address this, in the paper, we introduce DEFEND an LLM based tool designed to explicitly execute the underlying reasoning process of automated rebuttal generation, while keeping the author-in-the-loop. As opposed to writing the rebuttals from scratch, the author needs to only drive the reasoning process with minimal intervention, leading an efficient approach with minimal effort and less cognitive load. We compare DEFEND against three other paradigms: (i) Direct rebuttal generation using LLM (DRG), (ii) Segment-wise rebuttal generation using LLM (SWRG), and (iii) Sequential approach (SA) of segment-wise rebuttal generation without author intervention. To enable finegrained evaluation, we extend the ReviewCritique dataset, creating review segmentation, deficiency, error type annotations, rebuttal-action labels, and mapping to gold rebuttal segments. Experimental results and a user study demonstrate that directly using LLMs perform poorly in factual correctness and targeted refutation. Segment-wise generation and the automated sequential approach with author-in-the-loop, substantially improve factual correctness and strength of refutation.

### [101] [Heterogeneous Debate Engine: Identity-Grounded Cognitive Architecture for Resilient LLM-Based Ethical Tutoring](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27404)
*Jakub Masłowski,Jarosław A. Chudziak*

The authors seek a way to retain adversarial, dialectical richness without losing fidelity to specified ethical doctrines.

Method: They propose the Heterogeneous Debate Engine (HDE), a cognitive architecture that: (1) uses Identity-Grounded Retrieval-Augmented Generation (ID-RAG) to keep each agent aligned to a stable doctrinal identity (e.g., Deontology or Utilitarianism) via targeted retrieval; and (2) incorporates a Heuristic Theory of Mind (ToM) module that allows agents to strategically model and respond to opponents’ beliefs and strategies. They then evaluate debate dynamics and learning outcomes (Argument Complexity Scores) in ethical tutoring scenarios under various doctrinal initializations and architectural choices.

Result: The experiments show that heterogeneous architectures—where agents are initialized with contrary doctrinal identities (such as Deontology vs. Utilitarianism)—produce much richer, more complex arguments than homogeneous or unconstrained baselines. Argument Complexity Scores for students increase by about an order of magnitude, and debates remain more stable and doctrine-consistent, exhibiting less semantic drift and stagnation.

Conclusion: Architectural heterogeneity, combined with identity-grounded RAG and heuristic theory-of-mind modeling, is key to building stable, high-fidelity multi-agent debate systems for adversarial pedagogy. HDE demonstrates that enforcing doctrinal fidelity need not suppress generative flexibility; instead, it can enhance pedagogical value by sustaining rigorous, doctrine-grounded yet dynamic debates.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are being increasingly used as autonomous agents in complex reasoning tasks, opening the niche for dialectical interactions. However, Multi-Agent systems implemented with systematically unconstrained systems systematically undergo semantic drift and logical deterioration and thus can hardly be used in providing ethical tutoring where a precise answer is required. Current simulation often tends to degenerate into dialectical stagnation, the agents degenerate into recursive concurrence or circular arguments. A critical challenge remains: how to enforce doctrinal fidelity without suppressing the generative flexibility required for dialectical reasoning? To address this niche, we contribute the Heterogeneous Debate Engine (HDE), a cognitive architecture that combines Identity-Grounded Retrieval-Augmented Generation (ID-RAG) for doctrinal fidelity and Heuristic Theory of Mind for strategic opponent modeling. Our evaluation shows that architectural heterogeneity is a crucial variable to stability: contrary doctrinal initializations (e.g., Deontology vs. Utilitarianism) have increased the Argument Complexity Scores of students by an order of magnitude, over baselines. These findings validate the effectiveness of ID-RAG and Heuristic ToM as architectural requirements in maintaining high-fidelity (adversarial) pedagogy.

### [102] [On the Relationship between Bayesian Networks and Probabilistic Structural Causal Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27406)
*Peter J. F. Lucas,Eleanora Zullo,Fabio Stella*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how Bayesian networks (BNs) relate to probabilistic structural causal models (SCMs) and how to systematically transform a BN into a probabilistic SCM using linear algebra and linear programming, studying when such a transformation exists, is unique, and how it affects model semantics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although Bayesian networks and structural causal models are both graphical formalisms often used with similar-looking graphs, they differ conceptually: BNs are primarily probabilistic dependency models, whereas SCMs encode explicit causal mechanisms via structural equations. Practitioners often have BNs learned from data or elicited from experts and would like to interpret them causally or convert them into SCMs. It is not clear under what conditions this is possible, what operations are required, and what the consequences are for the network structure, probability distribution, and interpretability. The paper aims to bridge this gap by giving a principled way to relate and transform between these two formalisms.

Method: The authors treat structural causal models as deterministic systems specified by structural equations/functions, then introduce stochasticity via independent latent variables with probability distributions. They then formalize the problem of mapping a given Bayesian network—specified by its directed acyclic graph and associated conditional probability tables—to a probabilistic SCM. Using tools from linear algebra and linear programming, they derive conditions and procedures for constructing structural equations and latent variables that reproduce the BN’s joint distribution. They analyze the model’s parameter and variable dimensions to identify when solutions exist, when they are unique, and how alternative constructions differ. They also compare the semantics of the original BN and the resulting SCM.

Result: The paper shows that under certain dimensional and structural conditions, any given Bayesian network can be represented as a probabilistic structural causal model that preserves its joint probability distribution. Linear algebraic formulations allow expressing the mapping problem as systems of equations, while linear programming methods are used to search for feasible parameterizations. The authors characterize when solutions exist and provide criteria for uniqueness or multiplicity of corresponding SCMs. They identify structural properties that constrain or expand the space of admissible SCMs for a given BN and give examples illustrating successful and problematic mappings.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that Bayesian networks and probabilistic structural causal models are closely related but not interchangeable: a BN can often, but not always uniquely, be transformed into an SCM that preserves its probabilistic behavior. Linear algebra and linear programming provide effective tools for constructing such mappings and for diagnosing when no suitable SCM exists or when multiple, semantically distinct SCMs are compatible with the same BN. This has implications for interpreting learned or expert-specified Bayesian networks causally: additional assumptions are needed to secure a unique causal semantics, and the transformation process can clarify what those assumptions are and how they affect the model’s structure and interpretation.

Abstract: In this paper, the relationship between probabilistic graphical models, in particular Bayesian networks, and causal diagrams, also called structural causal models, is studied. Structural causal models are deterministic models, based on structural equations or functions, that can be provided with uncertainty by adding independent, unobserved random variables to the models, equipped with probability distributions. One question that arises is whether a Bayesian network that has obtained from expert knowledge or learnt from data can be mapped to a probabilistic structural causal model, and whether or not this has consequences for the network structure and probability distribution. We show that linear algebra and linear programming offer key methods for the transformation, and examine properties for the existence and uniqueness of solutions based on dimensions of the probabilistic structural model. Finally, we examine in what way the semantics of the models is affected by this transformation.
  Keywords: Causality, probabilistic structural causal models, Bayesian networks, linear algebra, experimental software.

### [103] [Greedy Is a Strong Default: Agents as Iterative Optimizers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27415)
*Yitao Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Using LLMs as intelligent proposal generators inside classical optimizers, the authors find that simple greedy hill climbing with early stopping is usually enough, because the LLM prior dominates the search.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical optimization relies on random perturbations to explore search spaces, but large language models can now reason about feedback and propose structured, informed candidates. The authors want to know whether the additional sophistication of simulated annealing, population methods, or multiple agents still adds value when proposal generation is no longer random.

Method: Replace random proposal steps in standard optimizers (hill climbing, simulated annealing, population-based/multi-investigator schemes) with an LLM agent that reads evaluation diagnostics and suggests next candidates. Evaluate this hybrid approach on four diverse tasks (rule-based cancer classification, CNN hyperparameter tuning, LoRA fine-tuning, and XGBoost hyperparameters), perform cross-task ablations comparing different optimization wrappers and acceptance rules, and analyze convergence behavior and interpretability of learned solutions.

Result: Across all four benchmark tasks and three independent runs per setting, using an LLM as proposer significantly improves performance versus naive random search or basic baselines, but the choice of optimization wrapper has little effect: greedy hill climbing achieves nearly the same final performance as more complex methods while using 2–3x fewer evaluations. Simulated annealing, parallel investigators, and using a second LLM model (Codex) provide no consistent benefits. Discovered solutions are also interpretable, with the cancer rules aligning with known cytopathology principles.

Conclusion: When an LLM provides strong, informed proposal distributions, the marginal value of sophisticated acceptance rules and exploration schedules is small. In this regime, simple greedy hill climbing with early stopping is an effective default optimizer that is sample-efficient and produces interpretable artifacts. Classical machinery like annealing or population-based search may be unnecessary for many practical tasks when paired with capable LLM agents.

Abstract: Classical optimization algorithms--hill climbing, simulated annealing, population-based methods--generate candidate solutions via random perturbations. We replace the random proposal generator with an LLM agent that reasons about evaluation diagnostics to propose informed candidates, and ask: does the classical optimization machinery still help when the proposer is no longer random? We evaluate on four tasks spanning discrete, mixed, and continuous search spaces (all replicated across 3 independent runs): rule-based classification on Breast Cancer (test accuracy 86.0% to 96.5%), mixed hyperparameter optimization for MobileNetV3-Small on STL-10 (84.5% to 85.8%, zero catastrophic failures vs. 60% for random search), LoRA fine-tuning of Qwen2.5-0.5B on SST-2 (89.5% to 92.7%, matching Optuna TPE with 2x efficiency), and XGBoost on Adult Census (AUC 0.9297 to 0.9317, tying CMA-ES with 3x fewer evaluations). Empirically, on these tasks: a cross-task ablation shows that simulated annealing, parallel investigators, and even a second LLM model (OpenAI Codex) provide no benefit over greedy hill climbing while requiring 2-3x more evaluations. In our setting, the LLM's learned prior appears strong enough that acceptance-rule sophistication has limited impact--round 1 alone delivers the majority of improvement, and variants converge to similar configurations across strategies. The practical implication is surprising simplicity: greedy hill climbing with early stopping is a strong default. Beyond accuracy, the framework produces human-interpretable artifacts--the discovered cancer classification rules independently recapitulate established cytopathology principles.

AstraAI can safely and effectively generate and modify complex scientific code within established HPC frameworks such as AMReX, and its flexible model deployment makes it suitable for diverse HPC environments.

AstraAI operates directly within a Linux terminal and integrates large language models (LLMs) with Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) and Abstract Syntax Tree (AST)-based structural analysis to enable context-aware code generation for complex scientific codebases. The central idea is to construct a high-fidelity prompt that is passed to the LLM for inference. This prompt augments the user request with relevant code snippets retrieved from the underlying framework codebase via RAG and structural context extracted from AST analysis, providing the model with precise information about relevant functions, data structures, and overall code organization. The framework is designed to perform scoped modifications to source code while preserving structural consistency with the surrounding code. AstraAI supports both locally hosted models from Hugging Face and API-based frontier models accessible via the American Science Cloud, enabling flexible deployment across HPC environments. The system generates code that aligns with existing project structures and programming patterns. We demonstrate AstraAI on representative HPC code generation tasks within AMReX, a DOE-supported HPC software infrastructure for exascale applications.

### [105] [The Novelty Bottleneck: A Framework for Understanding Human Effort Scaling in AI-Assisted Work](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27438)
*Jacky Liang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a simple model of human-AI collaboration focused on the “novelty bottleneck,” showing that the fraction of truly novel decisions forces a linear scaling of human effort in many cases, despite advanced AI assistance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand why, even with powerful AI systems, human labor may not shrink smoothly or dramatically for complex tasks, and to clarify misconceptions about limitless AI-driven productivity and intelligence explosions by identifying which parts of work are fundamentally constrained by human judgment.

Method: They build a stylized theoretical model where tasks break down into atomic decisions and a fixed fraction ν of those are genuinely novel to the AI (outside its prior). They assume specification, verification, and correction costs scale with task size, then use asymptotic and complexity-like analysis (inspired by Amdahl’s Law) to derive how human effort and team structure scale with task size and AI capability.

Result: From the model, they derive several predictions: (1) human effort doesn’t gradually become sublinear—there’s a sharp transition from linear O(E) to constant O(1) human effort with no intermediate regime; (2) improved AI mainly reduces constants but not the scaling exponent of human effort; (3) optimal human team size shrinks as AI improves; (4) total human effort remains linear O(E) even when calendar time can drop to O(√E) via parallel teams; and (5) AI is constrained by human novelty on frontier research but unconstrained when exploiting existing knowledge. They compare these predictions with data from coding benchmarks, science productivity statistics, and practitioner case studies, finding qualitative alignment.

Conclusion: The key determinant of AI-augmented productivity is the novelty fraction ν—the share of decisions requiring fresh human judgment—rather than raw AI capability alone. This framework explains why AI may greatly speed up exploitation of existing knowledge while still being bottlenecked on frontier innovation, moderating expectations of sudden intelligence explosions and showing that human effort can remain a linear bottleneck unless novelty itself is reduced.

Abstract: We propose a stylized model of human-AI collaboration that isolates a mechanism we call the novelty bottleneck: the fraction of a task requiring human judgment creates an irreducible serial component analogous to Amdahl's Law in parallel computing. The model assumes that tasks decompose into atomic decisions, a fraction $ν$ of which are "novel" (not covered by the agent's prior), and that specification, verification, and error correction each scale with task size. From these assumptions, we derive several non-obvious consequences: (1) there is no smooth sublinear regime for human effort it transitions sharply from $O(E)$ to $O(1)$ with no intermediate scaling class; (2) better agents improve the coefficient on human effort but not the exponent; (3) for organizations of n humans with AI agents, optimal team size decreases with agent capability; (4) wall-clock time achieves $O(\sqrt{E})$ through team parallelism but total human effort remains $O(E)$; and (5) the resulting AI safety profile is asymmetric -- AI is bottlenecked on frontier research but unbottlenecked on exploiting existing knowledge. We show these predictions are consistent with empirical observations from AI coding benchmarks, scientific productivity data, and practitioner reports. Our contribution is not a proof that human effort must scale linearly, but a framework that identifies the novelty fraction as the key parameter governing AI-assisted productivity, and derives consequences that clarify -- rather than refute -- prevalent narratives about intelligence explosions and the "country of geniuses in a data center."

</details>


### [106] [PeopleSearchBench: A Multi-Dimensional Benchmark for Evaluating AI-Powered People Search Platforms](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27476)
*Wei Wang,Tianyu Shi,Shuai Zhang,Boyang Xia,Zequn Xie,Chenyu Zeng,Qi Zhang,Lynn Ai,Yaqi Yu,Kaiming Zhang,Feiyue Tang*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI-driven people search tools are widely used in recruiting, sales, and networking, but there is no standard, rigorous, and transparent way to compare their performance. Existing evaluations often rely on subjective LLM-as-judge setups and lack reproducible, criteria-based relevance judgments. The paper aims to fill this gap by creating a principled benchmark and evaluation methodology for people search systems.

Method: The authors create PeopleSearchBench, a benchmark with 119 real-world people search queries across four use cases: corporate recruiting, B2B sales prospecting, deterministic expert search, and influencer/KOL discovery. They propose Criteria-Grounded Verification, a pipeline that extracts explicit, verifiable criteria from each query, then uses live web search to fact-check whether each retrieved person satisfies these criteria, yielding binary relevance labels. They evaluate four platforms along three axes: (1) Relevance Precision via padded nDCG@10 using the binary labels; (2) Effective Coverage via task completion rates and the yield of qualified results; and (3) Information Utility via measures of profile completeness and usefulness. They compute an overall score as the equal-weighted average of these dimensions. They further provide confidence intervals, human validation of the verification pipeline (including Cohen's kappa), ablation studies, and full documentation plus code and data on GitHub.

Result: Among four evaluated systems, Lessie, a specialized AI people search agent, achieves the highest overall score of 65.2, which is 18.5% higher than the second-best system, and is the only one with 100% task completion across all 119 queries. The Criteria-Grounded Verification pipeline achieves strong agreement with human judgments (Cohen's kappa = 0.84). The benchmark yields detailed comparative performance data across all three evaluation dimensions, with robustness analyses and confidence intervals.

Conclusion: PeopleSearchBench provides a rigorous, transparent, and reproducible benchmark for AI-powered people search systems, grounded in factual criteria verification rather than subjective assessments. The work demonstrates that specialized AI agents like Lessie can significantly outperform more general platforms on realistic people search tasks. By releasing the code, queries, and evaluation pipeline, the paper establishes a foundation for future research, comparison, and improvement of people search technologies.

Abstract: AI-powered people search platforms are increasingly used in recruiting, sales prospecting, and professional networking, yet no widely accepted benchmark exists for evaluating their performance. We introduce PeopleSearchBench, an open-source benchmark that compares four people search platforms on 119 real-world queries across four use cases: corporate recruiting, B2B sales prospecting, expert search with deterministic answers, and influencer/KOL discovery. A key contribution is Criteria-Grounded Verification, a factual relevance pipeline that extracts explicit, verifiable criteria from each query and uses live web search to determine whether returned people satisfy them. This produces binary relevance judgments grounded in factual verification rather than subjective holistic LLM-as-judge scores. We evaluate systems on three dimensions: Relevance Precision (padded nDCG@10), Effective Coverage (task completion and qualified result yield), and Information Utility (profile completeness and usefulness), averaged equally into an overall score. Lessie, a specialized AI people search agent, performs best overall, scoring 65.2, 18.5% higher than the second-ranked system, and is the only system to achieve 100% task completion across all 119 queries. We also report confidence intervals, human validation of the verification pipeline (Cohen's kappa = 0.84), ablations, and full documentation of queries, prompts, and normalization procedures. Code, query definitions, and aggregated results are available on GitHub.

This paper presents a scenario-centric framework for reproducible auditing of LLM-based risk reasoning in urban driving contexts. Result: The paper concludes that indicator-based probabilistic attributions of consciousness to contemporary AI are not sufficiently justified given theoretical disagreement, lack of validation, and absence of ground truth. Conclusion: The authors recommend suspending or greatly downgrading confidence in current probabilistic claims about AI consciousness. Abstract: Recent work on artificial consciousness shifts evaluation from behaviour to internal architecture, deriving indicators from theories of consciousness and updating credences accordingly. This is progress beyond naive Turing-style tests. But the indicator-based programme remains epistemically under-calibrated: consciousness science is theoretically fragmented, indicators lack independent validation, and no ground truth of artificial phenomenality exists. Under these conditions, probabilistic consciousness attribution to current AI systems is premature. A more defensible near-term strategy is to redirect effort toward biologically grounded engineering -- biohybrid, neuromorphic, and connectome-scale systems -- that reduces the gap with the only domain where consciousness is empirically anchored: living systems.

### [109] [What does a system modify when it modifies itself?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27611)
*Florentin Koch*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a formal framework to distinguish different kinds of self-modification in cognitive and AI systems, highlighting a structural asymmetry between humans and current AI and deriving predictions and open problems from this framework.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current cognitive science and AI both discuss systems that change their own functioning (e.g., executive control, metacognition, hierarchical learning, self-modifying algorithms), but they lack a shared, precise way to say what exactly is being changed and how to compare biological and artificial systems. The authors aim to provide a minimal, general structure that clarifies what it means for a system to be self-modifying and that can be used to systematically compare human and artificial cognition, including debates about artificial consciousness.

Method: The authors introduce a formal, hierarchical model of rules within a cognitive system. They distinguish: (a) a hierarchy of rules (from low-level operational rules up to high-level evaluative norms), (b) a fixed core that cannot be modified, and (c) three notions of rules (effective rules that actually govern behavior, represented rules that the system encodes, and causally accessible rules that it can change). Using this framework, they classify four regimes of self-modification: no modification, low-level modification, structural modification (changing the architecture or higher-level rules), and teleological revision (changing the evaluative norms that govern modifications). They then map each regime onto both human cognitive phenomena and corresponding artificial systems and analyze how self-representation and causal access are distributed across the hierarchy in humans vs AI. They apply the framework to existing theories of artificial consciousness, such as higher-order theories and the Attention Schema Theory, and derive conceptual predictions and open problems.

Result: The framework shows that any genuinely self-modifying system must have a minimal structure consisting of a rule hierarchy, a fixed core, and distinctions between effective, represented, and causally accessible rules. Within this structure, they identify four distinct regimes of modification, ranging from simple action to deep revisions of goals and evaluative norms. Applying the framework to humans vs current AI reveals a "crossing of opacities": humans have rich self-representation and strong causal control at high evaluative and metacognitive levels but limited access to low-level operational processes, whereas current reflexive AI systems have transparent, manipulable low-level operations but lack representation and control at the highest evaluative levels. This crossed asymmetry is proposed as a structural signature distinguishing human cognition from present AI. The framework also shows that many discussions of artificial consciousness (e.g., higher-order theories, Attention Schema Theory) can be recast within its hierarchy of rules and levels of self-modification.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that understanding self-modification requires a specific minimal architecture and that, within this architecture, humans and current AI systems occupy structurally inverted positions regarding which parts of the hierarchy are transparent and controllable. This "crossing of opacities" offers a principled way to compare human and artificial cognition and to structure debates about artificial consciousness. The authors propose four testable predictions and outline four open problems—about the independence of self-modification from autonomy, the possibility and limits of self-modifying systems, the risk of systems becoming trapped by their own goals (teleological lock), and how identity is preserved under deep transformation—thus providing a research agenda for both cognitive science and AI.

Abstract: When a cognitive system modifies its own functioning, what exactly does it modify: a low-level rule, a control rule, or the norm that evaluates its own revisions? Cognitive science describes executive control, metacognition, and hierarchical learning with precision, but lacks a formal framework distinguishing these targets of transformation. Contemporary artificial intelligence likewise exhibits self-modification without common criteria for comparison with biological cognition.
  We show that the question of what counts as a self-modifying system entails a minimal structure: a hierarchy of rules, a fixed core, and a distinction between effective rules, represented rules, and causally accessible rules. Four regimes are identified: (1) action without modification, (2) low-level modification, (3) structural modification, and (4) teleological revision. Each regime is anchored in a cognitive phenomenon and a corresponding artificial system.
  Applied to humans, the framework yields a central result: a crossing of opacities. Humans have self-representation and causal power concentrated at upper hierarchical levels, while operational levels remain largely opaque. Reflexive artificial systems display the inverse profile: rich representation and causal access at operational levels, but none at the highest evaluative level. This crossed asymmetry provides a structural signature for human-AI comparison. The framework also offers insight into artificial consciousness, with higher-order theories and Attention Schema Theory as special cases. We derive four testable predictions and identify four open problems: the independence of transformativity and autonomy, the viability of self-modification, the teleological lock, and identity under transformation.

*Jin Huang,Jie Yang,XinLei Zhou,Qihao Liu,Liang Gao,Xinyu Li*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Dynamic manufacturing systems frequently experience disruptions like machine breakdowns and new order arrivals, which invalidate fixed or single best dispatching rules. Current LLM-based Automatic Heuristic Design approaches converge to one elite rule and lack the adaptability and response speed required on real shop floors. There is a need for a practical framework that can both generate diverse, high-quality scheduling heuristics and rapidly select the most appropriate one as conditions change.

Method: The authors develop DSevolve, which works in two phases. Offline, it uses multi-persona LLM seeding and topology-aware evolutionary operators to evolve a behaviorally diverse set of dispatching rules organized via a MAP-Elites feature space, forming a quality-diverse archive. Online, upon each disruption event, a probe-based fingerprinting mechanism characterizes the current shop floor state, retrieves promising rules from the archive, and uses fast look-ahead simulation to select the best rule to apply, all within second-level response time.

Result: On 500 dynamic flexible job shop instances built from real industrial data, DSevolve is experimentally shown to outperform state-of-the-art LLM-powered AHD frameworks, traditional dispatching rules, genetic programming-based heuristics, and deep reinforcement learning approaches, indicating both higher scheduling quality and strong adaptability in dynamic environments.

Conclusion: Maintaining and adaptively deploying a diverse portfolio of dispatching rules is more effective for dynamic manufacturing scheduling than converging to a single elite rule. DSevolve demonstrates that combining LLM-based rule generation, quality-diversity evolution, and rapid online selection yields a practical, high-performing solution suitable for real industrial shop floor environments.

Abstract: In dynamic manufacturing environments, disruptions such as machine breakdowns and new order arrivals continuously shift the optimal dispatching strategy, making adaptive rule selection essential. Existing LLM-powered Automatic Heuristic Design (AHD) frameworks evolve toward a single elite rule that cannot meet this adaptability demand. To address this, we present DSevolve, an industrial scheduling framework that evolves a quality-diverse portfolio of dispatching rules offline and adaptively deploys them online with second-level response time. Multi-persona seeding and topology-aware evolutionary operators produce a behaviorally diverse rule archive indexed by a MAP-Elites feature space. Upon each disruption event, a probe-based fingerprinting mechanism characterizes the current shop floor state, retrieves high-quality candidate rules from an offline knowledge base, and selects the best one via rapid look-ahead simulation. Evaluated on 500 dynamic flexible job shop instances derived from real industrial data, DSevolve outperforms state-of-the-art AHD frameworks, classical dispatching rules, genetic programming, and deep reinforcement learning, offering a practical and deployable solution for intelligent shop floor scheduling.

In two classic atmospheric dynamic scenarios (squall-line cold pools and typhoon track deflections), TianJi accomplishes expert-level end-to-end experimental operations with zero human intervention, compressing the research cycle to a few hours. It also delivers detailed result analyses and autonomously judges and explains the validity of the hypotheses from outputs. TianJi reveals that the role of AI in Earth system science is transitioning from a "black-box predictor" to an "interpretable scientific collaborator", offering a new paradigm for high-throughput exploration of scientific mechanisms.

### [112] [SkyNet: Belief-Aware Planning for Partially-Observable Stochastic Games](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27751)
*Adam Haile*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: SkyNet (Belief-Aware MuZero) augments MuZero with belief-focused auxiliary prediction heads, yielding much stronger play in a partially observable, stochastic multi-player card game once enough training data is available.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: MuZero works very well in perfect-information settings but lacks explicit mechanisms to represent uncertainty about hidden state, which is crucial in partially observable, stochastic, multi-player domains such as card games, trading, negotiation, and robotics. The authors want to improve MuZero’s performance in such settings without redesigning its search procedure or adding full belief-state tracking, by nudging its learned latent representations to better capture outcome-relevant uncertainty.

Method: They propose SkyNet (Belief-Aware MuZero), which keeps MuZero’s overall model-based RL and MCTS structure but adds ego-conditioned auxiliary prediction heads for winner prediction and final rank estimation. These auxiliary tasks are trained jointly with the standard MuZero objectives and are designed to force the latent state to encode information useful for predicting eventual outcomes under partial observability, without changing the planner or explicitly maintaining a belief over hidden variables. They implement this for the partially observable card game Skyjo, using a decision-granularity environment, transformer-based encoders, and a training regime combining curriculum against heuristic bots with self-play.

Result: On the Skyjo benchmark, in 1000-game head-to-head matches at matched training checkpoints, SkyNet attains a peak 75.3% win rate versus the standard MuZero baseline, corresponding to about +194 Elo with extremely high statistical significance (p < 10^-50). Against heuristic opponents, it also wins much more often (0.720 vs. 0.466 win rate). Learning dynamics show that SkyNet initially underperforms the baseline but overtakes it after sufficient training, indicating a higher data requirement but better asymptotic performance.

Conclusion: Belief-aware auxiliary supervision—via ego-conditioned winner and rank prediction heads—can substantially improve MuZero’s performance in partially observable, stochastic multi-player settings by enhancing the quality of its latent representations, even without explicit belief modeling or modifications to MCTS. However, these benefits only manifest once enough training throughput is available, implying a trade-off between data efficiency and representational richness in such belief-augmented architectures.

Abstract: In 2019, Google DeepMind released MuZero, a model-based reinforcement learning method that achieves strong results in perfect-information games by combining learned dynamics models with Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS). However, comparatively little work has extended MuZero to partially observable, stochastic, multi-player environments, where agents must act under uncertainty about hidden state. Such settings arise not only in card games but in domains such as autonomous negotiation, financial trading, and multi-agent robotics. In the absence of explicit belief modeling, MuZero's latent encoding has no dedicated mechanism for representing uncertainty over unobserved variables.
  To address this, we introduce SkyNet (Belief-Aware MuZero), which adds ego-conditioned auxiliary heads for winner prediction and rank estimation to the standard MuZero architecture. These objectives encourage the latent state to retain information predictive of outcomes under partial observability, without requiring explicit belief-state tracking or changes to the search algorithm.
  We evaluate SkyNet on Skyjo, a partially observable, non-zero-sum, stochastic card game, using a decision-granularity environment, transformer-based encoding, and a curriculum of heuristic opponents with self-play. In 1000-game head-to-head evaluations at matched checkpoints, SkyNet achieves a 75.3% peak win rate against the baseline (+194 Elo, $p < 10^{-50}$). SkyNet also outperforms the baseline against heuristic opponents (0.720 vs.\ 0.466 win rate). Critically, the belief-aware model initially underperforms the baseline but decisively surpasses it once training throughput is sufficient, suggesting that belief-aware auxiliary supervision improves learned representations under partial observability, but only given adequate data flow.
