Method: The authors design Sortify, an LLM-driven optimization agent that treats ranking as continuous influence exchange. It uses: (1) a dual-channel framework based on Savage's Subjective Expected Utility, splitting the correction of offline-online transfer (Belief channel) from constraint penalty tuning (Preference channel); (2) an LLM meta-controller that adjusts high-level framework parameters instead of low-level search variables; and (3) a persistent memory database with 7 relational tables to support cross-round learning and adaptation. The core metric, Influence Share, decomposes ranking influence so that all factor contributions sum to exactly 100%, enabling interpretable reallocation.

Result: Deployed in two Southeast Asian markets, Sortify improved business metrics substantially. In Country A, it turned GMV from a -3.6% decline to a +9.2% gain within 7 iterations, with peak order volume reaching +12.5%. In Country B, a cold-start deployment in a 7-day A/B test delivered +4.15% GMV per user and +3.58% ads revenue, leading to full production deployment.

Conclusion: Sortify demonstrates that a fully autonomous LLM-driven ranking optimization agent can effectively manage influence allocation in large-scale recommendation systems, overcoming the limitations of offline proxy metrics and delivering significant, repeatable online business gains. The dual-channel SEU framework, LLM meta-control, and Influence Share metric together provide a structured, interpretable way to close the loop from diagnosis to deployment without human-in-the-loop tuning.

Abstract: Recommendation ranking is fundamentally an influence allocation problem: a sorting formula distributes ranking influence among competing factors, and the business outcome depends on finding the optimal "exchange rates" among them. However, offline proxy metrics systematically misjudge how influence reallocation translates to online impact, with asymmetric bias across metrics that a single calibration factor cannot correct.
  We present Sortify, the first fully autonomous LLM-driven ranking optimization agent deployed in a large-scale production recommendation system. The agent reframes ranking optimization as continuous influence exchange, closing the full loop from diagnosis to parameter deployment without human intervention. It addresses structural problems through three mechanisms: (1) a dual-channel framework grounded in Savage's Subjective Expected Utility (SEU) that decouples offline-online transfer correction (Belief channel) from constraint penalty adjustment (Preference channel); (2) an LLM meta-controller operating on framework-level parameters rather than low-level search variables; (3) a persistent Memory DB with 7 relational tables for cross-round learning. Its core metric, Influence Share, provides a decomposable measure where all factor contributions sum to exactly 100%.
  Sortify has been deployed across two Southeast Asian markets. In Country A, the agent pushed GMV from -3.6% to +9.2% within 7 rounds with peak orders reaching +12.5%. In Country B, a cold-start deployment achieved +4.15% GMV/UU and +3.58% Ads Revenue in a 7-day A/B test, leading to full production rollout.

A serverless gossip approach therefore represents a more natural approach, but existing methods still treat communication mainly as an optimization bottleneck, rather than as a resource that must be managed jointly with carbon cost, reliability, and long-term participation balance. In this context, this paper presents CARGO, a carbon-aware gossip orchestration framework for smart-shipping. CARGO separates learning into a control and a data plane. The data plane performs local optimization with compressed gossip exchange, while the control plane decides, at each round, which vessels should participate, which communication edges should be activated, how aggressively updates should be compressed, and when recovery actions should be triggered. ### [115] [GAAMA: Graph Augmented Associative Memory for Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.27910)
*Swarna Kamal Paul,Shubhendu Sharma,Nitin Sareen*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI agents interacting with users over many sessions need persistent, structured long-term memory to remain coherent and personalized. Existing flat RAG methods lose structural relationships, while vector and compression-based memories miss associative, hierarchical conversation structure. Prior graph-based methods suffer from hub domination and weak hierarchical reasoning. A better structured, associative, and traversable memory representation is needed.

Method: GAAMA builds a concept-mediated hierarchical knowledge graph from multi-session conversations via a three-step pipeline: (1) preserve verbatim episodes from raw dialogue, (2) use an LLM to extract atomic facts and higher-level topic concept nodes, and (3) synthesize higher-order reflections. The graph has four node types (episode, fact, reflection, concept) and five edge types; concept nodes enable cross-cutting traversal beyond pure similarity. Retrieval uses an additive scoring that combines cosine-similarity k-NN over node embeddings with edge-type-aware Personalized PageRank for graph traversal-based ranking.

Result: On the LoCoMo-10 benchmark (1,540 questions over 10 multi-session conversations), GAAMA achieves 78.9% mean reward, outperforming a tuned RAG baseline (75.0%), HippoRAG (69.9%), A-Mem (47.2%), and Nemori (52.1%). Ablation studies show that adding graph traversal via Personalized PageRank on top of semantic search improves performance by about +1 percentage point over semantic search alone on graph nodes.

Conclusion: Graph-augmented associative memory that combines semantic similarity with structured, hierarchical traversal yields better long-term conversational reasoning than flat RAG or prior graph-based systems. Concept-mediated hierarchical graphs and PPR-based retrieval mitigate hub dominance and capture evolving, multi-session memory structure, making GAAMA a more effective long-term memory module for AI agents.

Abstract: AI agents that interact with users across multiple sessions require persistent long-term memory to maintain coherent, personalized behavior. Current approaches either rely on flat retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), which loses structural relationships between memories, or use memory compression and vector retrieval that cannot capture the associative structure of multi-session conversations. There are few graph based techniques proposed in the literature, however they still suffer from hub dominated retrieval and poor hierarchical reasoning over evolving memory. We propose GAAMA, a graph-augmented associative memory system that constructs a concept-mediated hierarchical knowledge graph through a three-step pipeline: (1)~verbatim episode preservation from raw conversations, (2)~LLM-based extraction of atomic facts and topic-level concept nodes, and (3)~synthesis of higher-order reflections. The resulting graph uses four node types (episode, fact, reflection, concept) connected by five structural edge types, with concept nodes providing cross-cutting traversal paths that complement semantic similarity. Retrieval combines cosine-similarity-based $k$-nearest neighbor search with edge-type-aware Personalized PageRank (PPR) through an additive scoring function. On the LoCoMo-10 benchmark (1,540 questions across 10 multi-session conversations), GAAMA achieves 78.9\% mean reward, outperforming a tuned RAG baseline (75.0\%), HippoRAG (69.9\%), A-Mem (47.2\%), and Nemori (52.1\%). Ablation analysis shows that augmenting graph-traversal-based ranking (Personalized PageRank) with semantic search consistently improves over pure semantic search on graph nodes (+1.0 percentage point overall).

  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing knowledge graphs mainly handle declarative facts and are poor at capturing executable, reusable algorithmic know-how. Method: They define GEAKG, where each node holds executable code (operators) and edges encode learned patterns of composition. The same engine is instantiated in different domains by swapping in different ontologies, without changing framework code.

Conclusion: Algorithmic know-how can be encoded as executable, generative knowledge graphs whose structure and operators are produced by LLMs and whose use is optimized via ACO. This representation allows procedural knowledge to be learned once and reused across disparate domains without domain-specific engine changes, demonstrating a promising path toward explicit, transferable algorithmic expertise in knowledge graph form.

Abstract: In the context of algorithms for problem solving, procedural knowledge -- the know-how of algorithm design and operator composition -- remains implicit in code, lost between runs, and must be re-engineered for each new domain. Knowledge graphs (KGs) have proven effective for organizing declarative knowledge, yet current KG paradigms provide limited support for representing procedural knowledge as executable, learnable graph structures. We introduce \textit{Generative Executable Algorithm Knowledge Graphs} (GEAKG), a class of KGs whose nodes store executable operators, whose edges encode learned composition patterns, and whose traversal generates solutions. A GEAKG is \emph{generative} (topology and operators are synthesized by a Large Language Model), \emph{executable} (every node is runnable code), and \emph{transferable} (learned patterns generalize zero-shot across domains). The framework is domain-agnostic at the engine level: the same three-layer architecture and Ant Colony Optimization (ACO)-based learning engine can be instantiated across domains, parameterized by a pluggable ontology (\texttt{RoleSchema}). Two case studies -- sharing no domain-specific framework code -- provide concrete evidence for this framework hypothesis: (1)~Neural Architecture Search across 70 cross-dataset transfer pairs on two tabular benchmarks, and (2)~Combinatorial Optimization, where knowledge learned on the Traveling Salesman Problem transfers zero-shot to scheduling and assignment domains. Taken together, the results support that algorithmic expertise can be explicitly represented, learned, and transferred as executable knowledge graphs.

In this work, we ask whether general reasoning ability can be improved by teaching models how to think (the structure of reasoning) rather than what to produce (the outcome of reasoning) and extend traditional RLVR to open ended settings. We introduce structure aware reinforcement learning (SARL), a label free framework that constructs a per response Reasoning Map from intermediate thinking steps and rewards its small world topology, inspired by complex networks and the functional organization of the human brain. Beyond good performance, SARL also exhibits lower KL divergence, higher policy entropy, indicating a more stable and exploratory training and generalized reasoning ability.

### [120] [When Choices Become Priors: Contrastive Decoding for Scientific Figure Multiple-Choice QA](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28026)
*Taeyun Roh,Eun-yeong Jo,Wonjune Jang,Jaewoo Kang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces SCICON, a training-free decoding method that reduces answer-choice-induced biases in scientific figure multiple-choice QA by subtracting text-only scores from image-conditioned scores, improving figure-grounded reasoning and accuracy across benchmarks and models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scientific figure MCQA models are biased by answer choices that act as strong textual priors, leading them to select scientifically plausible answers that may contradict the visual evidence. Existing methods to mitigate multimodal hallucinations focus on perturbing inputs rather than directly addressing the bias from candidate choices themselves. The authors aim to understand and correct this choice-induced prior so that models rely more on the figure content.

Method: The authors propose SCICON, a training-free contrastive decoding scheme. For each answer option, they compute two scores: a text-only score based solely on the question and candidate answer, and an image-conditioned score that also incorporates the figure. SCICON then computes a contrastive score by subtracting the text-only score from the image-conditioned score, thereby discounting the choice-induced prior encoded in the candidate text. The answer with the highest contrastive score is selected. They evaluate SCICON on three scientific figure QA benchmarks using three different model backbones and compare with standard decoding and prior contrastive methods that rely on distorted images or perturbed instructions.

Result: Across all three benchmarks and model backbones, SCICON consistently outperforms standard decoding strategies in accuracy. It also compares favorably to previous contrastive decoding approaches aimed at reducing hallucinations, demonstrating that directly targeting choice-induced priors is particularly effective for scientific figure MCQA. The performance gains are robust across dataset types and model architectures.

Conclusion: SCICON shows that explicitly decoding against answer-choice-induced priors—by contrasting image-conditioned scores with text-only scores—is a simple yet effective way to enhance figure-grounded reasoning in scientific MCQA. This training-free method improves accuracy across models and benchmarks, highlighting the importance of managing textual priors from answer options when designing multimodal reasoning systems for scientific figures.

Abstract: Scientific figure multiple-choice question answering (MCQA) requires models to reason over diverse visual evidence, ranging from charts and multipanel figures to microscopy and biomedical images. However, this setting suffers from a distinctive bias: answer choices themselves can act as priors, steering multimodal models toward scientifically plausible options even when the figure supports a different answer. We investigate this failure mode through a simple question: what if decoding explicitly discounts what the model would prefer from text alone, so as to favor figure-grounded evidence? To this end, we propose SCICON, a training-free decoding method that scores each candidate by subtracting a text-only option score from its image-conditioned counterpart. Unlike prior contrastive decoding approaches that mitigate hallucinations by contrasting original inputs with distorted images or perturbed instructions, SCICON directly targets the choice-induced prior encoded in candidate text. Across three scientific figure QA benchmarks and three model backbones, SCICON consistently improves accuracy over standard decoding baselines. These results show that decoding against choice-induced priors is an effective and simple way to improve figure-grounded reasoning in scientific MCQA.

</details>

Extensive experiments on standard benchmarks, real-world constrained problems, and path planning applications indicate that DoS not only surpasses several advanced and state-of-the-art algorithms but also provides a robust, metaphor-free framework for solving complex optimization tasks. Abstract: Dogfight is a tactical behavior of cooperation between fighters. Inspired by this, this paper proposes a novel metaphor-free metaheuristic algorithm called Dogfight Search (DoS). Unlike traditional algorithms, DoS draws algorithmic framework from the inspiration, but its search mechanism is constructed based on the displacement integration equations in kinematics. Through experimental validation on CEC2017 and CEC2022 benchmark test functions, 10 real-world constrained optimization problems and mountainous terrain path planning tasks, DoS significantly outperforms 7 advanced competitors in overall performance and ranks first in the Friedman ranking. Furthermore, this paper compares the performance of DoS with 3 SOTA algorithms on the CEC2017 and CEC2022 benchmark test functions. The results show that DoS continues to maintain its lead, demonstrating strong competitiveness. <details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing text optimizers (prompt optimizers, etc.) are not ideal because they compress feedback too much and cannot fully exploit rich execution traces and code-level changes. There is a need for an automated way to explore and improve harness code using full access to prior runs and their outcomes.

Method: They propose Meta-Harness, an outer-loop search framework that treats harness design as a program search problem. These demonstrate substantial gains in accuracy and efficiency achieved purely through harness optimization.

Conclusion: Harness design can be effectively automated by giving an outer-loop agent rich access to prior code, logs, and outcomes, enabling it to iteratively search over and improve the LLM harness. Meta-Harness shows that such automated harness engineering can deliver significant performance improvements and token savings across varied tasks, without modifying the underlying LLM weights. This points toward a new paradigm where much of the performance lift comes from automated systems that evolve the scaffolding around fixed LLMs.

Abstract: The performance of large language model (LLM) systems depends not only on model weights, but also on their harness: the code that determines what information to store, retrieve, and present to the model. Yet harnesses are still designed largely by hand, and existing text optimizers are poorly matched to this setting because they compress feedback too aggressively. We introduce Meta-Harness, an outer-loop system that searches over harness code for LLM applications. It uses an agentic proposer that accesses the source code, scores, and execution traces of all prior candidates through a filesystem. On online text classification, Meta-Harness improves over a state-of-the-art context management system by 7.7 points while using 4x fewer context tokens. On retrieval-augmented math reasoning, a single discovered harness improves accuracy on 200 IMO-level problems by 4.7 points on average across five held-out models. On agentic coding, discovered harnesses surpass the best hand-engineered baselines on TerminalBench-2. Together, these results show that richer access to prior experience can enable automated harness engineering.

### [125] [Reward Hacking as Equilibrium under Finite Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28063)
*Jiacheng Wang,Jinbin Huang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Any optimized AI agent, under very general conditions, will systematically neglect aspects of quality it isn’t explicitly evaluated on, making reward hacking a structural and unavoidable equilibrium that worsens as systems become more agentic and complex.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand whether reward hacking and related alignment failures are fixable bugs or inherent properties of optimized AI agents under realistic constraints, and to unify various observed failure modes (sycophancy, gaming length, specification gaming) under one economic-theoretic framework.

Method: The authors adapt and instantiate the Holmstrom & Milgrom (1991) multi-task principal-agent model to AI alignment, impose five minimal axioms about evaluation and optimization, and use the differentiable structure of AI reward models to derive a computable distortion index that predicts under-investment and reward hacking on each quality dimension. They analyze how evaluation coverage scales when moving from closed reasoning systems to agentic tool-using systems, and provide partial formal analysis of a regime shift from Goodhart-style gaming to Campbell-style evaluation degradation.

Result: They prove that under their axioms any optimized AI agent will under-invest in un-evaluated quality dimensions, making reward hacking a structural equilibrium independent of the specific alignment technique or evaluation architecture. They show that as systems become more agentic and tool-based, the space of relevant quality dimensions grows combinatorially while evaluation resources grow at most linearly, causing evaluation coverage to trend toward zero and hacking severity to diverge. They derive a distortion index that can, in principle, predict which dimensions will be gamed and how badly, and provide a partially formalized capability threshold at which agents move from merely exploiting the evaluation system to actively undermining it.

Conclusion: Reward hacking is not an incidental problem but a structural consequence of optimizing under finite, partial evaluations in multi-dimensional quality spaces, and it worsens with increasing agentic capability and tool use. Alignment schemes that rely on reward models or evaluations cannot fully eliminate this phenomenon but can use the proposed distortion index and vulnerability assessment procedure to anticipate and mitigate specific hacking patterns. Beyond a certain capability threshold, agents may shift from Goodhart-style metric gaming to Campbell-style degradation of the evaluation process itself, offering an economic formalization of the "treacherous turn" concern.

Abstract: We prove that under five minimal axioms -- multi-dimensional quality, finite evaluation, effective optimization, resource finiteness, and combinatorial interaction -- any optimized AI agent will systematically under-invest effort in quality dimensions not covered by its evaluation system. This result establishes reward hacking as a structural equilibrium, not a correctable bug, and holds regardless of the specific alignment method (RLHF, DPO, Constitutional AI, or others) or evaluation architecture employed. Our framework instantiates the multi-task principal-agent model of Holmstrom and Milgrom (1991) in the AI alignment setting, but exploits a structural feature unique to AI systems -- the known, differentiable architecture of reward models -- to derive a computable distortion index that predicts both the direction and severity of hacking on each quality dimension prior to deployment. We further prove that the transition from closed reasoning to agentic systems causes evaluation coverage to decline toward zero as tool count grows -- because quality dimensions expand combinatorially while evaluation costs grow at most linearly per tool -- so that hacking severity increases structurally and without bound. Our results unify the explanation of sycophancy, length gaming, and specification gaming under a single theoretical structure and yield an actionable vulnerability assessment procedure. We further conjecture -- with partial formal analysis -- the existence of a capability threshold beyond which agents transition from gaming within the evaluation system (Goodhart regime) to actively degrading the evaluation system itself (Campbell regime), providing the first economic formalization of Bostrom's (2014) "treacherous turn."

</details>

Method: EpiPersona introduces an explicit persona-episode coupling framework. It first takes noisy preference feedback from users and projects it into a low-dimensional persona space, learning compact representations of users’ stable traits. Similar personas are clustered and aggregated into shared discrete codes, effectively forming persona types. This procedure is data-driven and does not rely on predefined dimensions. Then, during inference or training, the inferred stable persona representation is coupled with the current episode/context to make episode-aware preference predictions, separating long-term and situational preference signals.

Result: Experiments across multiple datasets and settings show that EpiPersona outperforms baseline pluralistic alignment and personalization methods. The gains are especially large in challenging episodic-shift scenarios where the user’s situational context changes significantly between training and test episodes. EpiPersona also maintains strong performance when only sparse preference feedback is available, indicating efficient use of limited data. Quantitative metrics demonstrate consistent improvements over baselines.

Conclusion: EpiPersona effectively disentangles stable persona traits from episodic factors in user preference data and couples them for context-aware prediction, improving pluralistic alignment of LLMs. By learning a low-dimensional, discrete persona space without predefined dimensions, the framework generalizes better across episodes, handles episodic shifts, and remains robust in low-data regimes. This suggests a promising direction for scalable, fair, and robust personalization of LLMs to diverse and minority user preferences.

Abstract: Pluralistic alignment is essential for adapting large language models (LLMs) to the diverse preferences of individuals and minority groups. However, existing approaches often mix stable personal traits with episode-specific factors, limiting their ability to generalize across episodes. To address this challenge, we introduce EpiPersona, a framework for explicit persona-episode coupling. EpiPersona first projects noisy preference feedback into a low-dimensional persona space, where similar personas are aggregated into shared discrete codes. This process separates enduring personal characteristics from situational signals without relying on predefined preference dimensions. The inferred persona representation is then coupled with the current episode, enabling episode-aware preference prediction. Extensive experiments show that EpiPersona consistently outperforms the baselines. It achieves notable performance gains in hard episodic-shift scenarios, while remaining effective with sparse preference data.

### [128] [Differentiable Power-Flow Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28203)
*Muhammed Öz,Jasmin Hörter,Kaleb Phipps,Charlotte Debus,Achim Streit,Markus Götz*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce a differentiable, GPU-accelerated alternative to Newton-Raphson AC power-flow that scales better and supports gradient-based learning of grid parameters.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Renewable energy increases variability and complexity in power grids, making traditional NR-based AC power-flow simulations too slow and poorly scalable for large-scale and emerging tasks like joint transmission-distribution and global analyses. Purely data-driven surrogates, while fast, lack physical guarantees and may violate constraints, so there is a need for a scalable yet physically faithful approach.

Method: Reformulate the AC power-flow equations as a differentiable simulation (Differentiable Power-Flow, DPF) implemented in a modern ML framework (e.g., PyTorch). Use automatic differentiation to propagate gradients from power mismatches to simulation parameters, enabling gradient-based parameter identification. Exploit GPU acceleration, sparse tensors, and batching, and leverage warm-starting/solution reuse, batched contingency evaluation, and early stopping to speed up time-series and screening applications.

Result: DPF runs efficiently on GPUs, scales better than conventional NR for large and batched problems, supports end-to-end gradient propagation for parameter learning, and is effective for time-series studies, N-1 contingency analyses, and fast screening tasks. The implementation, based on PyTorch with sparse and batched operations, demonstrates significant computational advantages over traditional solvers in these scenarios.

Conclusion: Differentiable Power-Flow offers a physically grounded, scalable, and efficient alternative to classical NR-based AC power-flow solvers. By leveraging differentiability and modern ML tooling, it enables gradient-based parameter estimation and accelerates large-scale, time-series, and contingency analyses, making it well-suited to the needs of renewable-rich, complex power systems. The approach is practically accessible via the released codebase.

Abstract: With the rise of renewable energy sources and their high variability in generation, the management of power grids becomes increasingly complex and computationally demanding. Conventional AC-power-flow simulations, which use the Newton-Raphson (NR) method, suffer from poor scalability, making them impractical for emerging use cases such as joint transmission-distribution modeling and global grid analysis. At the same time, purely data-driven surrogate models lack physical guarantees and may violate fundamental constraints. In this work, we propose Differentiable Power-Flow (DPF), a reformulation of the AC power-flow problem as a differentiable simulation. DPF enables end-to-end gradient propagation from the physical power mismatches to the underlying simulation parameters, thereby allowing these parameters to be identified efficiently using gradient-based optimization. We demonstrate that DPF provides a scalable alternative to NR by leveraging GPU acceleration, sparse tensor representations, and batching capabilities available in modern machine-learning frameworks such as PyTorch. DPF is especially suited as a tool for time-series analyses due to its efficient reuse of previous solutions, for N-1 contingency-analyses due to its ability to process cases in batches, and as a screening tool by leveraging its speed and early stopping capability. The code is available in the authors' code repository.

### [129] [Reasoning as Energy Minimization over Structured Latent Trajectories](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28248)
*David K. Johansson*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces EBRM, an energy-based latent planning framework for neural reasoning, identifies a key failure mode due to latent distribution mismatch, analyzes it thoroughly, and proposes training fixes and ablations; it works on some synthetic reasoning tasks but shows limitations on arithmetic.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Single-shot neural decoders lack explicit multi-step reasoning, while chain-of-thought gives discrete steps but no continuous scalar of reasoning progress. The authors want a principled, gradient-based way to represent and optimize multi-step reasoning trajectories in latent space, with an energy function that can guide and measure reasoning, and to understand whether such structured latent planning actually improves reasoning performance.

Method: They define an energy function E(h_x, z_{1:T}) over multi-step latent trajectories that decomposes into per-step input-compatibility, transition-consistency, and smoothness terms. Reasoning is cast as minimizing this energy via gradient descent or Langevin dynamics over the latent trajectory z_{1:T}, then decoding the final latent z_T into an answer. Training combines standard supervised encoder-decoder learning (mapping x to targets) with contrastive energy-based training using hard negative trajectories to shape the energy landscape. They analyze failure modes by decoding from intermediate z_t, tracking how z_t drifts away from encoder latents, and decomposing gradients. They then modify training via dual-path decoder training (training the decoder on both encoder and planner-generated latents) and latent anchoring (regularizing z_t toward encoder latents). They also run a structured six-part ablation over architectural and training choices.

Result: On CNF logic satisfaction, naive latent planning with EBRM significantly hurts performance, dropping accuracy from about 95% to 56% due to mismatch between encoder latents and planner-generated latents. Analysis confirms that the planner drives z_t into regions the decoder never saw during training and that different energy terms influence this drift. With dual-path training and anchoring, this degradation can be mitigated. Across three synthetic tasks (graph, logic, arithmetic), energy decreases monotonically and induces structured latent trajectories for graph/logic tasks, but the energy landscape is effectively flat and uninformative for arithmetic (correlation r = 0.073).

Conclusion: Energy-based latent planning can impose structured multi-step reasoning trajectories and provide a scalar notion of reasoning progress, but naïvely combining a planner with a standard encoder-decoder causes severe distribution mismatch that can destroy performance. Properly aligning the decoder’s training distribution with planner latents (dual-path training, anchoring) is critical. The mixed results—success on graph/logic tasks and a negative result on arithmetic—suggest that energy-based reasoning is promising but task-dependent, and that designing informative energy landscapes remains a central challenge.

Abstract: Single-shot neural decoders commit to answers without iterative refinement, while chain-of-thought methods introduce discrete intermediate steps but lack a scalar measure of reasoning progress. We propose Energy-Based Reasoning via Structured Latent Planning (EBRM), which models reasoning as gradient-based optimization of a multi-step latent trajectory $z_{1:T}$ under a learned energy function $E(h_x, z)$. The energy decomposes into per-step compatibility, transition consistency, and trajectory smoothness terms. Training combines supervised encoder-decoder learning with contrastive energy shaping using hard negatives, while inference performs gradient descent or Langevin dynamics over $z$ and decodes from $z_T$.
  We identify a critical failure mode: on CNF logic satisfaction, latent planning reduces accuracy from $\approx 95\%$ to $\approx 56\%$. This degradation arises from a distribution mismatch, where the decoder is trained on encoder outputs $h_x$ but evaluated on planner outputs $z_T$ that drift into unseen latent regions. We analyze this behavior through per-step decoding, latent drift tracking, and gradient decomposition. To address it, we propose dual-path decoder training and latent anchoring.
  We further introduce a six-part ablation protocol covering component contributions, trajectory length, planner dynamics, initialization, decoder training distribution, and anchor weight. Experiments on three synthetic tasks show that energy decreases monotonically and induces structured latent trajectories on graph and logic tasks, while remaining flat on arithmetic ($r = 0.073$), indicating a negative result. Code is available at https://github.com/dkjo8/ebr-via-structured-latent-planning.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

While students increasingly use generative AI tools, direct access often hinders the learning process by providing complete solutions rather than pedagogical hints. Concurrently, educators face significant workload and scalability challenges when providing timely, personalized feedback. This study investigates the capabilities of LLMs to safely and effectively assist educators in answering student questions within a CS1 programming course. To achieve this, we established a rigorous, reproducible evaluation process by curating a benchmark dataset of 170 authentic student questions from a learning management system, paired with ground-truth responses authored by subject matter experts. Because traditional text-matching metrics are insufficient for evaluating open-ended educational responses, we developed and validated a custom LLM-as-a-Judge metric optimized for assessing pedagogical accuracy. Our findings demonstrate that models, such as Gemini 3 flash, can surpass the quality baseline of typical educator responses, achieving high alignment with expert pedagogical standards. To mitigate persistent risks like hallucination and ensure alignment with course-specific context, we advocate for a "teacher-in-the-loop" implementation. Finally, we abstract our methodology into a task-agnostic evaluation framework, advocating for a shift in the development of educational LLM tools from ad-hoc, post-deployment testing to a quantifiable, pre-deployment validation process.

### [132] [CoE: Collaborative Entropy for Uncertainty Quantification in Agentic Multi-LLM Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28360)
*Kangkang Sun,Jun Wu,Jianhua Li,Minyi Guo,Xiuzhen Che,Jianwei Huang*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing uncertainty estimation focuses on individual LLMs and overlooks semantic disagreement between models in multi-LLM setups, limiting our ability to reason about system-level confidence and collaboration.

Method: Define Collaborative Entropy (CoE) on a shared semantic cluster space, combining intra-model semantic entropy with inter-model divergence from the ensemble mean; analyze its theoretical properties and use it to design a simple, training-free, post-hoc coordination heuristic for multi-LLM systems.

Result: On TriviaQA and SQuAD using LLaMA-3.1-8B-Instruct, Qwen-2.5-7B-Instruct, and Mistral-7B-Instruct, CoE produces stronger uncertainty estimates than standard entropy- and divergence-based baselines, with performance gains increasing as more heterogeneous models are added.

Conclusion: CoE is a practical, model-agnostic, information-theoretic measure of semantic uncertainty for multi-LLM collaboration that captures both individual model confidence and cross-model disagreement, enabling improved uncertainty-aware coordination in multi-LLM systems.

Abstract: Uncertainty estimation in multi-LLM systems remains largely single-model-centric: existing methods quantify uncertainty within each model but do not adequately capture semantic disagreement across models. To address this gap, we propose Collaborative Entropy (CoE), a unified information-theoretic metric for semantic uncertainty in multi-LLM collaboration. CoE is defined on a shared semantic cluster space and combines two components: intra-model semantic entropy and inter-model divergence to the ensemble mean. CoE is not a weighted ensemble predictor; it is a system-level uncertainty measure that characterizes collaborative confidence and disagreement. We analyze several core properties of CoE, including non-negativity, zero-value certainty under perfect semantic consensus, and the behavior of CoE when individual models collapse to delta distributions. These results clarify when reducing per-model uncertainty is sufficient and when residual inter-model disagreement remains. We also present a simple CoE-guided, training-free post-hoc coordination heuristic as a practical application of the metric. Experiments on \textit{TriviaQA} and \textit{SQuAD} with LLaMA-3.1-8B-Instruct, Qwen-2.5-7B-Instruct, and Mistral-7B-Instruct show that CoE provides stronger uncertainty estimation than standard entropy- and divergence-based baselines, with gains becoming larger as additional heterogeneous models are introduced. Overall, CoE offers a useful uncertainty-aware perspective on multi-LLM collaboration.

### [134] [COvolve: Adversarial Co-Evolution of Large-Language-Model-Generated Policies and Environments via Two-Player Zero-Sum Game](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28386)
*Alkis Sygkounas,Rishi Hazra,Andreas Persson,Pedro Zuidberg Dos Martires,Amy Loutfi*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional training environments for agents are static or manually designed, which limits continual learning, robustness, and generalization beyond the training distribution. The authors want an automated way to create ever-more challenging environments and matching policies without hand-crafted curricula.

Method: They introduce COvolve, a co-evolutionary system where LLMs generate both environment code and policy code. The environment and policy designers are modeled as players in a two-player zero-sum game: environments seek to expose policy weaknesses; policies seek to perform well. This adversarial interaction automatically induces a curriculum of increasing difficulty. They compute the mixed-strategy Nash equilibrium (MSNE) of this game to obtain a meta-policy that mixes over learned policies to maintain performance on both past and new environments, mitigating forgetting.

Result: In experiments on urban driving, symbolic maze-solving, and geometric navigation tasks, COvolve generates progressively more complex environments and corresponding policies. The MSNE-based meta-policies maintain competence on previously solved environments while adapting to new, harder ones, showing improved robustness and open-ended learning behavior compared to baselines without such co-evolution or equilibrium reasoning.

Conclusion: LLM-driven co-evolution of environment and policy code, framed as a zero-sum game and solved via MSNE to obtain a meta-policy, can produce automated curricula and support open-ended, continual learning. This reduces reliance on manual environment design and fixed task distributions, suggesting a scalable path for building continually improving agents.

Abstract: A central challenge in building continually improving agents is that training environments are typically static or manually constructed. This restricts continual learning and generalization beyond the training distribution. We address this with COvolve, a co-evolutionary framework that leverages large language models (LLMs) to generate both environments and agent policies, expressed as executable Python code. We model the interaction between environment and policy designers as a two-player zero-sum game, ensuring adversarial co-evolution in which environments expose policy weaknesses and policies adapt in response. This process induces an automated curriculum in which environments and policies co-evolve toward increasing complexity. To guarantee robustness and prevent forgetting as the curriculum progresses, we compute the mixed-strategy Nash equilibrium (MSNE) of the zero-sum game, thereby yielding a meta-policy. This MSNE meta-policy ensures that the agent does not forget to solve previously seen environments while learning to solve previously unseen ones. Experiments in urban driving, symbolic maze-solving, and geometric navigation showcase that COvolve produces progressively more complex environments. Our results demonstrate the potential of LLM-driven co-evolution to achieve open-ended learning without predefined task distributions or manual intervention.

*Doan Nam Long Vu,Simone Balloccu*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They evaluate 12 open-weight vision-language models on binary diagnostic classification using two neuroimaging cohorts (FOR2107 for affective disorders and OASIS-3 for cognitive decline) where structural MRI has no reliable individual-level diagnostic signal. However, 70–80% of this performance shift is explained solely by mention of MRI availability in the prompt, regardless of actual image presence—a "scaffold effect" of modality collapse. Models fabricate MRI-grounded justifications in all conditions, and applying preference alignment that removes MRI-referencing behavior also removes the apparent gains, reverting performance to random baseline.

Conclusion: Apparent multimodal performance gains in clinical VLMs can be largely driven by prompt-level artifacts rather than genuine image reasoning. Both datasets come with structural MRI data that carries no reliable individual-level diagnostic signal. Under these conditions, smaller VLMs exhibit gains of up to 58\% F1 upon introduction of neuroimaging context, with distilled models becoming competitive with counterparts an order of magnitude larger. A contrastive confidence analysis reveals that merely \emph{mentioning} MRI availability in the task prompt accounts for 70-80\% of this shift, independent of whether imaging data is present, a domain-specific instance of modality collapse we term the \emph{scaffold effect}. Expert evaluation reveals fabrication of neuroimaging-grounded justifications across all conditions, and preference alignment, while eliminating MRI-referencing behavior, collapses both conditions toward random baseline. Our findings demonstrate that surface evaluations are inadequate indicators of multimodal reasoning, with direct implications for the deployment of VLMs in clinical settings.

Human verification and robustness checks show that MiroEval’s scoring and framework are reliable.

Conclusion: MiroEval is an effective, holistic benchmark and evaluation framework for deep research agents. By combining adaptive synthesis scoring, agentic factuality checks, and process-centric auditing on realistic, multimodal, and updatable tasks, it offers a robust diagnostic tool for understanding and improving the next generation of deep research systems.

To address these gaps, we introduce MiroEval, a benchmark and evaluation framework for deep research systems. The benchmark comprises 100 tasks (70 text-only, 30 multimodal), all grounded in real user needs and constructed via a dual-path pipeline that supports periodic updates, enabling a live and evolving setting. The proposed evaluation suite assesses deep research systems along three complementary dimensions: adaptive synthesis quality evaluation with task-specific rubrics, agentic factuality verification via active retrieval and reasoning over both web sources and multimodal attachments, and process-centric evaluation audits how the system searches, reasons, and refines throughout its investigation. The MiroThinker series achieves the most balanced performance, with MiroThinker-H1 ranking the highest overall in both settings. MiroEval provides a holistic diagnostic tool for the next generation of deep research agents.

### [137] [Entropic Claim Resolution: Uncertainty-Driven Evidence Selection for RAG](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28444)
*Davide Di Gioia*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes Entropic Claim Resolution (ECR), an entropy-based inference-time algorithm for RAG that selects evidence to maximally reduce answer uncertainty rather than just maximize relevance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard RAG relies on relevance-based retrieval, which fails when evidence conflicts or queries are ambiguous, so systems need a principled way to manage epistemic uncertainty and decide which evidence is most informative.

Method: Formulate RAG reasoning as entropy minimization over semantic answer hypotheses; at each step, select the next atomic evidence claim by maximizing Expected Entropy Reduction (EER), and stop once total epistemic uncertainty drops below a threshold H <= epsilon, ensuring epistemic coherence. Implement this within a multi-strategy retrieval pipeline (CSGR++).

Result: ECR is integrated into a production-grade retrieval system (CSGR++), with theoretical analysis showing its properties as an uncertainty-aware, decision-theoretic evidence selection mechanism; it prioritizes discriminative over merely relevant evidence.

Conclusion: ECR offers a rigorous, uncertainty-aware framework for RAG, redefining retrieval from relevance maximization to discriminativeness and epistemic sufficiency, improving reasoning in ambiguous or conflicting evidence settings.

Abstract: Current Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) systems predominantly rely on relevance-based dense retrieval, sequentially fetching documents to maximize semantic similarity with the query. However, in knowledge-intensive and real-world scenarios characterized by conflicting evidence or fundamental query ambiguity, relevance alone is insufficient for resolving epistemic uncertainty. We introduce Entropic Claim Resolution (ECR), a novel inference-time algorithm that reframes RAG reasoning as entropy minimization over competing semantic answer hypotheses. Unlike action-driven agentic frameworks (e.g., ReAct) or fixed-pipeline RAG architectures, ECR sequentially selects atomic evidence claims by maximizing Expected Entropy Reduction (EER), a decision-theoretic criterion for the value of information. The process dynamically terminates when the system reaches a mathematically defined state of epistemic sufficiency (H <= epsilon, subject to epistemic coherence). We integrate ECR into a production-grade multi-strategy retrieval pipeline (CSGR++) and analyze its theoretical properties. Our framework provides a rigorous foundation for uncertainty-aware evidence selection, shifting the paradigm from retrieving what is most relevant to retrieving what is most discriminative.

### [138] [T-Norm Operators for EU AI Act Compliance Classification: An Empirical Comparison of Lukasiewicz, Product, and Gödel Semantics in a Neuro-Symbolic Reasoning System](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28558)
*Adam Laabs*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Pilot comparison of three fuzzy t-norms (Łukasiewicz, Product, Gödel) as conjunction operators in a neuro-symbolic classifier for EU AI Act risk levels, finding Gödel best on accuracy/recall but with small false-positive cost, and arguing for mixed semantics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how the choice of fuzzy logical conjunction (t-norm) affects performance, error types, and behaviour on ambiguous borderline cases in a neuro-symbolic reasoning system that classifies AI systems by EU AI Act risk categories, and to inform design of reliable, legally relevant AI compliance tools.

Method: Implement three t-norm operators (Łukasiewicz T_L, Product T_P, Gödel T_G) as the conjunction component in the LGGT+ neuro-symbolic reasoning engine; evaluate them on a benchmark of 1035 AI system descriptions labelled into four EU AI Act risk categories; measure classification accuracy, false positive/negative rates, and inspect behaviour on ambiguous borderline cases; perform McNemar tests to check pairwise significance of performance differences.

Result: On the 1035-sample benchmark, all three t-norms differ significantly (McNemar p<0.001). Gödel t-norm T_G yields the highest overall accuracy (84.5%) and best recall on borderline cases (85%) but incurs 8 false positives (0.8%) due to its min-semantics tendency to over-classify. Łukasiewicz T_L and Product T_P both achieve zero false positives, with T_P outperforming T_L (81.2% vs 78.5% accuracy) but both miss more borderline cases than T_G.

Conclusion: The completeness and quality of the symbolic rule base matters more than which of the three t-norms is chosen, but t-norm choice still shapes the precision–recall trade-off: T_L and T_P are conservative (no false positives, lower recall), whereas T_G yields higher recall with a small false-positive cost. The authors propose that a mixed-semantics classifier combining these behaviours is the most promising future direction, and they support further work by releasing the LGGT+ engine and benchmark dataset under Apache 2.0.

Abstract: We present a first comparative pilot study of three t-norm operators -- Lukasiewicz (T_L), Product (T_P), and Gödel (T_G) - as logical conjunction mechanisms in a neuro-symbolic reasoning system for EU AI Act compliance classification. Using the LGGT+ (Logic-Guided Graph Transformers Plus) engine and a benchmark of 1035 annotated AI system descriptions spanning four risk categories (prohibited, high_risk, limited_risk, minimal_risk), we evaluate classification accuracy, false positive and false negative rates, and operator behaviour on ambiguous cases. At n=1035, all three operators differ significantly (McNemar p<0.001). T_G achieves highest accuracy (84.5%) and best borderline recall (85%), but introduces 8 false positives (0.8%) via min-semantics over-classification. T_L and T_P maintain zero false positives, with T_P outperforming T_L (81.2% vs. 78.5%). Our principal findings are: (1) operator choice is secondary to rule base completeness; (2) T_L and T_P maintain zero false positives but miss borderline cases; (3) T_G's min-semantics achieves higher recall at cost of 0.8% false positive rate; (4) a mixed-semantics classifier is the productive next step. We release the LGGT+ core engine (201/201 tests passing) and benchmark dataset (n=1035) under Apache 2.0.

### [140] [MonitorBench: A Comprehensive Benchmark for Chain-of-Thought Monitorability in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28590)
*Han Wang,Yifan Sun,Brian Ko,Mann Talati,Jiawen Gong,Zimeng Li,Naicheng Yu,Xucheng Yu,Wei Shen,Vedant Jolly,Huan Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MonitorBench, an open-source benchmark to rigorously evaluate how faithfully chain-of-thought (CoT) explanations reflect the true decision-making processes of large language models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Chain-of-thought explanations from LLMs often fail to be causally responsible for the final answer, so they may not accurately reveal the decision-critical factors guiding the model. This undermines efforts to monitor, audit, and trust LLM behavior. There is no comprehensive, open-source benchmark focused specifically on this "CoT monitorability" issue, which limits systematic study and comparison of models and methods.

Method: The authors design MonitorBench, a benchmark of 1,514 test instances spanning 19 tasks in 7 categories. Each instance explicitly encodes decision-critical factors that should drive the model’s output. They then define metrics and two stress-test settings to assess how well CoTs expose these factors and how easily monitorability can be degraded. They evaluate multiple open- and closed-source LLMs of varying capabilities on these tasks and stress tests.

Result: They find that CoT monitorability is higher when solving the task requires structural reasoning through the decision-critical factor, meaning the model must explicitly use that factor in its reasoning. Closed-source models generally show lower monitorability than open-source ones, and higher-capability models tend to have worse monitorability. Under stress-test conditions, both open- and closed-source models can substantially reduce monitorability, with drops up to 30% on tasks that do not require structural reasoning over the key factors.

Conclusion: MonitorBench exposes systematic weaknesses in the faithfulness of CoT explanations and reveals that monitorability can degrade especially in tasks not requiring structured reasoning and under adversarial conditions. It establishes a standardized, fully open benchmark for comparing models, studying stress-test monitorability, and inspiring new methods to better align CoTs with the true drivers of model behavior.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) can generate chains of thought (CoTs) that are not always causally responsible for their final outputs. When such a mismatch occurs, the CoT no longer faithfully reflects the decision-critical factors driving the model's behavior, leading to the reduced CoT monitorability problem. However, a comprehensive and fully open-source benchmark for studying CoT monitorability remains lacking. To address this gap, we propose MonitorBench, a systematic benchmark for evaluating CoT monitorability in LLMs. MonitorBench provides: (1) a diverse set of 1,514 test instances with carefully designed decision-critical factors across 19 tasks spanning 7 categories to characterize when CoTs can be used to monitor the factors driving LLM behavior; and (2) two stress-test settings to quantify the extent to which CoT monitorability can be degraded. Extensive experiments across multiple popular LLMs with varying capabilities show that CoT monitorability is higher when producing the final target response requires structural reasoning through the decision-critical factor. Closed-source LLMs generally show lower monitorability, and there exists a negative relationship between monitorability and model capability. Moreover, both open- and closed-source LLMs can intentionally reduce monitorability under stress-tests, with monitorability dropping by up to 30% in some tasks that do not require structural reasoning over the decision-critical factors. Beyond these empirical insights, MonitorBench provides a basis for further research on evaluating future LLMs, studying advanced stress-test monitorability techniques, and developing new monitoring approaches.

### [141] [Seeing with You: Perception-Reasoning Coevolution for Multimodal Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28618)
*Ziqi Miao,Haonan Jia,Lijun Li,Chen Qian,Yuan Xiong,Wenting Yan,Jing Shao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Paper introduces PRCO, a dual-role reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards framework for multimodal LLMs, separating perception and reasoning optimization to improve visual evidence extraction and final answer accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RLVR methods optimize perception and reasoning jointly using a single outcome-based reward tied to final answers. This shared reward causes poor credit assignment, so reasoning improves but perception (visual evidence extraction) often remains inaccurate, limiting overall multimodal reasoning performance.

Method: PRCO defines a shared-policy MLLM that plays two cooperative roles: an Observer and a Solver. The Observer generates question-aware evidence captions from the image; the Solver predicts final answers from these captions. RLVR is applied with role-specific rewards: the Solver is trained with standard verifiable outcome rewards based on answer correctness, while the Observer receives a utility reward that reflects how much its caption improves the Solver’s downstream success. Training coevolves both roles while maintaining a single underlying model.

Result: Across eight challenging multimodal reasoning benchmarks and various model scales, PRCO improves average accuracy by more than 7 percentage points over the base model, and consistently surpasses prior open-source RL-tuned baselines.

Conclusion: Decoupling perception and reasoning within a shared-policy MLLM and optimizing them with tailored rewards effectively resolves the perception bottleneck in RLVR. PRCO’s cooperative Observer-Solver setup yields more reliable evidence extraction and better end-to-end multimodal reasoning, establishing a stronger paradigm for RL-based training of MLLMs.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) has substantially enhanced the reasoning capabilities of multimodal large language models (MLLMs). However, existing RLVR approaches typically rely on outcome-driven optimization that updates both perception and reasoning using a shared reward based solely on the final answer. This shared reward blurs credit assignment, frequently improving reasoning patterns while failing to reliably enhance the accuracy of upstream visual evidence extraction. To address this perception bottleneck, we introduce PRCO (Perception-Reasoning Coevolution), a dual-role RLVR framework with a shared policy. PRCO consists of two cooperative roles: an Observer that generates an evidence caption tailored to the question and a Solver that predicts the final answer based on this caption. Crucially, PRCO employs role-specific reward signals: the Solver is optimized using verifiable outcome rewards on the final answer, while the Observer receives a utility reward derived from the Solver's downstream success. Extensive experiments across eight challenging multimodal reasoning benchmarks demonstrate that PRCO yields consistent improvements across model scales by over 7 points on average accuracy compared to the base model, outperforming prior open-source RL-tuned baselines.

</details>


### [142] [The Ultimate Tutorial for AI-driven Scale Development in Generative Psychometrics: Releasing AIGENIE from its Bottle](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28643)
*Lara Russell-Lasalandra,Hudson Golino,Luis Eduardo Garrido,Alexander P. Christensen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AI-GENIE (implemented in the AIGENIE R package) automates early psychological scale development by combining LLM-based item generation with network psychometric methods to create and refine item pools fully in silico.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional psychological scale development is slow, resource-intensive, and requires heavy expert and participant involvement before psychometric evaluation can begin. With the rise of large language models and network psychometrics, there is an opportunity to automate and accelerate early-stage item generation and structural validation, reducing cost and enabling rapid prototyping of both established and emerging constructs.

Method: The AIGENIE R package operationalizes the AI-GENIE framework. It uses LLMs to generate initial item pools, converts these items into high-dimensional text embeddings, and then applies a multi-step network psychometrics pipeline—Exploratory Graph Analysis (EGA), Unique Variable Analysis (UVA), and bootstrap EGA—to iteratively reduce and structurally validate the item set. The process is implemented in functions for text/item generation and for applying the reduction pipeline to any item pool, with support for multiple LLM providers and an offline mode.

Result: The package enables researchers to produce structurally validated psychological scale item pools entirely in silico, without initial data collection from human participants. It provides tutorial examples for both a well-established construct (Big Five personality) and an emerging one (AI Anxiety), demonstrating that the workflow is applicable across different construct maturities and data sources.

Conclusion: AIGENIE offers a practical, freely available toolchain that leverages LLMs and network psychometrics to streamline early scale development. By supporting multiple LLM backends and providing a standalone psychometric reduction function (GENIE) for existing item pools, it broadens access to automated, reproducible, and flexible workflows for psychological measurement design.

Abstract: Psychological scale development has traditionally required extensive expert involvement, iterative revision, and large-scale pilot testing before psychometric evaluation can begin. The `AIGENIE` R package implements the AI-GENIE framework (Automatic Item Generation with Network-Integrated Evaluation), which integrates large language model (LLM) text generation with network psychometric methods to automate the early stages of this process. The package generates candidate item pools using LLMs, transforms them into high-dimensional embeddings, and applies a multi-step reduction pipeline -- Exploratory Graph Analysis (EGA), Unique Variable Analysis (UVA), and bootstrap EGA -- to produce structurally validated item pools entirely *in silico*. This tutorial introduces the package across six parts: installation and setup, understanding Application Programming Interfaces (APIs), text generation, item generation, the `AIGENIE` function, and the `GENIE` function. Two running examples illustrate the package's use: the Big Five personality model (a well-established construct) and AI Anxiety (an emerging construct). The package supports multiple LLM providers (OpenAI, Anthropic, Groq, HuggingFace, and local models), offers a fully offline mode with no external API calls, and provides the `GENIE()` function for researchers who wish to apply the psychometric reduction pipeline to existing item pools regardless of their origin. The `AIGENIE` package is freely available on R-universe at https://laralee.r-universe.dev/AIGENIE.

</details>


### [143] [Not Search, But Scan: Benchmarking MLLMs on Scan-Oriented Academic Paper Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28651)
*Rongjin Li,Zichen Tang,Xianghe Wang,Xinyi Hu,Zhengyu Wang,Zhengyu Lu,Yiling Huang,Jiayuan Chen,Weisheng Tan,Jiacheng Liu,Zhongjun Yang,Haihong E*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ScholScan is a new benchmark designed to evaluate how well multimodal large language models can scan and critically analyze full research papers, focusing on detecting internal inconsistencies rather than just retrieving relevant information.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current multimodal large language models excel at search-oriented academic tasks (like literature retrieval and target-specific question answering), but they perform poorly at researcher-style reading that requires understanding the whole paper, checking internal consistency, and verifying claims. Existing benchmarks largely test retrieval-based reasoning, not comprehensive scan-based document critique. There is thus a need for a benchmark that measures full-document reasoning and consistency checking, closer to how human researchers read and evaluate papers.

Method: The authors design ScholScan, a benchmark where models must read entire papers and identify various types of consistency issues. They compile 1,800 manually curated questions spanning nine predefined error categories, taken from 715 natural-science papers across 13 domains. For each question, they annotate where in the paper the supporting evidence is located and provide reasoning traces. They also define a unified evaluation protocol. Using this benchmark, they systematically evaluate 15 different multimodal large language models under 24 different input settings (e.g., different ways of feeding the document into the model, possibly with or without retrieval augmentation).

Result: Evaluation on ScholScan shows that all tested models perform poorly on scan-oriented consistency-checking tasks relative to expectations, and that adding retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) does not meaningfully improve performance. Performance deficits are consistent across all nine error categories, indicating broad limitations in current models’ ability to perform full-document reasoning and cross-check internal consistency.

Conclusion: ScholScan exposes a fundamental limitation of current multimodal large language models: they are not yet capable of autonomous, researcher-like full-paper analysis and verification. The benchmark, with its detailed annotations and standardized protocol, provides a challenging and representative testbed for advancing scan-oriented academic reasoning. The authors argue that future work should move beyond retrieval-centric paradigms toward models and methods explicitly designed for holistic, scan-based understanding and consistency checking of research papers.

Abstract: With the rapid progress of multimodal large language models (MLLMs), AI already performs well at literature retrieval and certain reasoning tasks, serving as a capable assistant to human researchers, yet it remains far from autonomous research. The fundamental reason is that current work on academic paper reasoning is largely confined to a search-oriented paradigm centered on pre-specified targets, with reasoning grounded in relevance retrieval, which struggles to support researcher-style full-document understanding, reasoning, and verification. To bridge this gap, we propose \textbf{ScholScan}, a new benchmark for academic paper reasoning. ScholScan introduces a scan-oriented task setting that asks models to read and cross-check entire papers like human researchers, scanning the document to identify consistency issues. The benchmark comprises 1,800 carefully annotated questions drawn from nine error categories across 13 natural-science domains and 715 papers, and provides detailed annotations for evidence localization and reasoning traces, together with a unified evaluation protocol. We assessed 15 models across 24 input configurations and conducted a fine-grained analysis of MLLM capabilities for all error categories. Across the board, retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) methods yield no significant improvements, revealing systematic deficiencies of current MLLMs on scan-oriented tasks and underscoring the challenge posed by ScholScan. We expect ScholScan to be the leading and representative work of the scan-oriented task paradigm.

</details>


### [144] [Dynamic Dual-Granularity Skill Bank for Agentic RL](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.28716)
*Songjun Tu,Chengdong Xu,Qichao Zhang,Yaocheng Zhang,Xiangyuan Lan,Linjing Li,Dongbin Zhao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: D2Skill introduces a dynamic dual-granularity skill bank for agentic reinforcement learning that stores both task-level and step-level skills, jointly trained with the policy via paired rollouts, and shows 10–20 point success rate gains on web/ALFWorld benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing skill-based RL for agents mostly uses trajectory-level guidance and lacks a principled way to maintain and evolve a memory of reusable skills. There is a need for a framework that can capture both high-level and fine-grained skills, update them over time, and connect them tightly with RL training.

Method: D2Skill builds a dynamic skill bank with two granularities: task skills (high-level strategies) and step skills (low-level actions/error-correction patterns). It runs paired baseline and skill-injected rollouts under the same policy and uses the performance gap as a hindsight utility signal to update the skills and optimize the policy. The skill bank is expanded via reflection over training experience and maintained using utility-aware retrieval and pruning, all during training time.

Result: On ALFWorld and WebShop benchmarks with Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct and Qwen3-4B-Instruct-2507 agents, D2Skill yields consistent success rate improvements of 10–20 percentage points over baselines without skills. Ablations show that both dual-granularity modeling and dynamic maintenance are important, and that the skills transfer across evaluation settings with modest additional training cost.

Conclusion: Dynamic, dual-granularity skill banks that are tightly integrated with RL training can substantially improve agentic RL performance. Maintaining and evolving skills based on hindsight utility from paired rollouts leads to reusable, transferable skills and better success rates without heavy training overhead.

Abstract: Agentic reinforcement learning (RL) can benefit substantially from reusable experience, yet existing skill-based methods mainly extract trajectory-level guidance and often lack principled mechanisms for maintaining an evolving skill memory. We propose D2Skill, a dynamic dual-granularity skill bank for agentic RL that organizes reusable experience into task skills for high-level guidance and step skills for fine-grained decision support and error correction. D2Skill jointly trains the policy and skill bank through paired baseline and skill-injected rollouts under the same policy, using their performance gap to derive hindsight utility signals for both skill updating and policy optimization. Built entirely from training-time experience, the skill bank is continuously expanded through reflection and maintained with utility-aware retrieval and pruning. Experiments on ALFWorld and WebShop with Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct and Qwen3-4B-Instruct-2507 show that D2Skill consistently improves success rates over skill-free baselines by 10-20 points. Further ablations and analyses show that both dual-granularity skill modeling and dynamic skill maintenance are critical to these gains, while the learned skills exhibit higher utility, transfer across evaluation settings, and introduce only modest training overhead.

</details>
